Metaphorical Interpretations of Logic's Opening Line
Commentator explains possible metaphors in the line about logic and the sky/star.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit and implicit metaphor in the matn's opening line and identify the reinforcing detail (tarshih).

Core Idea
The poet uses two metaphors to show logic's difficulty and guidance.

The commentator unpacks the rhetorical possibilities in the matn's line about logic and the sky/star. By analyzing the explicit and implicit metaphors, we see how the Arabic rhetorical tradition enriches our understanding of the text. This lesson will guide you through each type.

Guided Path
1
Parse the line for metaphor
Read the Arabic line and note the words 'samā'' (sky) and 'najm' (star) as potential metaphorical terms.
2
Identify explicit metaphor
Find where the commentator explains that 'sky' is used for the difficult issues of logic. Note the commonality: difficulty of attainment.
3
Identify implicit metaphor
Find where the commentator says logic is likened to a star, but the star is omitted; only its concomitant 'sky' is mentioned. The commonality is guidance.
4
Analyze the tarshih
See how the phrase 'yurqa bihi' (by which one ascends) functions as a reinforcing detail appropriate to the sky.
5
Evaluate al-Anbabi's refinement
Read the added comment about synecdoche and the conditions for tarshih. Understand when tarshih applies and when it does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا ؛ وَيَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ فِي كَلَامِهِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ تَصْرِيحِيَّةٌ، أَوْ مَكْنِيَّةٌ : - فَعَلَى الأَوَّلِ : يَكُونُ قَدْ شَبَّهَ المَسَائِلَ الصَّعْبَةَ مِنْ عِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ بِمَعْنَى : السَّمَاءِ؛ بِجَامِعِ : عُسْرِ التَنَاوِلِ فِي كُلِّ، وَاسْتَعَارَ اسْمَ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ لِلمُشَبَّهِ . - وَعَلَى الثَّانِيَةِ : يَكُونُ قَدْ شَبَّهَ عِلْمَ المَنْطِقِ بِالنَّجْمِ ؛ بِجَامِعِ : الاهْتِدَاءِ بِكُلِّ تَشْبِيها مُضْمَراً فِي النَّفْسِ، وَحَذَفَ اسْمَ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ وَأَثْبَتَ لَهُ شَيْئاً مِنْ لَوَازِمِهِ وَهُوَ السَّمَاءُ؛ إِمَّا بَاقِياً عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ الحَقِيقِيِّ، أَوْ مُسْتَعَاراً لِلمَسَائِلِ الصَّعْبَةِ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ مِنْ هَذِهِ الْأَوْجُهِ الأَرْبَعَةِ) يَكُونُ قَوْلُهُ: «يُرْقَى بِهِ» تَرْشِيحاً ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ . الأنبابي نعم؛ إن أريد بعلم المنطق الصَّعبُ منه على سبيل المجاز المرسل؛ من إطلاق الكلِّ على البعض صح أيضاً، وعليه يحمل كلام شيخنا المحشي ـ نفعنا الله تعالى به ـ. قوله : (تَرْشِيحاً) هذا ظاهر على جعل التّركيب مِن إضافة المشبه به للمشبَّه؛ لأنَّ الترشيح يكون للتشبيه ومن باب الاستعارة التّصريحيَّة فقط، أو المكنيَّة مع استعارة قرينتها، فيكون ترشيحاً لاستعارة القرينة، وأما على جعله من باب المكنيَّة من غير استعارة القرينة فلا؛ إذ الرُّقي لا يناسب المشبه به الَّذِي النَّجم، وإنَّما يناسب السَّماء؛ إلَّا أن يقال: أنَّه يناسب النّجم لكونه يرقى إليه نفسه، أو لمحله وهو السَّماء؛ تأمل .
This; and it is correct that in his speech there is an explicit metaphor or implicit metaphor. In the first: he likened the difficult issues of logic to the meaning of 'sky' due to the commonality of difficulty of attainment in each, and he transferred the name of the likened-to to the likened. In the second: he likened the science of logic to the star due to the commonality of guidance in each, as an implied comparison in the mind, and he omitted the name of the likened-to and established for it something from its concomitants, which is the sky, either remaining on its literal meaning or transferred metaphorically to the difficult issues. And on each of these four aspects, his saying 'by which one ascends' is a reinforcing detail (tarshih), so let it be pondered. Al-Anbabi: Yes; if by 'science of logic' is meant the difficult part of it via synecdoche (majaz mursal) where the whole is used for a part, it is also valid, and upon that is carried the speech of our teacher the annotator. His saying 'tarshihan' is apparent on the construction being an addition of the likened-to to the likened, because the tarshih is for the comparison and from the chapter of explicit metaphor only, or implicit metaphor with the borrowing of its evidence, so it becomes a tarshih for the borrowing of the evidence. However, on the construction being from the chapter of implicit metaphor without borrowing the evidence, it is not, because 'ascending' is not appropriate to the likened-to which is the star, but rather is appropriate to the sky, unless it is said that it is appropriate to the star because one ascends to it itself, or to its place which is the sky; ponder.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage explains the possible rhetorical figures in the line of the matn. The commentator first states that the poet's words can contain either an explicit metaphor (isti'ara tashrihiyya) or an implicit metaphor (isti'ara makniyya). For the explicit metaphor: the poet likens the difficult issues of logic to the sky, based on the common difficulty of attainment in both, and uses the name of the likened-to (sky) for the likened (difficult issues). For the implicit metaphor: the poet likens the science of logic to a star, based on guidance, but does not mention the star; instead, he mentions a concomitant of it, which is the sky. The sky can be taken literally or also metaphorically for the difficult issues. In all four cases (two types each with two possibilities), the phrase 'yurqa bihi' (by which one ascends) serves as a reinforcing detail (tarshih). Then al-Anbabi adds a comment: if 'science of logic' is meant by synecdoche (majaz mursal) where the whole is used for the part (i.e., the difficult part), then the interpretation is also valid, and that is what the annotator (al-Bajuri?) intended. He further clarifies that the tarshih is only applicable if the metaphor is explicit or if the implicit metaphor includes the borrowing of the evidence (qarina). If the implicit metaphor is without the borrowing of the evidence, then 'ascending' does not fit the star, unless one argues that one ascends to the star itself or to its place. This is a detailed analysis of rhetorical possibilities in the opening line.

Key Terms
الاستعارة التصريحية
الاستعارة المكنية
الترشيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the explicit metaphor, what is the commonality (jami') between the difficult issues of logic and the sky?
A) Guidance
B) Difficulty of attainment
C) Ascending
D) Implied comparison

Key Takeaway
The commentator shows that the same line can support multiple rhetorical figures, each highlighting different aspects of logic's nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the hadith: 'The believer is like a bee.' Identify whether this is an explicit or implicit metaphor and state the commonality.
?
Practice: In the line 'Logic is a star in the sky by which one ascends', if we interpret 'sky' as a metaphor for the difficult issues, what is the commonality? Write your answer.

The Definition of Hope (Raja') and its Distinction from Covetousness (Tama')
Definition of raja' and tama' with their genus and differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define raja' and tama' according to the commentator and distinguish between them using the criterion of taking means.

Core Idea
Hope is not just wishful thinking; it requires taking the necessary means.

In this passage, the commentator defines raja' (hope) in a technical sense that includes taking means, distinguishing it from tama' (covetousness) which is hope without means. This distinction has implications for understanding Islamic concepts of reliance on Allah and personal effort.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus of raja' and tama'
Both are species of al-amal (hope). The commentator uses 'al-amal' as the broader category.
2
Extract the differentia for raja'
The differentia is 'ma'a al-akhdh fi al-asbab' (accompanied by taking the means).
3
Extract the differentia for tama'
The differentia is the negation: hope even if not accompanied by taking means.
4
Understand the relationship
Every raja' is tama' (since raja' includes hope, which is also in tama'), but not vice versa. Sometimes tama' is restricted to hope without means, making them opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالله أَرْجُو اللَّفْظُ الشَّرِيفُ مَنْصُوبٌ عَلَى التَّعْظِيمِ، هَكَذَا الأَدَبُ، وَلَا يُقَالُ: إِنَّهُ مَنْصُوبٌ عَلَى المَفْعُولِيَّةِ ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ الوَاقِعُ ؛ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنَ الإِخْلَالِ بِالأَدَبِ. وَإِنَّمَا قَدَّمَهُ ؛ لإفَادَةِ الحَصْرِ ، فَكَأَنَّهُ قَالَ: وَأَرْجُو اللَّهَ لَا غَيْرَهُ . وَالرَّجَاءُ» : - بِالمَدِّ كَـ : «الرَّجْوِ عَلَى وَزْنِ الضَّرْبِ، وَ: «الرَّجَاوَةِ» عَلَى وَزْنِ السَّعَادَةِ؛ مَعْنَاهُ: «الأَمَلُ مَعَ الأَخْذِ فِي الأَسْبَابِ»، بِخِلَافِ الطَّمَع ، فَإِنَّهُ الأَمَلُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَعَ الأَخْذِ فِي الأَسْبَابِ، فَكُلُّ رَجَاءِ طَمَعٌ، وَلَا عَكْسُ، وَقَدْ يَخُصُّ الطَّمَعُ بِمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ مَعَ الأَخْذِ فِي الأَسْبَابِ، فَيَكُونُ مُبَايناً لِلرَّجَاءِ، وَقَدْ يُطْلَقُ الرَّجَاءُ عَلَى الخَوْفِ، وَمِنْهُ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَاَرْجُوا الْيَوْمَ الْآخِرَ﴾ [العنكبوت: ٣٦] أَيْ: خَافُوهُ، وَقَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى : ﴿وما لَكُمْ لَا تَرْجُونَ لِلَّهِ وَقَارَام﴾ [نوح: ۱۳] أَيْ: لَا تَخَافُونَ عَظَمَةَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى . وَأَمَّا بِالقَصْرِ فَهُوَ : النَّاحِيَةُ؛ كَمَا فِي المُخْتَارِ . الأنبابي قوله : (وَقَدْ يُطْلَقُ الرَّجَاءُ عَلَى الخَوْفِ أى حقيقةً. اهـ (صبَّان).
His saying: 'and Allah I hope' — the noble word is accusative for glorification, and such is etiquette. It is not said that it is accusative as an object, even though that is the operative factor, because that would violate etiquette. He placed it before the verb for the purpose of exclusivity, as if he said: 'I hope Allah, not other than Him.' And 'al-raja'': with lengthening [alif], like 'al-rajw' on the pattern of 'al-darb', and 'al-rajawa' on the pattern of 'al-sa'ada'; its meaning is 'hope accompanied by taking means', unlike 'al-tama'' (covetousness), for it is hope even if not accompanied by taking means. Thus every hope is covetousness, but not vice versa. Sometimes 'tama'' is restricted to what is without taking means, so it becomes opposed to 'raja'. And sometimes 'raja' is applied to fear, as in His saying 'and hope the Last Day' meaning 'fear it', and His saying 'what is the matter with you that you do not hope for Allah dignity' meaning 'do not fear the greatness of Allah'. As for with short [alif], it is 'al-nahiya' (direction), as in al-Mukhtar. Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'and sometimes raja' is applied to fear' means it is used in a real sense, according to al-Sabban.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage explains the grammar and meaning of the opening invocation. The commentator first addresses the word 'Allaha' in the matn's line. He says it is accusative for glorification, not as a verb object, because etiquette demands it. The poet placed it before the verb for exclusivity. Then he defines 'raja'' (hope). It is a word with three patterns: 'raja'' (with alif), 'rajw' (as in 'darb'), and 'rajawa' (as in 'sa'ada'). Its meaning is 'hope along with taking the necessary means'. This distinguishes it from 'tama'' (covetousness), which is hope without taking means. All raja' is tama', but not all tama' is raja'. Sometimes tama' is defined as hope without means, making them opposites. Additionally, 'raja' can mean 'fear', as in Quranic verses where it is interpreted as fear. Finally, with a short vowel, it means 'direction'. Al-Anbabi adds that the use of raja' for fear is a real meaning, not metaphorical.

Key Terms
الرجاء
الطمع
الأمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what distinguishes raja' from tama'?
A) Raja' is longer in duration.
B) Raja' involves taking means, tama' does not.
C) Raja' is for worldly matters, tama' for religious.
D) They are the same.

Key Takeaway
Raja' (hope) is defined as hope accompanied by taking means, while tama' (covetousness) is hope without means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following statement: 'I hope Allah will forgive me even though I do not pray.' Is this raja' or tama' according to the definition? Explain using the differentia.
?
In the Quran, Allah says: 'And hope for [meeting] the Last Day' (Qur'an 29:36). The commentator says this means 'fear'. Does this contradict the definition of raja'? How does the commentator reconcile this?

The Accusative of 'Allaha' for Glorification: Grammatical Etiquette in Invocations
Grammatical analysis of 'Allaha' in the matn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the word 'Allaha' in the invocation 'wa Allah arju' is accusative for glorification and not as a verb object.

Core Idea
Grammatical choices in invocations reflect reverence, not just syntactic rules.

The commentator explains that the noble word 'Allaha' in the poet's line is accusative for glorification (al-ta'zim), following proper etiquette. This teaches that even grammar can carry theological and adab (etiquette) significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical issue
The word 'Allaha' is accusative (mansub) after the verb 'arju' which normally takes an object. But the commentator says it is not an object (maf'uliyya) but for glorification.
2
Understand the etiquette reason
To say it is an object would imply that the verb acts on Allah directly in a mundane grammatical sense, which is disrespectful. So it is accusative due to glorification.
3
Recognize the purpose of preposing
The word 'Allaha' is placed before the verb for exclusivity (hasr), meaning 'I hope Allah, not other than Him'.
4
Apply to similar contexts
In Islamic poetry and supplications, such grammatical choices are common to show reverence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالله أَرْجُو اللَّفْظُ الشَّرِيفُ مَنْصُوبٌ عَلَى التَّعْظِيمِ، هَكَذَا الأَدَبُ، وَلَا يُقَالُ: إِنَّهُ مَنْصُوبٌ عَلَى المَفْعُولِيَّةِ ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ الوَاقِعُ ؛ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنَ الإِخْلَالِ بِالأَدَبِ. وَإِنَّمَا قَدَّمَهُ ؛ لإفَادَةِ الحَصْرِ ، فَكَأَنَّهُ قَالَ: وَأَرْجُو اللَّهَ لَا غَيْرَهُ . وَالرَّجَاءُ» : - بِالمَدِّ كَـ : «الرَّجْوِ عَلَى وَزْنِ الضَّرْبِ، وَ: «الرَّجَاوَةِ» عَلَى وَزْنِ السَّعَادَةِ؛ مَعْنَاهُ: «الأَمَلُ مَعَ الأَخْذِ فِي الأَسْبَابِ»، بِخِلَافِ الطَّمَع ، فَإِنَّهُ الأَمَلُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَعَ الأَخْذِ فِي الأَسْبَابِ، فَكُلُّ رَجَاءِ طَمَعٌ، وَلَا عَكْسُ، وَقَدْ يَخُصُّ الطَّمَعُ بِمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ مَعَ الأَخْذِ فِي الأَسْبَابِ، فَيَكُونُ مُبَايناً لِلرَّجَاءِ، وَقَدْ يُطْلَقُ الرَّجَاءُ عَلَى الخَوْفِ، وَمِنْهُ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَاَرْجُوا الْيَوْمَ الْآخِرَ﴾ [العنكبوت: ٣٦] أَيْ: خَافُوهُ، وَقَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى : ﴿وما لَكُمْ لَا تَرْجُونَ لِلَّهِ وَقَارَام﴾ [نوح: ۱۳] أَيْ: لَا تَخَافُونَ عَظَمَةَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى . وَأَمَّا بِالقَصْرِ فَهُوَ : النَّاحِيَةُ؛ كَمَا فِي المُخْتَارِ . الأنبابي قوله : (وَقَدْ يُطْلَقُ الرَّجَاءُ عَلَى الخَوْفِ أى حقيقةً. اهـ (صبَّان).
His saying: 'and Allah I hope' — the noble word is accusative for glorification, and such is etiquette. It is not said that it is accusative as an object, even though that is the operative factor, because that would violate etiquette. He placed it before the verb for the purpose of exclusivity, as if he said: 'I hope Allah, not other than Him.' And 'al-raja'': with lengthening [alif], like 'al-rajw' on the pattern of 'al-darb', and 'al-rajawa' on the pattern of 'al-sa'ada'; its meaning is 'hope accompanied by taking means', unlike 'al-tama'' (covetousness), for it is hope even if not accompanied by taking means. Thus every hope is covetousness, but not vice versa. Sometimes 'tama'' is restricted to what is without taking means, so it becomes opposed to 'raja'. And sometimes 'raja' is applied to fear, as in His saying 'and hope the Last Day' meaning 'fear it', and His saying 'what is the matter with you that you do not hope for Allah dignity' meaning 'do not fear the greatness of Allah'. As for with short [alif], it is 'al-nahiya' (direction), as in al-Mukhtar. Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'and sometimes raja' is applied to fear' means it is used in a real sense, according to al-Sabban.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage explains the grammar and meaning of the opening invocation. The commentator first addresses the word 'Allaha' in the matn's line. He says it is accusative for glorification, not as a verb object, because etiquette demands it. The poet placed it before the verb for exclusivity. Then he defines 'raja'' (hope). It is a word with three patterns: 'raja'' (with alif), 'rajw' (as in 'darb'), and 'rajawa' (as in 'sa'ada'). Its meaning is 'hope along with taking the necessary means'. This distinguishes it from 'tama'' (covetousness), which is hope without taking means. All raja' is tama', but not all tama' is raja'. Sometimes tama' is defined as hope without means, making them opposites. Additionally, 'raja' can mean 'fear', as in Quranic verses where it is interpreted as fear. Finally, with a short vowel, it means 'direction'. Al-Anbabi adds that the use of raja' for fear is a real meaning, not metaphorical.

Key Terms
التعظيم
النصب
الحصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is 'Allaha' accusative in 'wa Allah arju'?
A) Because it is the object of the verb.
B) Due to glorification and etiquette.
C) Because it is preceded by a particle.
D) To show exclusivity.

Key Takeaway
Grammatical analysis of divine names must consider reverential etiquette; 'Allaha' is accusative for glorification, not as a verb object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' (praise be to Allah), the word 'lillah' is a prepositional phrase. Some grammatically analyze it as a predicate. How does the concept of glorification apply here?
?
Find another example in Islamic supplications where a word related to Allah is given a grammatical case for reverential reasons, not merely syntactic. (Hint: the basmala).

Meaning of Sincerity and the Three Levels of Worship
Commentator explains sincerity and enumerates three levels of worship free from prohibited intentions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three levels of worship and explain how sincerity applies to each.

Core Idea
Sincerity means freedom from other-than-Allah, and it encompasses worship at all levels.

This passage defines sincerity (khalis) in the context of a poet's supplication and presents the three accepted levels of worship free from prohibited intentions. Understanding this helps distinguish between valid and invalid acts of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'khalis'
Find how the commentator defines 'khalis': free from turbidities that invalidate the deed, such as love of fame, notoriety, and praise.
2
List the three levels of worship
Read the enumerated levels: (1) seeking reward and fear of punishment (lowest), (2) seeking honor through relation to Allah, (3) pure servitude because Allah is God and you are His servant (highest).
3
Understand the condition 'free from prohibition'
Note that the levels are specifically those free from prohibited intentions (haraam). This defines the scope of the discussion.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
If 'khalis' means free from obstacles of highest perfection, it confines to the last level. Reflect on how the interpretation changes the scope.
The commentator invites deeper reflection.

قَوْلُهُ : (أَنْ يَكُونَ خَالِصَا) أَيْ : مِنَ المُكَدَّرَاتِ الَّتِي تُحْبِطُ العَمَلَ كَـ: حُبِّ الظُّهُورِ، وَالشُّهْرَةِ، وَالمَحْمَدَةِ . وَحَيْثُ كَانَ المُرَادُ مَا ذُكِرَ ، صَدَقَ ذَلِكَ بِكُلِّ مِنَ المَرَاتِبِ الثَّلَاثَةِ الَّتِي ذَكَرُوهَا لِلعِبَادَةِ الخَالِيَةِ مِنَ الحُرْمَةِ، وَهِيَ : (۱) - أَنْ تَعْبُدَ اللهَ تَعَالَى طَالِباً لِلثَّوَابِ وَهَارِباً مِنَ العِقَابِ ، وَهَذِهِ أَدْنَاهَا . (٢) - وَأَنْ تَعْبُدَهُ تَعَالَى لِتَتَشَرَّفَ بِعِبَادَتِهِ وَالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَيْهِ، وَهَذِهِ أَعْلَى مِنَ الَّتِي قَبْلَهَا . (۳) - وَأَنْ تَعْبُدَهُ تَعَالَى لِكَوْنِهِ إِلَهَكَ وَأَنْتَ عَبْدَهُ، وَهَذِهِ أَعْلَاهَا ؛ كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ المِنَاوِيُّ . وَأَمَّا إِذَا كَانَ المُرَادُ أَنْ يَكُونَ خَالِصاً مِنْ مَوَانِعِ الكَمَالِ الأَعْلَى ، كَانَ مِنَ الرُّتْبَةِ الأَخِيرَةِ عَيْناً، فَلْيُتَا مَّلْ . الأنبابي قوله : (صَدَقَ ذَلِكَ بِكُلِّ مِنَ المَرَاتِبِ كيف هذا مع نسبة الخلوص للذات، والخالص للذات لا يكون إلَّا حيث كانتِ الذَّات هي المقصودة، فيكون قاصراً على أعلى المراتب؟ ويمكن أن يقال : أنَّ المراد بقوله : لِوَجْهِهِ الكَرِيمِ أنَّ التأليف لخالص ذاته ؛ بمعنى : أنه ليس لغير الله، ولا يشركه فيه غيره ؛ فكأنه قال : أن يكون التأليف منفياً عنِ الشَّركة الله، ومنفياً عنه كونه لغير الله تعالى، فيصح الصدق حينئذ .
His saying: 'that it be pure' meaning from the turbidities that invalidate the deed, like love of fame, notoriety, and praise. And since the intended meaning is what was mentioned, that applies to all three levels that they mentioned for worship free from prohibition. They are: (1) That you worship Allah seeking reward and fleeing from punishment – this is the lowest. (2) That you worship Him to honor yourself by His worship and relation to Him – this is higher than the previous. (3) That you worship Him because He is your God and you are His servant – this is the highest, as al-Munawi mentioned. And if the intended meaning is that it be pure from the obstacles of the highest perfection, then it is specifically of the last rank. So reflect. Al-Anbabi: His statement 'that applies to all levels' – how is this with the attribution of purity to the essence? Purity for the essence only occurs when the essence is the intended goal, so it would be confined to the highest level? It can be said: the intended meaning of his saying 'for His noble face' is that the composition is for His pure essence, meaning that it is not for other than Allah and no one else shares in it. So it is as if he said: the composition is negated from partnership to Allah and negated from being for other than Allah. So then the applicability is correct.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of 'khalisan' (pure) in the poet's supplication that his work be pure for Allah. The commentator says it means free from the turbidities that ruin the deed, such as love of fame, notoriety, and praise. He then notes that if that is the meaning, it applies to all three levels of worship that are free from prohibition (haram). These levels are: (1) worshipping for reward and fear of punishment – the lowest; (2) worshipping seeking honor through the relationship with Allah; and (3) worshipping solely because Allah is one's God and one is His servant – the highest. But if purity means being free from all obstacles of the highest perfection, then it is specific to the last level. Al-Anbabi raises an objection: how can purity apply to all levels when the phrase 'for His noble face' indicates intention of the essence, which is only the highest level? He answers that 'for His face' means the composition is solely for Allah, not mixed with any other intention. This allows it to apply to all three levels because even the lower levels have no share of other than Allah; they are still for Allah, albeit with personal benefit. The answer clarifies that the purity is from partnership, not from seeking reward.

Key Terms
خالص
مراتب العبادة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the highest level of worship?
A) Worshipping to seek reward
B) Worshipping to honor oneself
C) Worshipping because Allah is your God and you are His servant
D) Worshipping out of habit

Key Takeaway
Sincerity (ikhlas) means the act is free from any intention other than Allah, and this applies to worship at all three levels of seeking reward, honor, or pure servitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own prayer (salat). Which of the three levels does it most closely match? Explain why it still satisfies the definition of sincerity as 'free from other-than-Allah'.
?

Resolving the Scope of Sincerity: Al-Anbabi's Objection
Al-Anbabi critiques the commentator's claim and provides a resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Anbabi's objection and the commentator's resolution regarding the applicability of sincerity to all worship levels.

Core Idea
A refined definition of 'for His face' as negating partnership allows sincerity to encompass lower levels.

Al-Anbabi raises a critical objection: if sincerity means purely for Allah's essence, how can it include worship for reward? The commentator's answer clarifies the intended meaning of 'for His noble face' and resolves the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
State al-Anbabi's objection
He argues that if khalis means intended for Allah's essence, it should only apply to the highest level, not those seeking reward or honor.
The objection is based on the phrase 'li-wajhihi al-karim' (for His noble face).
2
Identify the key term causing confusion
The confusion centers on 'li-wajhihi al-karim'. Determine what it means here: does it imply exclusivity to the divine essence or negation of other intentions?
3
Explain the commentator's resolution
He interprets 'for His face' as negating any share for other than Allah, not negating personal benefit. Thus lower levels are included as long as they are not mixed with other than Allah.
The resolution distinguishes between partnership (shirk) and personal benefit.
4
Evaluate the resolution
Check if the resolution preserves the integrity of sincerity while allowing for the believer's natural desire for reward. Does it avoid the objection?
Consider whether the lower levels still have an element of self-interest; the resolution says as long as that interest is not a partner with Allah, it is acceptable.

The Three Degrees of Worship and the Meaning of 'Khāliṣ'
Bajuri defines rajā' and khāliṣ and explains the three degrees of worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three degrees of worship and explain how the term 'khāliṣ' (pure) applies to each.

Core Idea
Purity of intention applies to all degrees of worship, not just the highest.

In his commentary on the poem, al-Bajuri unpacks two key terms: rajā' and khāliṣ. While rajā' sometimes means fear in the Quran, the focus here is on khāliṣ—sincerity free from worldly motivations. Bajuri then presents the classic three degrees of worship and shows that the purity required by the poem fits all of them, depending on the type of impurity avoided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'khāliṣ' in context
In the line, the poet hopes his work is 'pure for Allah's noble face.' Bajuri explains 'pure' means free from contaminants like love of fame or praise.
2
Recognize the three degrees of worship
Bajuri lists them: (1) seeking reward and fearing punishment (lowest), (2) seeking honor through worship (middle), (3) worship because He is your Lord (highest).
The classification is attributed to al-Munawi.
3
Understand how purity applies to all three
If 'pure' means free from nullifiers like showing off, then it applies to any degree. But if it means free from even the highest selfish motives, it applies only to the third.
Bajuri concludes with a nuance: 'if the intended meaning is purity from the impediments of the highest perfection, then it is from the last rank exactly.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَدْ يُطْلَقُ الرَّجَاءُ عَلَى الخَوْفِ، وَمِنْهُ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَاَرْجُوا الْيَوْمَ الْآخِرَ [العنكبوت: ٣٦] أَيْ: خَافُوهُ، وَقَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى : وما لَكُمْ لَا تَرْجُونَ لِلَّهِ وَقَارَام [نوح: ۱۳] أَيْ: لَا تَخَافُونَ عَظَمَةَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى - وَأَمَّا بِالقَصْرِ فَهُوَ : النَّاحِيَةُ ؛ كَمَا فِي المُخْتَارِ (۱). قَوْلُهُ : (أَنْ يَكُونَ خَالِصَا) أَيْ : مِنَ المُكَدَّرَاتِ الَّتِي تُحْبِطُ العَمَلَ كَـ: حُبِّ الظُّهُورِ، وَالشُّهْرَةِ، وَالمَحْمَدَةِ . وَحَيْثُ كَانَ المُرَادُ مَا ذُكِرَ ، صَدَقَ (۲) ذَلِكَ بِكُلِّ مِنَ المَرَاتِبِ الثَّلَاثَةِ الَّتِي ذَكَرُوهَا لِلعِبَادَةِ الخَالِيَةِ مِنَ الحُرْمَةِ، وَهِيَ : (۱) - أَنْ تَعْبُدَ اللهَ تَعَالَى طَالِباً لِلثَّوَابِ وَهَارِباً مِنَ العِقَابِ (۳)، وَهَذِهِ أَدْنَاهَا . (٢) - وَأَنْ تَعْبُدَهُ تَعَالَى لِتَتَشَرَّفَ بِعِبَادَتِهِ وَالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَيْهِ، وَهَذِهِ أَعْلَى مِنَ الَّتِي قَبْلَهَا . (۳) - وَأَنْ تَعْبُدَهُ تَعَالَى لِكَوْنِهِ إِلَهَكَ وَأَنْتَ عَبْدَهُ، وَهَذِهِ أَعْلَاهَا ؛ كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ المِنَاوِيُّ (٤) . وَأَمَّا إِذَا كَانَ المُرَادُ أَنْ يَكُونَ خَالِصاً مِنْ مَوَانِعِ الكَمَالِ الأَعْلَى (٥) ، كَانَ مِنَ الرُّتْبَةِ الأَخِيرَةِ عَيْناً، فَلْيُتَا مَّلْ .
And 'rajā'' is sometimes applied to fear, and from it is His statement: 'and hope for the Last Day' (Al-Ankabut: 36) meaning: fear Him, and His statement: 'And what is the matter with you that you do not hope for majesty from Allah' (Nuh: 13) meaning: you do not fear the greatness of Allah. And as for with qasr (the letter qaf with sukun?), it is: the side; as in al-Mukhtar. His statement: 'that it be pure' means: from the contaminants that nullify action, like love of appearance, fame, and praise. And when the intended meaning is what was mentioned, that is true of all three degrees that they have mentioned for worship that is free from prohibition. They are: (1) that you worship Allah seeking reward and fleeing from punishment; this is the lowest. (2) that you worship Him to honor yourself by His worship and relation to Him; this is higher than the previous. (3) that you worship Him because He is your God and you are His servant; this is the highest, as al-Munawi mentioned. But if the intended meaning is that it be pure from the impediments of the highest perfection, then it is from the last rank exactly. So let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) by al-Bajuri on the line of the poem that mentions the author's hope that his work be pure for Allah's noble face. The commentator first explains the word 'rajā'' (hope) used in the line, noting that it can be applied to fear, citing two Quranic verses where 'rajā'' is interpreted as fear. He then distinguishes the word with qasr (shortening) meaning 'side' as in al-Mukhtar. Then he explains 'khāliṣan' (pure) as meaning free from things that nullify deeds, such as love of fame and praise. He then discusses the three degrees of worship to which this purity can apply: (1) worship seeking reward and fleeing punishment (lowest), (2) worship to honor oneself by being associated with Allah (higher), and (3) worship because He is your Lord (highest). This classification is attributed to al-Munawi. He concludes with a nuance: if the intention is purity from the highest impediments, then it is specifically the last rank. This is a definitional passage that also serves as a moral classification.

Key Takeaway
The term 'khāliṣ' in the poem applies to all three levels of worship, but when purity means freedom from even the highest spiritual obstacles, it specifically denotes the highest level.

Al-Anbabiy's Critique of 'Pure for His Face' in All Degrees of Worship
Al-Anbabiy critiques Bajuri's claim regarding applicability of 'pure for His face'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical tension in applying 'khāliṣ li-l-dhāt' to all degrees of worship and evaluate the proposed resolution.

Core Idea
Precision in theological language: the phrase 'for His face' must be interpreted consistently across all levels of worship.

This lesson examines al-Anbabiy's critical refinement of al-Bajuri's commentary on the poem. Al-Bajuri claimed that the phrase 'pure for His noble face' applies to all three degrees of worship (fear, hope, love). Al-Anbabiy objects: the wording implies exclusive focus on Allah's essence, which is only true for the highest degree. You will learn how the commentator resolves this tension by reinterpreting the phrase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under critique
Locate al-Bajuri's claim that 'pure for His noble face' applies to all three degrees. The passage references this as 'صَدَقَ ذَلِكَ بِكُلِّ مِنَ المَرَاتِبِ' (that is true for all the degrees).
This claim appears at the beginning of the passage in the line being commented on.
2
Understand the objection
Al-Anbabiy objects: 'how is this possible when the attribution of purity to the essence requires that the essence is the intended object, and that is limited to the highest degree?' The phrase 'for His face' seems to demand exclusive focus on Allah's essence, which is only fully realized in the highest degree of worship.
3
Extract the resolution
The response ('ويمكن أن يقال') reinterprets 'for His noble face' to mean that the composition is for the pure essence, i.e., not for other than Allah and not shared. This allows it to apply to all degrees because even lower degrees, while they involve seeking reward, are still ultimately for Allah's sake.
The resolution redefines the phrase as negating partnership and other-than-Allah purposes.
4
Analyze the further clarification
The passage then considers two possible meanings of 'khāliṣ': (1) free from hypocrisy and nullifiers, or (2) free from any secondary intention. The first allows broader application; the second would limit it to the highest degree. The commentator indicates the first is intended, though the apparent meaning is the second.
This shows the precision in distinguishing different shades of sincerity.
5
Evaluate the critique
Assess whether al-Anbabiy's objection is valid. The tension is real: literal reading of 'for His face' suggests exclusive focus. The resolution is a reinterpretation that preserves the phrase's applicability while maintaining theological consistency. This exemplifies the work of hashiya writers in refining earlier commentaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأنبابي
قوله : (وَقَدْ يُطْلَقُ الرَّجَاءُ عَلَى الخَوْفِ أى حقيقةً. اهـ (صبَّان)
قوله : (صَدَقَ ذَلِكَ بِكُلِّ مِنَ المَرَاتِبِ كيف هذا مع نسبة الخلوص للذات، والخالص للذات لا يكون إلَّا حيث كانتِ الذَّات هي المقصودة، فيكون قاصراً على أعلى المراتب؟
ويمكن أن يقال : أنَّ المراد بقوله : لِوَجْهِهِ الكَرِيمِ أنَّ التأليف لخالص ذاته ; بمعنى : أنه ليس لغير الله، ولا يشركه فيه غيره ; فكأنه قال : أن يكون التأليف منفياً عنِ الشَّركة الله، ومنفياً عنه كونه لغير الله تعالى، فيصح الصدق حينئذ .
نعم؛ لو لم يقيد بالجار والمجرور صدق بكل المراتب ; إلا أن يقال : مقصود المحشي أنَّه يحتمل أن يراد بالخالص لذاته ما ليس معه رياء ولا شيء مما يحبط العمل لا ما ليس معه شيء شيء أصلاً، بحيث تكون الذات هي المقصودة فقط، وحينئذ يصدق... إلخ، وإن كان هذا الاحتمال خلاف المتبادر، بل المتبادر هو الثاني المشار إليه بقوله : وأما إذا كان المراد ... إلخ».
Al-Anbabiy: His statement: 'And rajā' is applied to fear' meaning literally. End. (al-Sabban). His statement: 'That is true for all the degrees' – how is this possible when the attribution of purity to the essence (dhāt) requires that the essence is the intended object, and that is limited to the highest degree? It can be said: The intended meaning of His statement 'for His noble face' is that the composition is for the pure essence, meaning that it is not for other than Allah and none shares with Him in it; so it is as if he said: the composition is negated from partnership with Allah and negated from being for other than Allah, so the truth (of the statement) holds then. Yes, if it were not restricted by the prepositional phrase, it would be true for all degrees; except that it can be said that the aim of the commentator (al-muhashshī) is that it is possible that by 'pure for the essence' is meant what has no hypocrisy nor anything that nullifies the action, not what has nothing at all, such that the essence is the only intended object, and then it is true... etc., although this possibility is contrary to the obvious meaning, rather the obvious is the second indicated by his statement: 'But if the intended meaning is... etc.'
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) by al-Anbabiy on al-Bajuri's commentary. Al-Anbabiy raises an objection: al-Bajuri claimed that the phrase 'pure for His noble face' applies to all three degrees of worship. But the wording 'for His face' implies that the essence (dhāt) of Allah is the sole purpose, which is only true for the highest degree. The response (ويمكن أن يقال) offers a reinterpretation: the phrase means the composition is for the pure essence, i.e., it is not for anyone other than Allah nor does anyone share in it. Thus it can apply to all degrees because even the lower degrees, though they involve seeking reward, are still ultimately for Allah's sake (since reward is from Him). A further clarification (starting with 'نعم') notes that if the phrase were not restricted by 'li-wajhihi', it would indeed apply to all degrees; but as it stands, the restriction indicates the highest degree unless we interpret 'khāliṣan' as meaning free from hypocrisy and nullifiers rather than free from any secondary intention. This shows the precision in interpreting the poem's wording.

Key Terms
الذات
الخالص للذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main objection al-Anbabiy raises against al-Bajuri's claim?
A) The phrase 'for His noble face' is not mentioned in the Quran.
B) The phrase implies exclusive focus on Allah's essence, which is only for the highest degree.
C) The degrees of worship are not clearly defined.
D) The word 'khāliṣ' is ambiguous and cannot be used.

Key Takeaway
Al-Anbabiy's critique shows that theological claims must be examined for consistency; reinterpretation of key phrases can resolve apparent contradictions without abandoning the original wording.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In a fiqh context, consider the hadith 'Actions are by intentions.' Can an act with mixed intentions (seeking reward and seeking Allah's pleasure) be considered 'pure for Allah'? Use al-Anbabiy's resolution to argue either way.

Ta'wil, Tafwid, and the Etymology of Qāliṣ
Bajuri explains the necessity of ta'wil for anthropomorphic expressions and defines the term qāliṣ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between ta'wil and tafwid in interpreting divine attributes and trace the metaphorical extension of the term qāliṣ from its literal meaning.

Core Idea
Ta'wil and tafwid are complementary methods to guard divine transcendence.

When Qur'anic verses speak of Allah's 'face' or 'hand', Muslims unanimously agree that these cannot be taken literally. But how exactly should we understand them? The Ash'ari tradition offers two approaches: ta'wil (interpretation) and tafwid (delegation). In this lesson, we examine al-Bajuri's commentary on a poetic line that touches on both, and we also unpack the precise vocabulary he uses — including the rare term qāliṣ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: anthropomorphic verses
Notice that the passage begins with 'if there occurs in a Book or Sunnah something that suggests that the Exalted has a face or hand.' Recognise that this is the core issue: how to speak about Allah without falling into tashbīh (comparison).
2
Distinguish the two agreed-upon approaches
Bajuri states that both Salaf and Khalaf agree on the need for ta'wil (turning from the apparent meaning), but they differ on specifying the intended meaning. The Salaf delegate (tafwid) the true meaning to Allah, while the Khalaf assign specific interpretations (e.g., face = essence, hand = power).
This is not a disagreement on the necessity of ta'wil, only on the level of specification.
3
Analyse the poetic reference and its commentary
The poet says 'interpret it or delegate and intend its transcendence.' Bajuri clarifies that this line endorses both options, contrary to what one might initially think. Observe how the commentary resolves the apparent tension.
The matn (poem) itself is concise; the sharh expands its meaning.
4
Trace the etymology of qāliṣ
Bajuri provides the literal origin of qāliṣ: a camel's lip that is shorter than its counterpart. Then he shows it is figuratively applied to anything deficient. Note the technical classification: it is a majāz mursal (by one or two degrees) or istiʿāra.
This step illustrates how precise lexical knowledge enriches theological terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (لِوَجْهِهِ الكَرِيمِ اعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ إِذَا وَرَدَ فِي كِتَابٍ أَوْ سُنَّةٍ مَا يُوهِمُ أَنَّهُ تَعَالَى لَهُ وَجْهُ أَوْ يَدٌ أَوْ نَحْو ذَلِكَ (۱) ، فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَأْوِيلِهِ؛ بِمَعْنَى : صَرْفِهِ عَنْ ظَاهِرِهِ، وَهَذَا مَحَلُّ وِفَاقٍ مِنَ السَّلَفِ وَالخَلَفِ ; غَايَةُ الأَمْرِ أَنَّهُمُ اخْتَلَفُوا فِي تَعْيِينِ المَعْنَى المُرَادِ : - فَالسَّلَفُ لَا يُعَينُونَهُ، بَلْ يُفَوِّضُونَهُ إِلَيْهِ تَعَالَى، فَيَقُولُونَ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَيَبْقَى وَجْهُ رَبِّكَ [الرحمن: ٢٧] ، وَقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى : ﴿وَيَدُ اللَّهِ فَوْقَ أَيْدِيهِمْ ﴾ [الفتح: ١٠]: لَيْسَ لَهُ تَعَالَى وَجْهُ كَوَجْهِنَا، وَلَا يَدٌ كَيّدِنَا، وَلَا يَعْلَمُ المُرَادُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ تَعَالَى. - وَالخَلَفُ يُعَيِّنُونَهُ، فَيَقُولُونَ فِيمَا ذُكِرَ : لَيْسَ لَهُ وَجْهُ كَوَجْهِنا، وَلَا يَدٌ كَيَدِنَا، وَالمُرَادُ مِنَ الوَجْهِ» : الذَّاتُ، وَمِنَ اليَدِ»: القُدْرَةُ، وَهَذَا هُوَ المُرَادُ مِنْ قَوْلِ صَاحِبِ الجَوْهَرَةِ» : وَكُلُّ نَضٌ أَوْهَمَ التَّشْبِيهَا أَوّلَهُ أَوْ فَرْض وَرُمْ تَنْزِيهَا كَمَا يُؤخَذُ مِنْ شَرْحِهَا» لِلشَّيْخِ عَبْدِ السَّلَامِ ) ، وَإِنْ كَانَ المُتَبَادَ رُ(۳) مِنَ البَيْتِ المَذْكُورِ خِلَافَهُ . قَوْلُهُ : (لَيْسَ قَالِصا يُطْلَقُ القَالِصُ فِي الأَصْلِ عَلَى : إِحْدَى شَفَتَيِ البَعِيرِ وَنَحْوِهِ النَّاقِصَةِ عَنْ أُخْتِهَا، كَمَا يُسْتَفَادُ مِنَ المُخْتَارِ ) ، ثُمَّ أُطلِقَ عَلَى النَّاقِصِ(٥) مَجَازاً مُرْسَلاً : إِمَّا بِمَرْتَبَةٍ ) وَهُوَ الأَقْرَبُ، أَوْ بِمَرْتَبَتَيْنِ، أَوْ مَجَازاً بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ.
His statement: 'for His noble face'. Know that when there occurs in a book or Sunnah something that suggests that the Exalted has a face or hand or the like, it must be interpreted (ta'wil), i.e., turned away from its apparent meaning, and this is a matter of agreement between the Salaf and Khalaf; the only difference is in specifying the intended meaning. The Salaf do not specify it, but delegate it to the Exalted. They say concerning similar verses like 'And the face of your Lord remains' (al-Rahman:27) and 'And the hand of Allah is above their hands' (al-Fath:10): He does not have a face like our face, nor a hand like our hand, and no one knows the intended meaning except Allah. The Khalaf specify it, and say concerning what was mentioned: He does not have a face like our face, nor a hand like our hand, and the intended meaning of 'face' is the essence (dhāt), and of 'hand' is power (qudra). This is what is intended by the statement of the author of al-Jawhara: 'And every text that suggests similarity (tashbīh), interpret it or delegate and intend its transcendence (tanzīh)', as is taken from its commentary by Shaykh Abd al-Salam, even though the immediate understanding from the mentioned verse is contrary to that. His statement: 'not qāliṣan'. The word qāliṣ originally applies to one of the lips of a camel and the like that is missing (shorter) than its counterpart, as is derived from al-Mukhtar. Then it was applied to the deficient as a figurative usage (majāz mursal): either by one degree, which is closer, or by two degrees, or as a metaphorical usage (istiʿāra).
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) by al-Bajuri explaining two phrases from the poem: 'li-wajhihi al-karīm' and 'laysa qāliṣan'. For the first, he discusses the principle of ta'wil (interpretation) when scriptural texts seem to attribute body parts to Allah. He explains that both the Salaf and Khalaf agree that such texts cannot be taken literally, but they differ: the Salaf delegate the true meaning to Allah without specifying, while the Khalaf assign specific meanings (e.g., face = essence, hand = power). He quotes the poet of al-Jawhara as endorsing either ta'wil or tafwid. For the second phrase, he gives the literal meaning of 'qāliṣ' (a camel's shortened lip) and its metaphorical extension to anything deficient. This shows the precision of the poet's choice of words.

Key Terms
التأويل
التفويض
القَالِص

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The essence (dhāt)
C) The sight
D) The power

Key Takeaway
Ta'wil and tafwid are both legitimate responses to anthropomorphic verses, and precise terminology like qāliṣ enriches theological expression.

Distinguishing Majaz Mursal from Isti'ara in Bajuri's Commentary
Bajuri explains the metaphorical usage in the author's prayer, distinguishing majaz mursal and isti'ara.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of figurative language (majaz mursal or isti'ara) used in a given textual analysis based on the relationship of transfer (absolute/restricted vs. similarity).

Core Idea
The transfer type (al-alaqa) determines whether it is majaz mursal or isti'ara.

In this passage, Bajuri unpacks the metaphor in the author's prayer 'laysa qalisa' (not a deficient work). He provides a detailed analysis of how the term 'qalis' is used figuratively, distinguishing between two types of figurative language: majaz mursal (based on a relation other than similarity) and isti'ara (based on similarity). This lesson will guide you through his analytical steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original meaning of 'qalis'
The literal meaning is a she-camel with a cut lip. Note that this is a physical deficiency.
2
Understand the transfer to the absolute concept of deficiency
The word is transferred from the specific physical deficiency to the general concept of being deficient (itlaq).
This is the first step in the majaz mursal analysis.
3
Determine the relationship of transfer
If the relationship is absolute-restricted (i.e., the general concept applied to a specific instance), it's majaz mursal. If the relationship is similarity (tashbih), it's isti'ara.
Bajuri gives both possibilities in the passage.
4
Test the interpretation
Apply Bajuri's three interpretations: (a) deficiency in sincerity, (b) deficiency in benefit, (c) physical deficiency. Identify which type of figuration each interpretation uses.
This shows how the same term can have multiple figurative analyses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٣) وَاللهَ أَرْجُو أَنْ يَكُونَ خَالِصَا لِوَجْهِهِ الكَرِيمِ لَيْسَ قَالِصَا
وَبَيَانُ ذَلِكَ : أَنَّهُ إِذَا لُوحِظَ أَنَّ العَلَاقَةَ الإِطْلَاقُ وَالتَّقِييدُ (۱) ، وَنُقِلَ عَنِ المَعْنَى الأَصْلِيِّ إِلَى مُطْلَقِ النَّاقِصِ، وَاسْتُعْمِلَ فِي النَّاقِصِ المَعْنَوِي لِكَوْنِهِ فَرْداً مِنْ ذَلِكَ المُطْلَقِ ، فَهُوَ مَجَازٌ مُرْسَلٌ بِمَرْتَبَةٍ . وَإِذَا لُوحِظَ أَنَّ العَلَاقَةَ مَا ذُكِرَ (٢) ، وَنُقِلَ عَنِ المَعْنَى الأَصْلِيِّ إِلَى مُطْلَقِ النَّاقِصِ، ثُمَّ نُقِلَ عَنْهُ إِلَى النَّاقِصِ المَعْنَوِيِّ، فَهُوَ مَجَازٌ مُرْسَلٌ بِمَرْتَبَتَيْنِ . وَإِذَا لُوحِظَ أَنَّ العَلَاقَةَ المُشَابَهَةُ، كَانَ مَجَازاً بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ . ثُمَّ إِنْ كَانَ المُرَادُ : أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ نَاقِصاً بِسَبَبٍ قَصْدِ الظُّهُورِ وَالمَحْمَدَةِ وَنَحْوِ ذَلِكَ، كَانَ مَا ذُكِرَ تَأكِيدا لِقَوْلِهِ : أَنْ يَكُونَ خَالِصاً لِوَجْهِهِ الكَرِيمِ . وَإِنْ كَانَ المُرَادُ : أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ نَاقِصاً فِي النَّفْعِ بِحَيْثُ يَكُونُ مَطْرُوحاً فِي زَوَايَا الإِهْمَالِ لَا يُنْتَفَعُ كَانَ قَوْلُهُ: وَأَنْ يَكُونَ نَافِعاً» تَوْضِيحاً لِذَلِكَ. وَإِنْ كَانَ المُرَادُ : أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ نَاقِصاً حِسًّا بِحَيْثُ لَا يَتِمُّ بِأَنْ يُعَوِّقَهُ عَائِقٌ عَنْ إِكْمَالِهِ، كَانَ ذَلِكَ مُغَايراً لِمَا قَبْلَهُ وَمَا بَعْدَهُ ، لَكِنْ فِيهِ نَوْعُ بُعْدِ ) ، فَلْيُفهم .
And the explanation of that: if it is observed that the relationship is absolute and restricted, and a transfer is made from the original meaning to the absolute of 'deficient', and it is used for the spiritual deficient because it is an individual of that absolute, then it is a mursal metaphor of one degree. And if it is observed that the relationship is what was mentioned, and a transfer is made from the original meaning to the absolute of deficient, then transferred from it to the spiritual deficient, then it is a mursal metaphor of two degrees. And if it is observed that the relationship is similarity, then it is a metaphor by isti'ara. Then if what is meant is that it not be deficient due to the intention of appearance and praise and the like, then what was mentioned is an emphasis of his statement: 'pure for His noble face.' And if what is meant is that it not be deficient in benefit such that it is thrown in the corners of neglect, not benefited from, then his statement 'and that it be beneficial' is a clarification of that. And if what is meant is that it not be deficient physically such that it is not completed by an obstructing obstacle preventing its completion, then that is different from what preceded and what follows, but there is a type of distance in it, so understand.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage explains the metaphor in the verse 'Laysa qalisa'. The commentator first discusses two possibilities of majaz mursal: one involving a single transfer from the literal meaning of a she-camel with a cut lip to the absolute concept of deficiency, then to the spiritual deficiency—that is a one-step metaphor. The second involves two transfers, from original to absolute, then to spiritual, making it a two-step majaz mursal. Then he mentions that if the relationship is similarity, it is isti'ara. He then interprets the meaning of 'not deficient' in three ways: first, as not seeking fame, which makes the previous statement an emphasis; second, as not lacking benefit, which clarifies the author's next verse; third, as not physically incomplete, which is a different interpretation but somewhat far-fetched.

Key Terms
القَالِص
المَجَاز الْمُرْسَل
الِاسْتِعَارَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Bajuri, when the transfer from the original meaning to the figurative meaning is based on the relationship of 'absolute and restricted' (itlaq wa taqyid), what type of figurative language is used?
A) Haqiqa (literal)
B) Isti'ara
C) Majaz mursal
D) Tashbih

Key Takeaway
Bajuri's analysis shows that a single word can be interpreted through multiple figurative lenses, each based on a different relationship of transfer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Parsing 'al-Khalf' and 'ala Thalatha Aqwal'
Lexical and grammatical parsing of key terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the phrase 'ala thalatha aqwal' and explain why tanwin is required on 'thalatha'.

Core Idea
Grammatical parsing unlocks precise meanings in Islamic logic texts.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary is a grammatical and lexical parsing of two key phrases: 'al-khalf' meaning disagreement, and 'ala thalatha aqwal' meaning 'upon three statements'. The commentator clarifies that 'khalf' is an ism masdar for ikhtilaf, and that 'aqwal' is a badal (substitute) for 'thalatha', requiring tanwin. Understanding these grammatical nuances is essential for correctly interpreting logical summaries (matn).

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms 'al-khalf' and 'ala thalatha aqwal'. Note that 'al-khalf' is defined as a noun of source (ism masdar) meaning disagreement. Recognize that 'ism masdar' is a verbal noun that functions like a masdar but is not identical.
The term 'khalf' is not the usual masdar 'ikhtilaf'; it is a distinct form with the same meaning.
2
Understand the elliptical expression
The commentator notes conciseness (ikhtisar) in 'fī jawāz al-ishtighāl bihi', which implies both permission and prohibition. This shows that the author intends to cover both sides of the issue in a compressed manner.
Ellipsis is common in logical texts to save space.
3
Parse the phrase syntactically
In 'ala thalatha aqwal', identify that 'aqwal' is a badal (substitute) for 'thalatha'. This requires 'thalatha' to have tanwin (nunation) to prevent it from being read as a genitive construct (idafa). Without tanwin, 'thalatha' would be the first term of an idafa, making 'aqwal' the possessed noun, which would change the meaning.
The tanwin signals that 'thalatha' is indefinite and that 'aqwal' is an appositive, not a possessive.
4
Apply the rule to avoid misinterpretation
If one reads 'ala thalatha aqwal' without tanwin, it would imply that the three statements possess the disagreement, i.e., the disagreement belongs to the statements. With tanwin, it correctly indicates that the disagreement is about the three statements, i.e., the three statements themselves are what is disagreed upon.
This grammatical distinction is crucial for accurately understanding the logical structure of the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالخُلْفُ) أي: الاخْتِلَافُ، فَالخُلْفُ اسْمُ مَصْدَرٍ بِمَعْنَى : الاخْتِلَافِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (فِي جَوَازِ الِاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ) أَيْ: وَفِي عَدَمِهِ، فَفِيهِ اكْتِفَاءُ وَالضَّمِيرُ عَائِدٌ لِعِلْمِ المَنْطِقِ، لَكِنْ بِمَعْنَى القِسْمِ الثَّانِي مِنْهُ ؛ لِأَنَّ كَلَامَ المُصَنِّفِ مَحْمُولٌ عَلَيْهِ، كَمَا عُلِم مِمَّا مَرَّ. قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْوَالِ أَيْ : كَائِنٌ عَلَيْهَا، مِنْ كَيْنُونَةِ المُتَعَلِّقِ عَلَى المُتَعَلَّقِ )؛ لِمَا هُوَ ظَاهِرٌ مِنْ أَنَّ «الخُلْفُ) بِمَعْنَى : الِاخْتِلَافِ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِالأَقْوَالِ الثَّلَاثَةِ. : وَيَتَعَيَّنُ قَرَاءَةَ ثَلَاثَة بِالتَّنْوِينِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ قَوْلِهِ : «أَقْوَالِ بَدَلاً مِنْ «ثَلَاثَةٍ»، وَلَا يَجُوزُ تَرْكُ التَّنْوِينِ
His statement: (and the dispute) i.e., the disagreement, for 'al-khalf' is a noun of source meaning disagreement. And his statement: (regarding the permissibility of occupying oneself with it) i.e., and its impermissibility, so there is conciseness in it, and the pronoun refers to the science of logic, but in the sense of the second division of it, because the author's words are interpreted accordingly, as known from what has passed. His statement: (upon three statements) i.e., existing upon them, from the state of the dependent on the depended; because it is apparent that 'al-khalf' meaning disagreement is dependent on the three statements. And it is necessary to read 'thalatha' with tanwin, and then his word 'aqwal' is a substitute for 'thalatha', and it is not permissible to omit the tanwin.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and grammatical parsing exercise. The commentator defines 'al-khalf' as 'al-ikhtilaf' (disagreement) and notes it is an 'ism masdar' (noun of source). He then explains the phrase 'fī jawāz al-ishtighāl bihi' includes both permission and prohibition by conciseness (ikhtisār). The pronoun refers to 'science of logic' but specifically the second type. Finally, he parses 'alā thalātha aqwāl': 'thalātha' must be with tanwin, and 'aqwāl' is a substitute (badal) for 'thalātha'. Grammatically, this is because 'aqwāl' is the dependent (muta‘allaq) of the disagreement, so it cannot be a possessive construction (idāfa) without tanwin; the tanwin indicates that 'thalātha' is indefinite and 'aqwāl' is its appositive.

Key Terms
الخلف
اسم مصدر
بدل
تنوين

C) Because it is a plural noun.
D) Because the phrase is in the accusative case.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'ala thalatha aqwal' is correctly parsed with tanwin on thalatha and aqwāl as badal, avoiding an incorrect idafa.

Analyzing the Dialectical Structure of the Logic Permissibility Debate
Author presents three scholarly opinions on studying logic, with objection and answer about the author's classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection, answer, and critique in a classical jadal exchange and evaluate the implicit reasoning in a commentary.

Core Idea
The disagreement over logic is actually about its subdivisions, not the discipline itself.

In this passage, al-Bajuri unpacks a terse statement by al-Bannani about three opinions on studying logic. An objection arises: why mention a disputed subdivision when the undisputed one is the main topic? The defense uses an implicit argument. This lesson trains you to dissect such dialectical moves, which are foundational in the Islamic scholarly tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions and their scope
Read the opening: 'The disagreement concerning permissibility is upon three opinions.' Note that al-Bajuri immediately clarifies that this refers to the second subdivision, not the first.
The first subdivision is what the poem covers; it is undisputed.
2
Locate the objection (mu'tarid)
The objector argues: since disagreement only applies to the second subdivision, the author should not have mentioned the disagreement at all; he should have only given the ruling for the first.
The objection is about the relevance and structure of the matn.
3
Extract the answer (jawab)
The answerer defends: the author intended to explain the first subdivision, but that intention led him to also mention the second and its disagreement.
This answer treats the author's text as a coherent whole.
4
Analyze the critique (naqd) of the answer
The critic points out that if the author left his intended topic (the first subdivision) unmentioned, that is a flaw because it is more important. This sets up a tension.
The critique reveals a potential inconsistency in the answer.
5
Identify the implicit resolution
The resolution: by stating the condition for permissibility of the second subdivision (full intellect, practicing Sunna and Quran), the author implicitly declares the first subdivision permissible absolutely, since it lacks that danger. Thus, the first is covered indirectly.
This is a subtle logical inference, common in commentary analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في جواز الاشتغال به (١٥) والخلف في جواز الاشتغال به على ثلاثة أقوال. إذا تمهد هذا علمت أنه يتعين حمل كلام المصنف على هذا القسم. وحينئذ يرد عليه: أنه إذا لم يكن في القسم الأول - الذي منه ما في هذا النظم - خلاف، وإنما الخلاف في القسم الثاني، كان ينبغي له أن لا يذكر هذا الخلاف المتعلق بالقسم الثاني، وإنما يذكر حكم القسم الأول. وأجيب: بأن المصنف قصد أولاً بيان حكم القسم الأول، فجره ذلك القصد إلى ذكر حكم القسم الثاني، فترجم له وبين الخلاف فيه. ونوقش هذا الجواب: بأنه يلزم عليه أنه ترك ما فصده، مع أنه أهم مما ذكر؛ اللهم إلا أن يقال: إنه ذكره ضمنًا؛ لأنه بين أن الأصح جواز القسم الثاني لكامل الفريحة ممارس السنة والكتاب بخلاف غيره؛ لعدم الأمن عليه من ضلالات الفلاسفة، ولا يخفى أن هذا يتضمن جواز القسم الأول مطلقًا؛ لعدم المحذور المذكور، فليتأمل.
Chapter: On the permissibility of occupying oneself with it. (15) And the disagreement concerning the permissibility of occupying oneself with it is upon three opinions. When this is established, you know that the author's words must be interpreted to refer to this division. Then an objection is raised against him: that if there is no disagreement in the first division—from which is what is in this poem—and the disagreement is only in the second division, he should not have mentioned this disagreement pertaining to the second division, but rather should have mentioned the ruling of the first division. And it was answered: that the author first intended to explain the ruling of the first division, and that intention led him to mention the ruling of the second division, so he gave it a heading and explained the disagreement in it. And this answer was critiqued: that it follows from it that he left what he intended, which is more important than what he mentioned; unless it be said that he mentioned it implicitly, because he explained that the correct view is the permissibility of the second division for one with complete intellect who practices the Sunna and the Book, unlike others, due to lack of safety from the errors of the philosophers. And it is not hidden that this implies the permissibility of the first division absolutely, due to the absence of the mentioned harm. So let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange concerning the classification of the author's discussion on the permissibility of studying logic. The author (the matn) states that there are three opinions on the permissibility of studying logic. The commentator (Bajuri) then clarifies that the author's words refer specifically to the second subdivision of logic, not the first. An objection is raised: since the disagreement only exists in the second subdivision, the author should not have mentioned it at all, but should have focused on the first subdivision, which is undisputed. The answer is that the author intended to first explain the ruling of the first subdivision, but that led him to also address the second. However, this answer is critiqued: if the author left his intended topic (the first subdivision) unmentioned, that is a flaw because the first is more important. A possible resolution is that the author did mention the first implicitly—by stating the condition for permissibility of the second (inference of the first's permissibility). The passage ends by inviting reflection. This is a typical jadal-style objection and answer in the commentary tradition.

الخلف
الجواب
النقاش
ضمنًا

A) By stating that the second subdivision is permissible for experts in Sunna and Quran, implying the first is permissible for everyone.
B) By explicitly stating the ruling of the first subdivision elsewhere.
C) By using the word 'implicit' in the text.
D) By relying on a consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic scholarly debates, an implicit argument can resolve an apparent inconsistency when the explicit text is interpreted coherently.

Dissecting the Descriptors 'Well-Known' and 'Sound' for a Scholarly Opinion
Commentator defines and defends the well-known sound opinion on permissibility of studying logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the two descriptors 'well-known' (mashhura) and 'sound' (sahiha) applied to a scholarly opinion, and explain how their combination distinguishes it from other opinions.

Core Idea
An opinion's authority rests on two independent criteria: widespread acceptance and strong evidence.

In the Islamic scholarly tradition, not all opinions carry equal weight. When a commentator like al-Bajuri discusses the ruling on studying logic, he carefully labels the preferred opinion as 'the well-known sound statement'. But what do these terms mean, and how do they work together? In this lesson, we will dissect the commentator's explanation to understand the precise logic behind this characterization, including how he defends it against a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opinion being characterized
Locate the exact opinion that the commentator labels as 'the well-known sound statement'. It is the permissibility of studying logic for one with a sound intellect. Note the context: this is one of several possible rulings on logic (obligatory, forbidden, permissible).
The opinion is not the only one; it is the one the commentator advocates as the best.
2
Distinguish the two components of the characterization
The commentator defines 'well-known' (mashhura) as having many proponents, and 'sound' (sahiha) as having strong evidence. Understand that each component is independent. An opinion could be well-known but weak in evidence, or sound but not well-known. The combination is what makes this opinion special.
The Arabic terms are explained explicitly: بسبب كثرة قائلها and بسبب قوة دليلها.
3
Reconstruct the objection and the response
The commentator anticipates an objection: if this opinion is both well-known and sound, then the other opinions (like obligation or prohibition) would lack these qualities, which is false because they are also well-known. The response clarifies that the uniqueness lies in the combination of both qualities, not each individually. The other opinions may be well-known but not sound (in the commentator's view), or sound but not well-known.
This shows a logical move: the defender points out that the objector misinterprets 'combination' as 'each description applied separately', which is not the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في جواز الاشتغال به (١٧) وَالقَوْلَةُ المَشْهُورَةُ الصَّحِيحَة : جَوَازُهُ لِعَامِلِ القَرِيحَة . قَوْلُهُ : ( وَالقَوْلَةُ المَشْهُورَةُ) أَيْ : بِسَبَبٍ كَفْرَةِ قَائِلِيهَا . وَقَوْلُهُ : (الصَّحِيحَهُ) أَيْ : بِسَبَبٍ قُوَّةِ دَلِيلِهَا . فَإِنْ قِيلَ: هَذَا يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ كُلَّا مِنَ القَوْلَيْنِ السَّابِقَيْنِ غَيْرُ مَشْهُورٍ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ. أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ الَّذِي اخْتَصَّتْ بِهِ هَذِهِ القَوْلَةُ مَجْمُوعُ الوَصْفَيْنِ المَذْكُورَيْنِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَلَا يُنَافِي شُهْرَةَ القَوْلَيْنِ الأَوَّلَيْنِ أَيْضاً؛ لِكَثْرَةِ قَائِلِهِمَا.
Chapter: On the permissibility of engaging in it (logic). (17) The well-known sound statement: its permissibility for the one with a sound intellect. His statement: 'the well-known' meaning: due to the multitude of its proponents. And his statement: 'the sound' meaning: due to the strength of its evidence. For if it is said: This implies that each of the two previous sayings is not well-known, and that is not the case. It is answered: That which this statement is specifically characterized by is the combination of the two mentioned descriptions; and then it does not contradict the well-known status of the two previous sayings either, because of the multitude of their proponents.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines and defends the well-known sound opinion (القولة المشهورة الصحيحة) regarding the permissibility of studying logic. The commentator (Bajuri) explains that 'well-known' (مشهورة) refers to the fact that many scholars hold this view, and 'sound' (صحيحة) refers to the strength of its evidence. He then anticipates an objection: if this opinion is characterized by being well-known and sound, then the two earlier opinions mentioned (presumably saying logic is obligatory or forbidden) would lack these qualities, which is untrue because they are also well-known. The answer is that this opinion uniquely combines both descriptions—being well-known and sound—whereas the earlier opinions may be well-known but not sound (in the view of the proponent of this opinion), or sound but not well-known. Thus, the combination is what is special to this opinion, not each description individually.

Key Terms

جواز
عامل القريحة

Key Takeaway
The commentator's analysis teaches us that a robust scholarly opinion often combines widespread acceptance (mashhura) with strong evidence (sahiha), and defending this combination requires logical clarity about what 'combination' means.

Contextual Reinterpretation of Jawaz in Logic
Commentator refines the definition of jawaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'jawaz' in this logical text is redefined as 'legally permitted' and what it now includes.

Core Idea
'Jawaz' must be read as 'legally permitted' to align with logic's guiding purpose.

In logic, precision is everything. When a term like 'jawaz' seems to contradict the very purpose of the discipline, scholars do not ignore it—they reinterpret it. This lesson shows you how the commentator resolves the tension between the literal meaning of jawaz (equal possibility) and the goal of guiding to truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal definition
Find in the passage the literal meaning of jawaz: a thing whose action and omission are equal. This is the standard usul al-fiqh definition.
This definition implies no preference for action or omission.
2
Spot the contradiction
The passage says this literal meaning does not agree with the purpose of guiding to truth (li-yahudiya bihi ila al-sawab), because that purpose prefers action over omission.
The contradiction forces a reinterpretation.
3
Understand the solution
Some scholars redefined jawaz here as 'legally permitted' (ma'dhun fihi shar'an). This new meaning can include obligation (wajib) and recommendation (mandub), which align with preferring action.
Thus jawaz is not the middle level but a broader permission that accommodates the purpose.
4
Apply to the scope
The passage adds that this applies where one is not independent of it (ma lam yustaghna 'anhu), linking back to the earlier context of needing logic.
This ensures the definition fits the specific context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (جَوَازُهُ ... إلخ) مِنَ المَعْلُومِ أَنَّ الجَوَازَ : كَوْنُ الشَّيْء بِحَيْثُ يَسْتَوِي نِسْبَتَا الفِعْلِ وَالتَّرْكِ إِلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ بِهَذَا المَعْنَى لَا يُلَائِمُ قَوْلَهُ: «لِيَهْتَدِي بِهِ إِلَى الصَّوَابِ» ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الفِعْلِ إِلَيْهِ أَرْجَحُ ، وَلِذَلِكَ قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ مَا مَعْنَاهُ : أَنَّهُ أَرَادَ بِالجَوَازِ كَوْنَهُ مَأذُونَا فِيهِ شَرْعاً، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلوُجُوبِ وَالنَّدْبِ ، وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّ مَحَلَّهُ مَا لَمْ يُسْتَغْنَ عَنْهُ ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ . قَوْلُهُ : (لِكَامِلِ القَرِيحَهُ) أَيْ : لِشَخْصٍ كَامِلِ القَرِيحَةِ . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الفَرِيحَةَ فِي الأَصْلِ: «أَوَّلُ مُسْتَنْبَطَ مِنَ المَاءِ»، نُقِلَتْ إِلَى أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ العِلْمِ (۱) ، أَوْ إِلَى المُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنْهُ مُطْلَقاً؛ أَي وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ أَوَّلاً إِمَّا بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ، أَوِ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ، ثُمَّ نُقِلَتْ لِلعَقْلِ إِمَّا بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ أَوِ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ . وَيُتَحَصَّلُ مِنْ هَذَا أَنَّهُ يُحْتَمَلُ : ۱ - أَنْ يَكُونَ التَّجَوُّزَانِ المَذْكُورَانِ مِنَ الْمَجَازِ بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ . ٢ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَا مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ. ٣ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ المَجَازِ بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ، وَالثَّانِي مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ . ٤ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ، وَالثَّانِي مِنَ المَجَازِ بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ.
His statement: 'its permissibility ... etc.' It is known that permissibility (jawaz) is: a thing being such that the two relations of action and omission are equal to it. And in this meaning it does not agree with his statement: 'so that he may be guided by it to what is correct', because that implies that the relation of action to him is preferable. Therefore some have said meaning: that he intended by permissibility (jawaz) that it is legally permitted (ma'dhun fihi shar'an). And then it becomes inclusive of obligation and recommendation, and it is known that its scope is where one is not independent of it, as earlier. His statement: 'for the one with a complete qariha' means: for a person with a complete qariha. And know that al-qariha originally is: 'the first extracted from water', then transferred to the first extracted from knowledge, or to what is extracted from it absolutely, i.e., even if not first, either by isti'ara (metaphor) or by majaz mursal (generalized metaphor). Then it was transferred to intellect, either by isti'ara or majaz mursal. From this it is obtained that it is possible: 1- that both transfers are from metaphor (isti'ara). 2- that both are from generalized metaphor (majaz mursal). 3- that the first is from metaphor and the second from generalized metaphor. 4- that the first is from generalized metaphor and the second from metaphor.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of two key terms in the previous lesson: 'jawaz' (permissibility) and 'kamil al-qariha' (one with a sound intellect). The commentator (Bajuri) begins by noting that the literal meaning of jawaz—where action and omission are equal—contradicts the stated purpose of logic, which is guiding to truth, because that purpose makes action preferable. To resolve this, some scholars reinterpret jawaz here as 'legally permitted' (مأذون فيه شرعاً), which then can include obligation or recommendation, making it consistent. The commentator then explains 'qariha' by tracing its etymology: originally it meant 'the first water extracted from a well' (أول مستنبط من الماء). It was then metaphorically transferred to mean 'first extracted from knowledge' or 'extracted absolutely', and then further to 'the intellect'. He outlines four possible combinations for the types of metaphorical transfer (isti'ara or majaz mursal) for these two steps. This shows the precision with which classical scholars analyzed the language of the texts, ensuring no ambiguity remains.

Key Terms
جواز (jawaz): Here interpreted as 'legally permitted' (مأذون فيه شرعاً) rather than the literal ethical neutrality, to align with the purpose of guiding to truth. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, jawaz often means the middle level between wajib and haram, but here it is broadened to include wajib and mandub.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reinterpret 'jawaz' as 'legally permitted' (مأذون فيه شرعاً)?
A) Because the literal meaning is not well-known
B) Because the literal meaning (equal possibility) contradicts the purpose of guiding to truth
C) Because the original author required it to be obligatory
D) Because it is the only meaning in the Arabic language

Key Takeaway
The term 'jawaz' is redefined from 'equal possibility' to 'legally permitted' to maintain consistency with logic's goal of guiding to truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Compare the definition of 'jawaz' in this passage with its standard usage in usul al-fiqh. What specific change occurs, and why?
?
