Metaphorical Origin of Qariha
Commentator traces the metaphor of qariha from water to intellect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the metaphorical transfer of 'qariha' from water to intellect and identify the types of majaz used.

Core Idea
'Qariha' journeys from well-water to intellect via precise metaphorical transfers.

How does a word for 'first water drawn from a well' come to mean 'intellect'? The answer lies in the careful analysis of metaphorical language. This lesson unpacks the layered transfers and the scholarly options for classifying them as isti'ara or majaz mursal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original meaning
Find in the Arabic the statement: 'al-qariha originally is: the first extracted from water' (أول مستنبط من الماء). This is the concrete, literal meaning.
The root is q-r-h, related to extracting water.
2
Trace the first transfer
The passage says qariha was then transferred to 'the first extracted from knowledge' (أول مستنبط من العلم). This is a metaphorical shift from water to knowledge.
The transfer can be isti'ara (metaphor) or majaz mursal (generalized metaphor).
3
Trace the second transfer
Then it was transferred to 'intellect' (العقل) itself. This is another metaphorical move.
Again, the type of transfer is debated among four possibilities.
4
Classify the transfers
The passage lists four possibilities: both isti'ara, both majaz mursal, first isti'ara second majaz mursal, or first majaz mursal second isti'ara. Understand each combination.
This shows the precision of classical Arabic rhetorical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (جَوَازُهُ ... إلخ) مِنَ المَعْلُومِ أَنَّ الجَوَازَ : كَوْنُ الشَّيْء بِحَيْثُ يَسْتَوِي نِسْبَتَا الفِعْلِ وَالتَّرْكِ إِلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ بِهَذَا المَعْنَى لَا يُلَائِمُ قَوْلَهُ: «لِيَهْتَدِي بِهِ إِلَى الصَّوَابِ» ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الفِعْلِ إِلَيْهِ أَرْجَحُ ، وَلِذَلِكَ قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ مَا مَعْنَاهُ : أَنَّهُ أَرَادَ بِالجَوَازِ كَوْنَهُ مَأذُونَا فِيهِ شَرْعاً، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلوُجُوبِ وَالنَّدْبِ ، وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّ مَحَلَّهُ مَا لَمْ يُسْتَغْنَ عَنْهُ ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ . قَوْلُهُ : (لِكَامِلِ القَرِيحَهُ) أَيْ : لِشَخْصٍ كَامِلِ القَرِيحَةِ . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الفَرِيحَةَ فِي الأَصْلِ: «أَوَّلُ مُسْتَنْبَطَ مِنَ المَاءِ»، نُقِلَتْ إِلَى أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ العِلْمِ (۱) ، أَوْ إِلَى المُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنْهُ مُطْلَقاً؛ أَي وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ أَوَّلاً إِمَّا بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ، أَوِ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ، ثُمَّ نُقِلَتْ لِلعَقْلِ إِمَّا بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ أَوِ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ . وَيُتَحَصَّلُ مِنْ هَذَا أَنَّهُ يُحْتَمَلُ : ۱ - أَنْ يَكُونَ التَّجَوُّزَانِ المَذْكُورَانِ مِنَ الْمَجَازِ بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ . ٢ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَا مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ. ٣ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ المَجَازِ بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ، وَالثَّانِي مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ . ٤ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ، وَالثَّانِي مِنَ المَجَازِ بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ.
His statement: 'its permissibility ... etc.' It is known that permissibility (jawaz) is: a thing being such that the two relations of action and omission are equal to it. And in this meaning it does not agree with his statement: 'so that he may be guided by it to what is correct', because that implies that the relation of action to him is preferable. Therefore some have said meaning: that he intended by permissibility (jawaz) that it is legally permitted (ma'dhun fihi shar'an). And then it becomes inclusive of obligation and recommendation, and it is known that its scope is where one is not independent of it, as earlier. His statement: 'for the one with a complete qariha' means: for a person with a complete qariha. And know that al-qariha originally is: 'the first extracted from water', then transferred to the first extracted from knowledge, or to what is extracted from it absolutely, i.e., even if not first, either by isti'ara (metaphor) or by majaz mursal (generalized metaphor). Then it was transferred to intellect, either by isti'ara or majaz mursal. From this it is obtained that it is possible: 1- that both transfers are from metaphor (isti'ara). 2- that both are from generalized metaphor (majaz mursal). 3- that the first is from metaphor and the second from generalized metaphor. 4- that the first is from generalized metaphor and the second from metaphor.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of two key terms in the previous lesson: 'jawaz' (permissibility) and 'kamil al-qariha' (one with a sound intellect). The commentator (Bajuri) begins by noting that the literal meaning of jawaz—where action and omission are equal—contradicts the stated purpose of logic, which is guiding to truth, because that purpose makes action preferable. To resolve this, some scholars reinterpret jawaz here as 'legally permitted' (مأذون فيه شرعاً), which then can include obligation or recommendation, making it consistent. The commentator then explains 'qariha' by tracing its etymology: originally it meant 'the first water extracted from a well' (أول مستنبط من الماء). It was then metaphorically transferred to mean 'first extracted from knowledge' or 'extracted absolutely', and then further to 'the intellect'. He outlines four possible combinations for the types of metaphorical transfer (isti'ara or majaz mursal) for these two steps. This shows the precision with which classical scholars analyzed the language of the texts, ensuring no ambiguity remains.

Key Terms
قريحة (qariha): Originally 'first extracted water from a well', then transferred metaphorically to 'first extracted knowledge' or 'intellect'. The transfer can be isti'ara (metaphor) or majaz mursal (generalized metaphor). [Note: This term is stable in later mantiq works as a synonym for 'aql (intellect) or fitra (natural disposition), but its original connection to water is often forgotten.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Intellect
C) Knowledge
D) A well

Key Takeaway
The term 'qariha' undergoes two metaphorical transfers from water to knowledge to intellect, with four possible classifications of those transfers.

Clarifying 'Jawaz' in the Context of Studying Logic
Definition of jawaz as legal permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical meaning of jawaz (equal probability) from the legal meaning (permission) as used in the context of studying logic.

Core Idea
Jawaz in logic is not equal permission; it is legal permission that includes obligation.

The commentator begins by noting that the term 'jawaz' in the statement about permissibility of studying logic cannot mean the logical notion of equal probability of action and omission, because studying logic aims at guidance to correctness, which implies a stronger inclination towards action. Therefore, jawaz here must mean legal permissibility, which includes obligation and recommendation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical meaning of jawaz
The passage states that jawaz usually means equal probability of action and omission. Recognize this standard definition.
This is the default understanding in logic.
2
Spot the inconsistency
The phrase 'to be guided by it to correctness' implies a preference for action, which contradicts the idea of equal probability.
The commentator explicitly says this does not suit.
3
Apply the new interpretation
The commentator redefines jawaz as legal permission, which can include obligation and recommendation, resolving the inconsistency.
This reinterpretation aligns the statement with the purpose of studying logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (جَوَازُهُ ... إلخ) مِنَ المَعْلُومِ أَنَّ الجَوَازَ : كَوْنُ الشَّيْء بِحَيْثُ يَسْتَوِي نِسْبَتَا الفِعْلِ وَالتَّرْكِ إِلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ بِهَذَا المَعْنَى لَا يُلَائِمُ قَوْلَهُ: «لِيَهْتَدِي بِهِ إِلَى الصَّوَابِ» ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الفِعْلِ إِلَيْهِ أَرْجَحُ ، وَلِذَلِكَ قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ مَا مَعْنَاهُ : أَنَّهُ أَرَادَ بِالجَوَازِ كَوْنَهُ مَأذُونَا فِيهِ شَرْعاً، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلوُجُوبِ وَالنَّدْبِ ، وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّ مَحَلَّهُ مَا لَمْ يُسْتَغْنَ عَنْهُ ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ . قَوْلُهُ : (لِكَامِلِ القَرِيحَهُ) أَيْ : لِشَخْصٍ كَامِلِ القَرِيحَةِ . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الفَرِيحَةَ فِي الأَصْلِ: «أَوَّلُ مُسْتَنْبَطَ مِنَ المَاءِ»، نُقِلَتْ إِلَى أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ العِلْم (۱) ، أَوْ إِلَى المُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنْهُ مُطْلَقاً ؛ أَي وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ أَوَّلاً إِمَّا بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ، أَوِ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ، ثُمَّ نُقِلَتْ لِلعَقْلِ إِمَّا بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ أَوِ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ . وَيُتَحَصَّلُ مِنْ هَذَا أَنَّهُ يُحْتَمَلُ : 1 - أَنْ يَكُونَ التَّجَوُّزَانِ المَذْكُورَانِ مِنَ الْمَجَازِ بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ . ٢ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَا مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ. ٣ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ المَجَازِ بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ، وَالثَّانِي مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ . ٤ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ، وَالثَّانِي مِنَ المَجَازِ بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ. (ثم تعليق الأنبابي) قوله : (مُسْتَنبط) أي : مستخرج، فالقريحةُ بمعنى: «المقروحة»؛ أي: المستخرجة. قوله : (ثُمَّ نُقِلَتْ لِلعَقْلِ إِمَّا بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ ... إلخ) قد يقال : يصح أن يكون هناك مجاز واحد؛ بأن تنقل القريحة من أوَّل مستنبط من الماء للعقل ؛ لعلاقة المشابهة في الانتفاع بكل . قوله : (إِمَّا بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ) أي: التَّبعيَّة؛ لأنَّ «قريحة على وزن فعيلة» بمعنى: مفعولة، فهي من القرح. نعم؛ إن نظر لكونها جرت مجرى الجوامد كانت أصلية، وهكذا يقال في المجاز المرسل. (ثم الشرشيمي: قوله : (نُقِلَتْ إِلَى أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ العِلْمِ) هذا ظاهر على احتمال الاستعارة، أما على احتمال المجاز المرسل فلا ؛ لأنَّ المنقول إليه أوّلاً إِمَّا أوّل مستنبط علماً أو غيره، أو إلى مطلق مستنبط علماً أو غيره، أو لا أو لا ؛ إلا أن يقال: إنَّه نظر لمآل الأمر باستعماله في الفرد الخاص، أو نقله إليه كما يؤخذ من آخر عبارته، وكذا يقال في قوله بعد: «إِلَى أَوَّلِ سْتَنْبَط ... إلخ»، فالعبارة غير مكررة عن الوجه الظاهر؛ تأمل. (ثم صفحة 147) فصل: في جواز الاشتغال به ١٤٧ (۱۷) وَالقَوْلَةُ المَشْهُورَةُ الصَّحِيحَة : جَوَازُهُ لِعَامِلِ القَرِيحَة الباجوري وَتَقْرِيرُ الِاحْتِمَالِ الأَوَّلِ أَنْ يُقَالَ : شَبَّهَ أَوَّلَ مُسْتَنْبَطَ مِنَ العِلْمِ، أَوِ المُسْتَنْبَطَ مِنْهُ مُطْلَقاً بِأَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ المَاءِ ؛ بِجَامِعِ : الحَيَاةِ فِي كُلِّ وَإِنْ كَانَتِ الحَيَاةُ فِي المُشَبَّهِ لِلرُّوحِ وَفِي المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ للجِسْمِ، وَاسْتُعِيرَ لَفْظُ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ وَهُوَ لَفْظُ القَرِيحَةِ لِلمُشَبَّهِ، ثُمَّ شَبَّهَ العَقْلَ بِالمَعْنَى المَنْقُولِ إِلَيْهِ بِتَنْزِيلِهِ مَنْزِلَةَ المَعْنَى الحَقِيقِيِّ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلمَعْنَى المُتَجَوَّزِ إِلَيْهِ بَعْدُ ؛ بِجَامِعِ : الانْتِفَاعِ وَالِاهْتِدَاءِ بِكُلِّ، وَاسْتُعِيرَ لَفْظُ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ وَهُوَ لَفْظُ «القَرِيحَةِ» لِلمُشَبَّهِ . وَتَقْرِيرُ الاِحْتِمَالِ الثَّانِي أَنْ يُقَالَ : نُقِلَ لَفْظُ الفَرِيحَةِ مِنْ أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ المَاءِ إِلَى أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ العِلْمِ، أَوْ إِلَى المُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنْهُ مُطْلَقاً مَجَازاً مُرْسَلاً إِمَّا بِمَرْتَبَةٍ أَوْ بِأَكْثَرَ، وَالعَلَاقَةُ فِي ذَلِكَ دَائِرَةٌ بَيْنَ الإِطْلَاقِ وَالتَّقْيِيدِ ، ثُمَّ نُقِلَ إِلَى العَقْلِ مَجَازاً مُرْسَلاً مِنْ إِطْلَاقِ اسْمِ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى آلَتِهِ، وَإِنْ شِئْتَ قُلْتَ : مِنْ إِطْلَاقِ اسْمِ المُسَبَّبِ عَلَى سَبَبِهِ . وَتَقْرِيرُ الِاحْتِمَالَيْنِ الْأَخِيرَيْنِ وَاضِحٌ مِمَّا مَرَّ. وَبَعْدَ هَذَا قَدْ صَارَتِ القَرِيحَةُ حَقِيقَةٌ عُرْفِيَّةٌ فِي العَقْلِ ؛ لِهَجْرِ المَعْنَى الأَصْلِيُّ الأَوَّلِ وَالثَّانِي ؛ بِحَيْثُ إِذَا أُطْلِقَتِ انْصَرَفَتْ إِلَى العَقْلِ، لَا إِلَى المَعْنَى الأَصْلِيِّ الأَوَّلِ أَوِ الثَّانِي، حَتَّى إِذَا أُرِيدَ أَحَدُهُمَا كَانَ بِطَرِيقِ المَجَازِ العُرْفِيّ ، فَلَا بُدَّ عَلَيْهِ مِنْ قَرِينَةٍ، فَلْيُفهم . (ثم الأنبابي) قوله : ( بِتَنْزِيلِهِ مَنْزِلَةَ المَعْنَى الحَقِيقِي ... إلخ) يحتاج لهذا في المجاز المرسل أيضاً. قوله : (إِلَى أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنَ العِلْمِ ... إلخ) أي: بأن يتجوز بـ «القريحة» إلى أوَّل مستنبط مطلقاً، ثمَّ إن استعملت في أوَّل مستنبط مِنَ العلم ؛ لكونه فرداً من أفراد ذلك المطلق، كان مجازاً بمرتبة واحدة، وإن نقلت ثانياً [ب/ ٢٠] من مطلق أوّل مستنبط إلى أوَّل مستنبط مِنَ العلم كان مجازاً بمرتبتين [ج/ ١٤]. هذا ؛ إن كان النَّقل مِن أَوَّلِ لأوَّل، فإن كان مِن أوَّلِ لِمَا يستنبط من العِلم مطلقاً، كان بثلاث مراتب؛ بأن يتجوز بها إلى أوّل مستنبط مطلقاً، ثمَّ إلى أوَّل مستنبط مِنَ العِلم، ثمَّ إلى مطلق مستنبط مِنَ العِلم . اهـ (صبَّان (۱) ، وهذا مبني على ما اشتهر وإلا فهو من بناء المجاز على المجاز، وإنَّما بيان كونه بمرتبة أو بمرتبتين فأكثر، يعلم مما ذكرناه فيما سبق.
His saying: (Its permissibility... etc.) It is known that permissibility (jawaz) is: the state of a thing such that the two relations of action and omission to it are equal. And in this sense it does not suit his saying: 'to be guided by it to correctness'; because it requires that the relation of action to it is more preponderant. Therefore some said, its meaning: that he intended by 'permissibility' its being legally permitted, and in that case it is possible for obligation and recommendation, and it is known that its place is when there is no dispensation from it, as previously. His saying: (for the one of complete qariha) i.e., for a person of complete qariha. And know that qariha originally means: 'the first thing extracted from water', it was transferred to the first thing extracted from knowledge (1), or to the extracted thing absolutely; i.e., even if it is not first, either by metaphor (isti'ara) or by conventional figurative language (majaz mursal), then it was transferred to the intellect either by metaphor or by conventional figurative language. From this it is derived that it is probable: 1 - That the two figurative transfers are by metaphor. 2 - And that they are both by conventional figurative language. 3 - And that the first is by metaphor and the second by conventional figurative language. 4 - And that the first is by conventional figurative language and the second by metaphor. (Then the commentary of al-Anbabi:) His saying: (mustanbat) i.e., extracted, so qariha means 'al-maqruha', i.e., the extracted thing. His saying: (then it was transferred to the intellect either by metaphor... etc.) It could be said: it is correct that there is one figurative transfer: by transferring qariha from the first extracted from water to the intellect, due to the relation of similarity in benefiting from each. His saying: (either by metaphor) i.e., derivative metaphor; because 'qariha' on the pattern of 'fa'ila' means 'maf'ula', so it is from 'qarh' (extraction). Yes, if it is considered that it runs the course of solid nouns, it is original, and likewise is said in conventional figurative language. (Then al-Shirshimi:) His saying: (it was transferred to the first extracted from knowledge) This is apparent on the probability of metaphor, but on the probability of conventional figurative language it is not; because the thing transferred to is either the first extracted, knowledge or other, or absolute extracted, knowledge or other, or not... except if it is said: That he considered the final result of its usage in the specific instance, or its transfer to it as taken from the end of his expression, and likewise is said in his later saying: 'to the first extracted... etc.', so the expression is not repeated in the manifest manner; reflect. (Then page 147) Section: On the permissibility of occupying oneself with it 147 (17) And the famous correct statement: Its permissibility is for the one who possesses qariha. Al-Bajuri: And the exposition of the first probability is that it is said: He likened the first extracted from knowledge, or the extracted from it absolutely, to the first extracted from water; with the commonality: life in each, even though life in the likened is for the spirit and in the thing likened to is for the body, and the word of the thing likened to, which is the word 'qariha', was borrowed for the likened. Then he likened the intellect to the meaning transferred to, by making it take the place of the real meaning in relation to the meaning figuratively transferred to afterwards; with the commonality: benefit and guidance by each, and the word of the thing likened to, which is the word 'qariha', was borrowed for the likened. And the exposition of the second probability is that it is said: The word 'qariha' was transferred from the first extracted from water to the first extracted from knowledge, or to the extracted from it absolutely, by conventional figurative language, either by one degree or more, and the relation in that revolves between absolute and restricted. Then it was transferred to the intellect by conventional figurative language from applying the name of the thing to its instrument, or if you wish, from applying the name of the effect to its cause. And the exposition of the last two probabilities is clear from what preceded. And after this, 'qariha' became a customary reality (haqiqa urfiyya) in the intellect; due to abandonment of the first and second original meanings; such that when it is used absolutely, it is understood as the intellect, not the first or second original meaning, such that if one of them is intended, it is by customary figurative language, so a contextual indicator is necessary. So understand. (Then al-Anbabi:) His saying: (by making it take the place of the real meaning... etc.) This is also needed in conventional figurative language. His saying: (to the first extracted from knowledge... etc.) i.e., by figuratively using 'qariha' for absolute first extracted, then if it is used for the first extracted from knowledge, because it is an individual of that absolute, it is a figurative transfer of one degree; and if transferred a second time from absolute first extracted to the first extracted from knowledge, it is a figurative transfer of two degrees. This, if the transfer is from first to first. But if it is from first to what is extracted from knowledge absolutely, it is of three degrees: by figuratively using it for absolute first extracted, then for first extracted from knowledge, then for absolute extracted from knowledge. End. (Sabban (1)) And this is based on what is well-known, otherwise it is building figurative language upon figurative language. And the clarification of it being of one degree or two degrees or more is known from what we mentioned previously.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a statement about the permissibility of studying logic, focusing on two key terms: 'jawaz' (permissibility) and 'qariha' (acumen/intellect). The commentator begins by clarifying that 'jawaz' in this context does not mean the logical notion of equal probability of action and omission, but rather legal permissibility, which includes obligation and recommendation. This is because the purpose of studying logic is to be guided to correctness, which implies a preponderance of action over omission. Then he turns to 'qariha', explaining its origin: it originally meant 'the first extraction from water' (like a wellspring). It was first transferred to mean 'the first extraction from knowledge' or 'extracted knowledge absolutely', either by metaphorical borrowing (isti'ara) or by conventional figurative language (majaz mursal). Then it was transferred again to mean 'the intellect', again by either type of figurative language. This yields four possible combinations for the two transfers. The commentator then details each probability with examples. For the first combination (both by metaphor), he explains that the first extraction from knowledge is likened to the first extraction from water due to the commonality of 'life' (spiritual vs. bodily), and then the intellect is likened to that transferred meaning due to the commonality of benefit and guidance. For the second combination (both by conventional figurative language), the relation is between absolute and restricted ('itlaq' and 'taqyid') and then from thing to its instrument or effect to cause. The last two combinations are left as clear from the preceding. Finally, he notes that through frequent use, 'qariha' became a customary reality (haqiqa urfiyya) for the intellect, abandoning its original meanings, so that when used absolutely it refers to the intellect, and if an original meaning is intended, a contextual indicator is required. The subsequent comments by al-Anbabi further refine the number of degrees of figurative transfer, discussing whether the transfer is one, two, or three steps, depending on whether the intermediate step between water and intellect is absolute first extracted or specifically first extracted from knowledge.

Key Terms
الجواز (al-jawaz): In this passage, it means legal permissibility (ma'dhun fihi shar'an), not the logical notion of equal probability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the logical meaning of jawaz (equal probability) not suit the phrase 'to be guided by it to correctness'?
A) Because guidance implies a preponderance of action over omission.
B) Because guidance is impossible without certainty.
C) Because equality of action and omission is desirable.
D) Because the phrase is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
In this passage, jawaz is redefined from logical permissibility to legal permission, allowing the study of logic to be considered obligatory or recommended for those who possess the requisite acumen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a text on the permissibility of studying kalam, the author says 'jawaz al-nazar fi al-kalam'. Using the lesson's method, determine whether jawaz here likely means legal permission or logical equivalence. Explain your reasoning.

The Metaphorical Evolution of 'Qariha' from Water to Intellect
Analysis of qariha's semantic shift from water to knowledge to intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the metaphorical transfers of the term 'qariha' and identify the type of figurative language (isti'ara or majaz mursal) used in each transfer.

Core Idea
Qariha's journey from water extraction to intellect shows how language evolves through metaphor and convention.

The commentator explains that 'qariha' originally meant 'the first extraction from water'. It was then transferred first to knowledge (the first extraction from knowledge or extracted knowledge in general) and then to the intellect. Each transfer could be either by metaphorical borrowing (isti'ara) or by conventional figurative language (majaz mursal), leading to four possible combinations. The commentator also notes that through frequent usage, 'qariha' became a customary reality (haqiqa urfiyya) for the intellect, abandoning its original meanings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original meaning of qariha
Qariha originally meant 'first extraction from water' (like a wellspring).
This is the literal, concrete meaning.
2
Trace the first transfer to knowledge
The term was applied to 'first extraction from knowledge' or 'extracted knowledge' by similarity (isti'ara) or conventional metaphor (majaz mursal).
The commentator lists both possibilities.
3
Trace the second transfer to intellect
Then it was applied to the intellect, again by one of the two types of figurative language.
This yields four combinations in total.
4
Recognize the result as customary reality
After the transfers, qariha became a customary reality (haqiqa urfiyya) for the intellect, so that when used absolutely, it refers to the intellect.
Original meanings require a contextual indicator.

قَوْلُهُ : (جَوَازُهُ ... إلخ) مِنَ المَعْلُومِ أَنَّ الجَوَازَ : كَوْنُ الشَّيْء بِحَيْثُ يَسْتَوِي نِسْبَتَا الفِعْلِ وَالتَّرْكِ إِلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ بِهَذَا المَعْنَى لَا يُلَائِمُ قَوْلَهُ: «لِيَهْتَدِي بِهِ إِلَى الصَّوَابِ» ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الفِعْلِ إِلَيْهِ أَرْجَحُ ، وَلِذَلِكَ قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ مَا مَعْنَاهُ : أَنَّهُ أَرَادَ بِالجَوَازِ كَوْنَهُ مَأذُونَا فِيهِ شَرْعاً، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلوُجُوبِ وَالنَّدْبِ ، وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّ مَحَلَّهُ مَا لَمْ يُسْتَغْنَ عَنْهُ ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ . قَوْلُهُ : (لِكَامِلِ القَرِيحَهُ) أَيْ : لِشَخْصٍ كَامِلِ القَرِيحَةِ . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الفَرِيحَةَ فِي الأَصْلِ: «أَوَّلُ مُسْتَنْبَطَ مِنَ المَاءِ»، نُقِلَتْ إِلَى أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ العِلْم (۱) ، أَوْ إِلَى المُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنْهُ مُطْلَقاً ؛ أَي وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ أَوَّلاً إِمَّا بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ، أَوِ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ، ثُمَّ نُقِلَتْ لِلعَقْلِ إِمَّا بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ أَوِ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ . وَيُتَحَصَّلُ مِنْ هَذَا أَنَّهُ يُحْتَمَلُ : 1 - أَنْ يَكُونَ التَّجَوُّزَانِ المَذْكُورَانِ مِنَ الْمَجَازِ بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ . ٢ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَا مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ. ٣ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ المَجَازِ بِالاسْتِعَارَةِ، وَالثَّانِي مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ . ٤ - وَأَنْ يَكُونَ الأَوَّلُ مِنَ المَجَازِ المُرْسَلِ، وَالثَّانِي مِنَ المَجَازِ بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ. (ثم تعليق الأنبابي) قوله : (مُسْتَنبط) أي : مستخرج، فالقريحةُ بمعنى: «المقروحة»؛ أي: المستخرجة. قوله : (ثُمَّ نُقِلَتْ لِلعَقْلِ إِمَّا بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ ... إلخ) قد يقال : يصح أن يكون هناك مجاز واحد؛ بأن تنقل القريحة من أوَّل مستنبط من الماء للعقل ؛ لعلاقة المشابهة في الانتفاع بكل . قوله : (إِمَّا بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ) أي: التَّبعيَّة؛ لأنَّ «قريحة على وزن فعيلة» بمعنى: مفعولة، فهي من القرح. نعم؛ إن نظر لكونها جرت مجرى الجوامد كانت أصلية، وهكذا يقال في المجاز المرسل. (ثم الشرشيمي: قوله : (نُقِلَتْ إِلَى أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ العِلْمِ) هذا ظاهر على احتمال الاستعارة، أما على احتمال المجاز المرسل فلا ؛ لأنَّ المنقول إليه أوّلاً إِمَّا أوّل مستنبط علماً أو غيره، أو إلى مطلق مستنبط علماً أو غيره، أو لا أو لا ؛ إلا أن يقال: إنَّه نظر لمآل الأمر باستعماله في الفرد الخاص، أو نقله إليه كما يؤخذ من آخر عبارته، وكذا يقال في قوله بعد: «إِلَى أَوَّلِ سْتَنْبَط ... إلخ»، فالعبارة غير مكررة عن الوجه الظاهر؛ تأمل. (ثم صفحة 147) فصل: في جواز الاشتغال به ١٤٧ (۱۷) وَالقَوْلَةُ المَشْهُورَةُ الصَّحِيحَة : جَوَازُهُ لِعَامِلِ القَرِيحَة الباجوري وَتَقْرِيرُ الِاحْتِمَالِ الأَوَّلِ أَنْ يُقَالَ : شَبَّهَ أَوَّلَ مُسْتَنْبَطَ مِنَ العِلْمِ، أَوِ المُسْتَنْبَطَ مِنْهُ مُطْلَقاً بِأَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ المَاءِ ؛ بِجَامِعِ : الحَيَاةِ فِي كُلِّ وَإِنْ كَانَتِ الحَيَاةُ فِي المُشَبَّهِ لِلرُّوحِ وَفِي المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ للجِسْمِ، وَاسْتُعِيرَ لَفْظُ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ وَهُوَ لَفْظُ القَرِيحَةِ لِلمُشَبَّهِ، ثُمَّ شَبَّهَ العَقْلَ بِالمَعْنَى المَنْقُولِ إِلَيْهِ بِتَنْزِيلِهِ مَنْزِلَةَ المَعْنَى الحَقِيقِيِّ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلمَعْنَى المُتَجَوَّزِ إِلَيْهِ بَعْدُ ؛ بِجَامِعِ : الانْتِفَاعِ وَالِاهْتِدَاءِ بِكُلِّ، وَاسْتُعِيرَ لَفْظُ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ وَهُوَ لَفْظُ «القَرِيحَةِ» لِلمُشَبَّهِ . وَتَقْرِيرُ الاِحْتِمَالِ الثَّانِي أَنْ يُقَالَ : نُقِلَ لَفْظُ الفَرِيحَةِ مِنْ أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ المَاءِ إِلَى أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَط مِنَ العِلْمِ، أَوْ إِلَى المُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنْهُ مُطْلَقاً مَجَازاً مُرْسَلاً إِمَّا بِمَرْتَبَةٍ أَوْ بِأَكْثَرَ، وَالعَلَاقَةُ فِي ذَلِكَ دَائِرَةٌ بَيْنَ الإِطْلَاقِ وَالتَّقْيِيدِ ، ثُمَّ نُقِلَ إِلَى العَقْلِ مَجَازاً مُرْسَلاً مِنْ إِطْلَاقِ اسْمِ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى آلَتِهِ، وَإِنْ شِئْتَ قُلْتَ : مِنْ إِطْلَاقِ اسْمِ المُسَبَّبِ عَلَى سَبَبِهِ . وَتَقْرِيرُ الِاحْتِمَالَيْنِ الْأَخِيرَيْنِ وَاضِحٌ مِمَّا مَرَّ. وَبَعْدَ هَذَا قَدْ صَارَتِ القَرِيحَةُ حَقِيقَةٌ عُرْفِيَّةٌ فِي العَقْلِ ؛ لِهَجْرِ المَعْنَى الأَصْلِيُّ الأَوَّلِ وَالثَّانِي ؛ بِحَيْثُ إِذَا أُطْلِقَتِ انْصَرَفَتْ إِلَى العَقْلِ، لَا إِلَى المَعْنَى الأَصْلِيِّ الأَوَّلِ أَوِ الثَّانِي، حَتَّى إِذَا أُرِيدَ أَحَدُهُمَا كَانَ بِطَرِيقِ المَجَازِ العُرْفِيّ ، فَلَا بُدَّ عَلَيْهِ مِنْ قَرِينَةٍ، فَلْيُفهم . (ثم الأنبابي) قوله : ( بِتَنْزِيلِهِ مَنْزِلَةَ المَعْنَى الحَقِيقِي ... إلخ) يحتاج لهذا في المجاز المرسل أيضاً. قوله : (إِلَى أَوَّلِ مُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنَ العِلْمِ ... إلخ) أي: بأن يتجوز بـ «القريحة» إلى أوَّل مستنبط مطلقاً، ثمَّ إن استعملت في أوَّل مستنبط مِنَ العلم ؛ لكونه فرداً من أفراد ذلك المطلق، كان مجازاً بمرتبة واحدة، وإن نقلت ثانياً [ب/ ٢٠] من مطلق أوّل مستنبط إلى أوَّل مستنبط مِنَ العلم كان مجازاً بمرتبتين [ج/ ١٤]. هذا ؛ إن كان النَّقل مِن أَوَّلِ لأوَّل، فإن كان مِن أوَّلِ لِمَا يستنبط من العِلم مطلقاً، كان بثلاث مراتب؛ بأن يتجوز بها إلى أوّل مستنبط مطلقاً، ثمَّ إلى أوَّل مستنبط مِنَ العِلم، ثمَّ إلى مطلق مستنبط مِنَ العِلم . اهـ (صبَّان (۱) ، وهذا مبني على ما اشتهر وإلا فهو من بناء المجاز على المجاز، وإنَّما بيان كونه بمرتبة أو بمرتبتين فأكثر، يعلم مما ذكرناه فيما سبق.
His saying: (Its permissibility... etc.) It is known that permissibility (jawaz) is: the state of a thing such that the two relations of action and omission to it are equal. And in this sense it does not suit his saying: 'to be guided by it to correctness'; because it requires that the relation of action to it is more preponderant. Therefore some said, its meaning: that he intended by 'permissibility' its being legally permitted, and in that case it is possible for obligation and recommendation, and it is known that its place is when there is no dispensation from it, as previously. His saying: (for the one of complete qariha) i.e., for a person of complete qariha. And know that qariha originally means: 'the first thing extracted from water', it was transferred to the first thing extracted from knowledge (1), or to the extracted thing absolutely; i.e., even if it is not first, either by metaphor (isti'ara) or by conventional figurative language (majaz mursal), then it was transferred to the intellect either by metaphor or by conventional figurative language. From this it is derived that it is probable: 1 - That the two figurative transfers are by metaphor. 2 - And that they are both by conventional figurative language. 3 - And that the first is by metaphor and the second by conventional figurative language. 4 - And that the first is by conventional figurative language and the second by metaphor. (Then the commentary of al-Anbabi:) His saying: (mustanbat) i.e., extracted, so qariha means 'al-maqruha', i.e., the extracted thing. His saying: (then it was transferred to the intellect either by metaphor... etc.) It could be said: it is correct that there is one figurative transfer: by transferring qariha from the first extracted from water to the intellect, due to the relation of similarity in benefiting from each. His saying: (either by metaphor) i.e., derivative metaphor; because 'qariha' on the pattern of 'fa'ila' means 'maf'ula', so it is from 'qarh' (extraction). Yes, if it is considered that it runs the course of solid nouns, it is original, and likewise is said in conventional figurative language. (Then al-Shirshimi:) His saying: (it was transferred to the first extracted from knowledge) This is apparent on the probability of metaphor, but on the probability of conventional figurative language it is not; because the thing transferred to is either the first extracted, knowledge or other, or absolute extracted, knowledge or other, or not... except if it is said: That he considered the final result of its usage in the specific instance, or its transfer to it as taken from the end of his expression, and likewise is said in his later saying: 'to the first extracted... etc.', so the expression is not repeated in the manifest manner; reflect. (Then page 147) Section: On the permissibility of occupying oneself with it 147 (17) And the famous correct statement: Its permissibility is for the one who possesses qariha. Al-Bajuri: And the exposition of the first probability is that it is said: He likened the first extracted from knowledge, or the extracted from it absolutely, to the first extracted from water; with the commonality: life in each, even though life in the likened is for the spirit and in the thing likened to is for the body, and the word of the thing likened to, which is the word 'qariha', was borrowed for the likened. Then he likened the intellect to the meaning transferred to, by making it take the place of the real meaning in relation to the meaning figuratively transferred to afterwards; with the commonality: benefit and guidance by each, and the word of the thing likened to, which is the word 'qariha', was borrowed for the likened. And the exposition of the second probability is that it is said: The word 'qariha' was transferred from the first extracted from water to the first extracted from knowledge, or to the extracted from it absolutely, by conventional figurative language, either by one degree or more, and the relation in that revolves between absolute and restricted. Then it was transferred to the intellect by conventional figurative language from applying the name of the thing to its instrument, or if you wish, from applying the name of the effect to its cause. And the exposition of the last two probabilities is clear from what preceded. And after this, 'qariha' became a customary reality (haqiqa urfiyya) in the intellect; due to abandonment of the first and second original meanings; such that when it is used absolutely, it is understood as the intellect, not the first or second original meaning, such that if one of them is intended, it is by customary figurative language, so a contextual indicator is necessary. So understand. (Then al-Anbabi:) His saying: (by making it take the place of the real meaning... etc.) This is also needed in conventional figurative language. His saying: (to the first extracted from knowledge... etc.) i.e., by figuratively using 'qariha' for absolute first extracted, then if it is used for the first extracted from knowledge, because it is an individual of that absolute, it is a figurative transfer of one degree; and if transferred a second time from absolute first extracted to the first extracted from knowledge, it is a figurative transfer of two degrees. This, if the transfer is from first to first. But if it is from first to what is extracted from knowledge absolutely, it is of three degrees: by figuratively using it for absolute first extracted, then for first extracted from knowledge, then for absolute extracted from knowledge. End. (Sabban (1)) And this is based on what is well-known, otherwise it is building figurative language upon figurative language. And the clarification of it being of one degree or two degrees or more is known from what we mentioned previously.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a statement about the permissibility of studying logic, focusing on two key terms: 'jawaz' (permissibility) and 'qariha' (acumen/intellect). The commentator begins by clarifying that 'jawaz' in this context does not mean the logical notion of equal probability of action and omission, but rather legal permissibility, which includes obligation and recommendation. This is because the purpose of studying logic is to be guided to correctness, which implies a preponderance of action over omission. Then he turns to 'qariha', explaining its origin: it originally meant 'the first extraction from water' (like a wellspring). It was first transferred to mean 'the first extraction from knowledge' or 'extracted knowledge absolutely', either by metaphorical borrowing (isti'ara) or by conventional figurative language (majaz mursal). Then it was transferred again to mean 'the intellect', again by either type of figurative language. This yields four possible combinations for the two transfers. The commentator then details each probability with examples. For the first combination (both by metaphor), he explains that the first extraction from knowledge is likened to the first extraction from water due to the commonality of 'life' (spiritual vs. bodily), and then the intellect is likened to that transferred meaning due to the commonality of benefit and guidance. For the second combination (both by conventional figurative language), the relation is between absolute and restricted ('itlaq' and 'taqyid') and then from thing to its instrument or effect to cause. The last two combinations are left as clear from the preceding. Finally, he notes that through frequent use, 'qariha' became a customary reality (haqiqa urfiyya) for the intellect, abandoning its original meanings, so that when used absolutely it refers to the intellect, and if an original meaning is intended, a contextual indicator is required. The subsequent comments by al-Anbabi further refine the number of degrees of figurative transfer, discussing whether the transfer is one, two, or three steps, depending on whether the intermediate step between water and intellect is absolute first extracted or specifically first extracted from knowledge.

Key Terms
القريحة (al-qariha): Originally 'first extraction from water', then transferred to 'first extraction from knowledge' or 'extracted knowledge', and ultimately to 'intellect' as a customary reality.
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): Metaphorical borrowing based on resemblance; here used for the transfer of meaning from water to knowledge and then to intellect.
المجاز المرسل (al-majaz al-mursal): Conventional figurative language based on a non-similarity relation (e.g., causality, part-whole); here the relations are 'itlaq/taqyid' (absolute/restricted) and 'ism al-shay' ala alatihi' (thing for its instrument).
الحقيقة العرفية (al-haqiqa al-urfiyya): A reality established by customary usage; 'qariha' becomes a customary reality for the intellect after the original meanings are abandoned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A customary reality (haqiqa urfiyya) for the intellect
C) A logical term for probability

Key Takeaway
Qariha evolved through layers of figurative language to become a customary term for intellect, illustrating how technical terms can shift in meaning through usage.

The Metaphorical Development of the Term al-Qariha
Bajuri analyzes the metaphorical transfer of 'al-qariha' from water to intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the metaphorical development of the term 'al-qariha' from its literal meaning to its technical meaning as a customary reality, and distinguish between the two types of figurative transfer (isti'ara and majaz mursal).

Core Idea
A term becomes a technical reality through a series of metaphorical transfers and abandonment of original meanings.

In this lesson, we examine how the term 'al-qariha' (innate ability) evolved from its literal meaning of 'first water drawn from a well' to become a standard term for the intellect itself. The author, Bajuri, presents two possible paths for this transformation: one through a chain of metaphors (isti'ara) and the other through metonymy (majaz mursal). Understanding this process is essential for grasping how technical terms in the Islamic sciences acquire their settled meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original literal meaning of 'al-qariha'
Find the first definition given in the passage: it originally meant 'the first water drawn from a well'. Note the source and the context.
This literal sense is the starting point for the entire development.
2
Follow the first possible transfer (isti'ara)
Read the section starting with 'The explanation of the first possibility'. Two stages: first, the first thing derived from knowledge is compared to the first water, with the commonality of 'life'. Then the intellect is compared to that metaphorical meaning, with the commonality of 'benefit and guidance'. Identify each step of the analogy.
This is a double metaphor: from water to first knowledge, then from first knowledge to intellect.
3
Follow the second possible transfer (majaz mursal)
Read the section on the second possibility. The term is transferred from water to knowledge via metonymy (relation of absolute to restricted), then from knowledge to intellect via the relation of instrument or cause-effect. Note the different number of steps (one or more).
Majaz mursal does not require similarity; it uses other relations.
4
Recognize the final stage: customary reality (haqiqa 'urfiyya)
The passage concludes that after these transfers, the original meanings were abandoned. Now 'al-qariha' when used absolutely refers only to the intellect. If the original sense is intended, it requires a contextual clue.
This is the key point: the term has become a technical reality in logic.

Chapter: On the permissibility of engaging in it. The well-known correct statement: Its permissibility is for one of sound innate ability. The explanation of the first possibility is to say: He compared the first thing derived from knowledge, or what is derived from it absolutely, to the first water drawn from a well; with the commonality of life in each, although life in the compared (the first derived) is for the spirit and in the thing compared to (the water) is for the body. And the word of the thing compared to, which is the word 'al-qariha', was used metaphorically for the compared. Then he compared the intellect to the meaning to which it was transferred, by treating it as if it were the real meaning in relation to the meaning to which it is metaphorically transferred later; with the commonality of benefit and guidance in each. And the word of the thing compared to, which is the word 'al-qariha', was used metaphorically for the compared. The explanation of the second possibility is to say: The word 'al-qariha' was transferred from the first water drawn from a well to the first thing derived from knowledge, or to what is derived from it absolutely, as a metonymy, either with one step or with more, and the relationship in that circles between the absolute and the restrictive. Then it was transferred to the intellect as a metonymy from using the name of a thing for its instrument, or if you wish, from using the name of the effect for its cause. The explanation of the last two possibilities is clear from what preceded. After this, 'al-qariha' became a customary reality for the intellect, due to the abandonment of the first and second original meanings, such that when it is used absolutely it directs to the intellect, not to the first or second original meaning. So if one of them is intended, it must be through customary metaphor, requiring a context. So understand. Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'by treating it as if it were the real meaning... etc.' also needs this in the case of metonymy. His statement: 'to the first thing derived from knowledge... etc.' means: that one uses 'al-qariha' metaphorically to mean the first derived thing absolutely. Then if it is used for the first derived from knowledge, because it is an individual of that absolute, it is a metaphor with one degree. If it was transferred a second time from the absolute first derived to the first derived from knowledge, it is a metaphor with two degrees. This is if the transfer is from first to first. If it was from first to what is derived from knowledge absolutely, it has three degrees: by using it metaphorically for the absolute first derived, then for the first derived from knowledge, then for the absolute derived from knowledge. End. (Sabban). And this is based on what is well-known, otherwise it is from building metaphor upon metaphor. And the explanation of its being with one degree or two or more is known from what we mentioned previously. (See: Sabban's gloss on al-Mallawi's al-Sharh al-Saghir, p. 175).
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a definitional-linguistic analysis of the term 'al-qariha' as it is used in the context of logic. The author, Bajuri, explains the metaphorical development of the term from its original meaning (the first water drawn from a well) to its technical meaning (innate intellectual ability). He presents two possibilities for this transfer. The first possibility is through a double metaphor (tashbih): first, comparing the first thing derived from knowledge to the first water drawn from a well, with the commonality of 'life' (though in different senses). The word 'al-qariha' is then used metaphorically for the first derived knowledge. Then, the intellect is compared to this metaphorical meaning, treating it as if it were the real meaning, and again 'al-qariha' is used metaphorically for the intellect. The commonality here is benefit and guidance. The second possibility is through metonymy (majaz mursal): the word is transferred from the first water to the first derived knowledge, and then to the intellect, using either the relation of instrument or cause-effect. After these transfers, the term became a customary reality (haqiqa 'urfiyya) for the intellect, meaning that when used absolutely it refers to the intellect, not the original meanings. Al-Anbabi's gloss further clarifies the degrees of metaphor involved, noting that the number of steps depends on the details of the transfer, and that the analysis is based on well-known principles of figurative language.

استعارة
مجاز مرسل
حقيقة عرفية

Understanding the Grammatical Debate Over 'Awwala'
Author discusses preferred case of 'awwala' and responds to objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two competing grammatical analyses of 'awwala' in the matn and explain why the objection to ishtighal arises based on conditional syntax.

Core Idea
Grammatical parsing of a single word can determine the logical structure of an argument.

In this passage from al-Bajuri's commentary, we encounter a grammatical controversy over the case ending of the word 'awwala' in the matn. The author prefers the accusative due to ishtighal, but an objection is raised based on the rules of conditional sentences. Understanding this debate is crucial for correctly interpreting the logical flow of the matn. Let's break down the grammatical principles at play and see how they affect textual meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposed grammatical cases for 'awwala'
The author states that the preferred (arjah) analysis is that 'awwala' is accusative (mansub) due to ishtighal. The objection implies an alternative: that it should be nominative (marfu') as the subject (ibtida') of a nominal sentence.
The objection argues that the accusative reading is invalid, so the only valid reading is nominative.
2
Understand the grammatical construction 'ishtighal'
Ishtighal is a construction where a verb governs a preceding noun by way of a pronoun that refers back to that noun. For example: 'Zaidan darabtuhu' (Zaid, I hit him) – 'Zaidan' is accusative because the verb 'darabtu' governs it via the pronoun 'hu'.
For ishtighal to occur, the verb must be able to govern the noun directly.
3
Grasp the objection's grammatical basis
The objection states: after the conditional particle 'in' and the response particle 'fa', the verb that follows cannot govern anything that precedes these particles. Since 'awwala' comes before the conditional structure, the verb in the response clause cannot govern it, so ishtighal is impossible.
In Arabic, the protasis (if-clause) and apodosis (result clause) have restrictions on what can be governed across them.
4
Analyze the author's response: 'tafsil' (detail)
Al-Bajuri responds that the justification for the accusative is 'al-tafsil', meaning that the structure here is not a true conditional but a detailed listing or explanation. The 'in' may be interpreted as a particle of explanation or the sentence is parsed differently, allowing the verb to govern 'awwala' via ishtighal.
This response shows that the author considers the grammatical context flexible enough to allow the accusative reading.
5
Connect grammatical parsing to logical interpretation
Consider how each reading affects the meaning: if 'awwala' is nominative, it is the subject of a nominal sentence, making the statement about 'awwala' a topic. If accusative, it is an object of a verb, implying a different syntactic relationship. This impacts how the entire argument of the matn is understood.
The logical content of the matn may depend on this grammatical choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا ; وَقَدْ ذَكَرَ الْمُصَنِّفُ : أَنَّ الأَرْجَحَ نَصْبُ أَوَّلاً عَلَى الاشْتِغَالِ . وَيُعْتَرَضُ فِيهِ : بِأَنَّ مَا بَعْدَ كُلِّ مِنْ أَدَاةِ الشَّرْطِ وَ(فَاءِ الجَوَابِ لَا يَعْمَلُ فِيمَا قَبْلَهُ، وَمَا لَا يَعْمَلُ لا يُفَسَّرُ عَامِلاً ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَجِبُ رَفْعُهُ عَلَى الابْتِدَاءِ، وَالمُسَوِّغُ التَّفْصِيلُ (۳)
This; and the author mentioned that the preferred (view) is that 'awwala' is accusative (mansub) due to ishtighal (occupation). And an objection is raised against it: that what comes after each of the conditional particle and the fa of the response does not govern what comes before it, and what does not govern cannot be interpreted as governing (in the construction of ishtighal). Then it would be necessary to make it nominative (marfu') as a subject (ibtida'), and the justification is the detail (tafsil).
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical note on the matn line. The author (al-Bajuri) states that the preferred grammatical analysis of 'awwala' is that it is accusative (mansub) due to ishtighal, a grammatical construction where a verb acts on a preceding noun via an intervening pronoun. However, an objection is raised: conditional sentences with 'in' (if) and the fa of response do not allow the verb after the fa to govern a noun before the conditional. Therefore, 'awwala' cannot be mansub from ishtighal and must be marfu' (nominative) as the subject of a nominal sentence. The response is that the justification for the accusative is the 'detail' (tafsil), meaning that the structure is not a true conditional but a detailed listing (perhaps the 'in' is not a conditional but a particle of explanation? Or the sentence is parsed differently). The passage highlights the grammatical sophistication in interpreting the matn.

فاء الجواب (fa' al-jawab)

Key Takeaway

The Grammatical Objection to 'Awwala' and its Resolution
Author addresses grammatical objections to the case of 'awwala'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grammatical objection raised against the word 'awwala' in the Sullam line and explain the two answers given by the commentators.

Core Idea
Grammatical rules are sometimes bent to serve logical terminology.

This lesson focuses on a specific grammatical dispute: how can the word 'awwala' (firstly) act as an accusative adverbial when it follows a conditional particle? The logicians' line seems to violate Arabic grammar. We will follow how al-Bajuri and al-Anbabi defend the text.

Guided Path
1
Locate the objection
Find the sentence 'وَيُحِثَ فِيهِ' and identify the precise grammatical issue: the conditional particle 'in' and the answer particle 'fa' prevent what follows from governing what precedes. So 'awwala' cannot be in the accusative as an object of an implied verb.
The objection assumes standard Arabic syntax rules for conditional sentences.
2
Understand the first answer
Al-Bajuri offers an answer: the conditional particle is considered postponed in estimation, the 'fa' is extra, and the original word order is different. This allows 'awwala' to govern what precedes. Note that al-Bajuri himself admits this is artificial (تكلف).
The original would be 'وَأَوَّلاً لِلذَّاتِ انْسِبُهُ إِنِ انْدَرَجَ'.
3
Consider the alternative approach
Al-Anbabi suggests that the entire phrase can be treated as 'absolute interpretation' (مطلق التفسير), meaning the word is simply being explained without grammatical connection. This avoids the objection entirely.
This is less artificial but relies on a different reading of the line.
4
Evaluate the strength of each answer
Compare the two approaches: the first is grammatically elaborate, the second is pragmatic. Which one better preserves the intended meaning of the Sullam line?

وَيُحِثَ فِيهِ : بِأَنَّ مَا بَعْدَ كُلِّ مِنْ أَدَاةِ الشَّرْطِ وَ(فَاءِ الجَوَابِ لَا يَعْمَلُ فِيمَا قَبْلَهُ، وَمَا لَا يَعْمَلُ لا يُفَسَّرُ عَامِلاً ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَجِبُ رَفْعُهُ عَلَى الابْتِدَاءِ، وَالمُسَوِّغُ التَّفْصِيلُ. الأنبابي: قوله : (فَالنَّوْعُ ذَاتِيُّ) وعلى هذا يكون منسوباً للذات بمعنى: الماصدقات، لا الماهية، أو هو تسمية اصطلاحيَّةٌ على صورة النِّسبة، فلا يقال : يلزمُ نسبة الشَّيء لنفسه، أو هو منسوب للماهية لقصد المبالغة. اهـ صبان. قوله : (وَيُحِثَ فِيهِ ... إلخ) لك جعله من باب مطلق التفسير، فلا إشكال. (١) الشرشيمي : قوله : (فَالنَّوْعُ ذَاتِيٌّ) إن قلت : إنَّ الذَّات المنسوب إليها هو الماهيَّة، والمنسوب هو الماهية، فيلزم اتحاد المنسوب والمنسوب إليه . قلت أراد بـ «الذات» المنسوب إليها : الأفراد ؛ لأنَّ الذَّات تُطلق على الماصدق [۲۸/۱] والأفراد، كما تُطلق على الماهية ، وتكون نسبتها لغويَّةً، أو يُقال: لا مانع من نسبة الشَّيء إلى نفسه مبالغة. هذا ؛ وأما نسبة «الحَيَوان» إلى الماهية فهي نسبة الجزء إلى الكلِّ، وكذا نسبة «ناطق» إليها . (۲) انظر : مجموع السُّلَّم المرونق» (ص : ٤١٨). (۳) الشرشيمي : قوله : (التَّفْصِيلُ ) أي : كونه مفصلاً إلى : ذاتي، وعَرَضي . (٤) انظر : حاشية الصبان على الشرح الصغير للملوي (ص: ٢٥٨). • 1 .  ١٩٦ جابة الامم الناجون على السلم المرونق ٣٠) وَأَوَّلاً لِلذَّاتِ إِنْ فِيهَا انْدَرَجُ فَانْسِبْهُ، أَوْ لِعَارِضِ إِذَا خَرَجْ الباجوري: وَأُجِيبَ بِأَنَّ أَدَاةَ الشَّرْطِ مُؤَخَّرَةٌ عَنِ العَامِلِ تَقْدِيراً ، وَالفَاءُ : زَائِدَةٌ، وَالأَصْلُ: «وَأَوَّلاً لِلذَّاتِ انْسِبُهُ إِنِ انْدَرَجَ فِيهَا وَجَوَابُ الشَّرْطِ مَحْذُوفٌ لِدَلَالَةِ الفِعْلِ المَذْكُورِ عَلَيْهِ، وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِي هَذَا مِنَ التَّكَلُّفِ. قَوْلُهُ : (لِلذَّاتِ) أَيْ : لِلمَاهِيَّةِ كَمَا هُوَ أَحَدُ إِطْلَاقَيْهَا ، وَثَانِيَهُمَا: إِطْلَاقُهَا عَلَى المَاصَدَقِ . قَوْلُهُ : (إِنْ فِيهَا انْدَرَج) أَيْ : بِأَنْ كَانَ جُزْءاً مِنْهَا ، وَهُوَ الجِنْسُ وَالفَصْلُ كَمَا مَرَّ . قَوْلُهُ : (فَانْسِبْهُ) أَيْ: بِأَنْ تَقُولَ: «ذَاتِيُّ ، كَمَا هُوَ الشَّائِعُ عِنْدَ المَنَاطِقَةِ . وَيُحِثَ فِيهِ : بِأَنَّ مُقْتَضَى قَوَاعِدِ النَّسَبِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «ذَوَوِيُّ» ؛ لِأَنَّ أَصْلَ المَنْسُوبِ إِلَيْهِ ذَوَو» (٢) ، وَالنَّسَبُ يَرِدُّ الأَشْيَاءَ إِلَى أُصُولِهَا . وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ لَيْسَ نَسَباً حَقِيقَةٌ، بَلْ تَسْمِيةٌ اصْطِلَاحِيَّةٌ عَلَى صُورَةِ النَّسَبِ، وَعَلَى تَسْلِيمِ أَنَّهُ نَسَبٌ حَقِيقَةٌ، فَهُوَ عَلَى غَيْرِ قِيَاسِ . أَيْضاً . قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ لِعَارِضِ) أَيْ : أَوِ انْسِبُهُ لِعَارِضِ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ : عَرَضَيٌّ ، كَمَا هُوَ الشَّائِعُ عِنْدَ المَنَاطِقَةِ . وَيُحِثَ فِيهِ أَيْضاً : بِأَنَّهُ كَانَ مُقْتَضَى الظَّاهِرِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «عَارِضِيُّ». وَيُجَابُ بِمَا تَقَدَّمَ آنفاً . وَالمُرَادُ بِـ العَارِضِ»: المَنْسُوبُ إِلَيْهِ الأَمْرُ الَّذِي يَعْرِضُ لِلشَّيْء كَ: «الضَّحِكِ»، وَبِـ«العَرَضِي : المَنْسُوبُ نَحْوُ : «الصَّاحِكِ ، فَالعَارِضُ غَيْرُ العَرَضِيِّ، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى. الأنبابي: قوله : (وَبِـه العَرَضِيّ : المَنْسُوبُ ... إلخ) فالنسبةُ مِن نسبة اللازم إلى الملزوم . اهـ (صبَّان (٤)
And there is an objection in it: that what comes after each of the conditional particle and the particle of the answer does not operate on what precedes it, and what does not operate cannot serve as an interpreted operator; therefore it must be in the nominative as a subject, and the licencing factor is the detail. Al-Anbabi: His statement: (so the species is essential) means it is relative to the essence in the sense of individuals, not quiddity; or it is a terminological designation in the form of a relative adjective, so one does not say: it implies the relation of a thing to itself; or it is relative to the quiddity for emphasis. End quote from al-Sabban. His statement: (and there is an objection in it ... etc.) for you, it is from the category of absolute interpretation, so there is no problem. Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (the species is essential) If you say: the essence to which it is relative is the quiddity, and that which is relative is also the quiddity, so the relative and the relative to would be identical. I say: He intended by 'the essence' to which it is relative: the individuals; because 'essence' is applied to the referents and individuals as it is applied to the quiddity, and its relation is linguistic; or it is said: there is no impediment to relating a thing to itself for emphasis. This; and as for the relation of 'animal' to the quiddity, it is the relation of part to whole, and similarly the relation of 'rational' to it. See: Majmu' al-Sullam al-Munawraq (p. 418). Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (the detail) i.e., it being detailed into essential and accidental. See: Hashiyat al-Sabban 'ala al-Sharh al-Saghir lil-Malawi (p. 258). [Page 196] Answer of the successful imams on al-Sullam al-Munawraq. (30) And 'firstly' for the essence if it is included therein, then ascribe it to it, or to an accident if it exits. Al-Bajuri: And it was answered that the conditional particle is postponed from the operator in estimation, and the 'fa' is extra, and the original is: 'And firstly for the essence, ascribe it to it if it is included in it,' and the answer of the condition is omitted, indicated by the mentioned verb. And it is not hidden what is in this of artificiality. His statement: (for the essence) i.e., for the quiddity, as it is one of its two usages; the second of them is its application to the referent. His statement: (if it is included in it) i.e., in that it is a part of it, and it is the genus and differentia, as passed. His statement: (then ascribe it) i.e., by saying 'essential,' as is common among logicians. And there is an objection in it: that the rule of attribution requires saying 'dhawawi' because the root of that to which attribution is made is 'dhawaw'; and attribution returns things to their origins. And it was answered: that is not a genuine attribution, but rather a terminological designation in the form of attribution; and even if it is conceded that it is a genuine attribution, it is irregular. Also. His statement: (or to an accident) i.e., or ascribe it to an accident by saying 'accidental,' as is common among logicians. And there is an objection in it also: that the apparent requirement would be to say 'aaridiyy.' And it is answered by what preceded just now. And what is meant by 'the accident' is that to which the thing that befalls the thing is attributed, like 'laughter'; and by 'the accidental' is the attributed, like 'laughing.' So the accident is other than the accidental, as is not hidden. Al-Anbabi: His statement: (and by it the accidental is the attributed ... etc.) So the attribution is of the necessary to the necessary. End quote from Sabban.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical commentary in the hashiya style, addressing grammatical and terminological issues arising from the Sullam line. The commentary begins with an objection raised about the word 'awwala' (firstly): in Arabic grammar, what follows a conditional particle and its answer particle cannot grammatically govern what precedes it. Therefore, 'awwala' should be in the nominative as a subject, not accusative as an object of an implied verb. The licencing factor for this is that it is 'detailed' (i.e., the classification into essential and accidental allows the nominative). Al-Anbabi then discusses the statement 'the species is essential'. The concern is that if 'essence' (dhat) means quiddity, and species is the entire quiddity, then we would be relating a thing to itself (the species to the essence). To avoid this, some argue that 'essence' here refers to individuals (the referents) not the quiddity, so the relation is linguistic (part-to-whole?). Others say it's a terminological designation not a real relation, or it's for emphasis. Al-Shirshimi's note elaborates: if you object that relating the species to essence is self-relation, the answer is that 'essence' here means individuals, so it's fine. Then the commentary returns to the grammatical issue on the next page. The answer given is that the conditional particle is considered postponed in estimation, the 'fa' is extra, and the original word order is different. This solution is acknowledged as artificial. Then the commentary explains each part of the line: 'for the essence' means quiddity; 'if it is included' means it is a part, i.e., genus or differentia; 'then ascribe it' means say 'dhati' (essential). Then a second objection is raised: according to the rules of Arabic nisba (attribution), from 'dhat' (essence) one should derive 'dhawawi', not 'dhati'. The answer is that 'dhati' is not a genuine grammatical derivation but a terminological convention used by logicians. Similarly, for 'aradi' (accidental), the expected form would be 'aaridiyy', but 'aradi' is used as a conventional term. Finally, the distinction between 'al-araḍ' (the accident) and 'al-araḍi' (the accidental) is clarified: the accident is the attribute like laughter, and the accidental is the thing described by it like 'laughing'. So the relationship is that of the necessary to the necessary (the attribute is necessary for the description, not vice versa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, what is the main grammatical objection to the word 'awwala' in the Sullam line?
A) It is not an Arabic word
B) It follows a conditional particle and its answer, so it cannot govern what precedes
C) It is in the accusative case, which is not allowed in Arabic
D) It is a proper noun that cannot be declined

Key Takeaway
The grammatical objection to 'awwala' is resolved either by a reordering of the sentence (al-Bajuri) or by treating the phrase as absolute interpretation (al-Anbabi).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the phrase 'al-insanu hayawanun natiq' and examine if there are any grammatical objections to its formation. If a conditional particle were inserted, how would that affect the case endings? Use the same method as al-Bajuri to propose a resolution.
?
In the commentary tradition, find another instance where a logical term deviates from standard Arabic grammar (e.g., 'mawjud' from 'wujud'). Practice identifying the objection and the answer given by logicians.

Commentary Notes
intermediate
6 min
The Terminological Derivation of 'Dhati' and 'Aradi'
Author addresses objections to the derivation of 'dhati' and 'aradi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians use 'dhati' and 'aradi' instead of the grammatically expected forms 'dhawawi' and 'aaridiyy'.

Core Idea
Logical terminology trumps regular Arabic morphology.

This lesson focuses on the second objection in the passage: why does the nisba from 'dhat' produce 'dhati' instead of the regular 'dhawawi'? The same applies to 'aradi' instead of 'aaridiyy'. We will see how the commentators justify these terms as conventional designations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expected nisba form
From the noun 'dhat' (essence), the regular Arabic rule for forming relative adjectives would produce 'dhawawi', because the root is 'dhawaw'. Note the phrase 'النَّسَبُ يَرِدُّ الأَشْيَاءَ إِلَى أُصُولِهَا' (attribution returns things to their origins).
The expected form is based on the triliteral root.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says that the rule of nisba would require 'dhawawi', not 'dhati'. Similarly, for 'arad' (accident), the expected nisba would be 'aaridiyy', not 'aradi'. This seems to violate Arabic morphology.
3
Examine the answer
The answer is that 'dhati' and 'aradi' are not genuine nisba forms; they are terminological designations (تسمية اصطلاحية) that merely resemble nisba. Even if considered genuine, they are irregular (على غير قياس).
The logicians' usage is a convention, not a mistake.
4
Apply the same reasoning to 'aradi'
The objection against 'aradi' is identical to that against 'dhati'. The answer is the same. Notice the symmetry in the commentary: 'وَيُحِثَ فِيهِ أَيْضاً' and 'وَيُجَابُ بِمَا تَقَدَّمَ آنفاً'.

And there is an objection in it: that what comes after each of the conditional particle and the particle of the answer does not operate on what precedes it, and what does not operate cannot serve as an interpreted operator; therefore it must be in the nominative as a subject, and the licencing factor is the detail. Al-Anbabi: His statement: (so the species is essential) means it is relative to the essence in the sense of individuals, not quiddity; or it is a terminological designation in the form of a relative adjective, so one does not say: it implies the relation of a thing to itself; or it is relative to the quiddity for emphasis. End quote from al-Sabban. His statement: (and there is an objection in it ... etc.) for you, it is from the category of absolute interpretation, so there is no problem. Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (the species is essential) If you say: the essence to which it is relative is the quiddity, and that which is relative is also the quiddity, so the relative and the relative to would be identical. I say: He intended by 'the essence' to which it is relative: the individuals; because 'essence' is applied to the referents and individuals as it is applied to the quiddity, and its relation is linguistic; or it is said: there is no impediment to relating a thing to itself for emphasis. This; and as for the relation of 'animal' to the quiddity, it is the relation of part to whole, and similarly the relation of 'rational' to it. See: Majmu' al-Sullam al-Munawraq (p. 418). Al-Shirshimi: His statement: (the detail) i.e., it being detailed into essential and accidental. See: Hashiyat al-Sabban 'ala al-Sharh al-Saghir lil-Malawi (p. 258). [Page 196] Answer of the successful imams on al-Sullam al-Munawraq. (30) And 'firstly' for the essence if it is included therein, then ascribe it to it, or to an accident if it exits. Al-Bajuri: And it was answered that the conditional particle is postponed from the operator in estimation, and the 'fa' is extra, and the original is: 'And firstly for the essence, ascribe it to it if it is included in it,' and the answer of the condition is omitted, indicated by the mentioned verb. And it is not hidden what is in this of artificiality. His statement: (for the essence) i.e., for the quiddity, as it is one of its two usages; the second of them is its application to the referent. His statement: (if it is included in it) i.e., in that it is a part of it, and it is the genus and differentia, as passed. His statement: (then ascribe it) i.e., by saying 'essential,' as is common among logicians. And there is an objection in it: that the rule of attribution requires saying 'dhawawi' because the root of that to which attribution is made is 'dhawaw'; and attribution returns things to their origins. And it was answered: that is not a genuine attribution, but rather a terminological designation in the form of attribution; and even if it is conceded that it is a genuine attribution, it is irregular. Also. His statement: (or to an accident) i.e., or ascribe it to an accident by saying 'accidental,' as is common among logicians. And there is an objection in it also: that the apparent requirement would be to say 'aaridiyy.' And it is answered by what preceded just now. And what is meant by 'the accident' is that to which the thing that befalls the thing is attributed, like 'laughter'; and by 'the accidental' is the attributed, like 'laughing.' So the accident is other than the accidental, as is not hidden. Al-Anbabi: His statement: (and by it the accidental is the attributed ... etc.) So the attribution is of the necessary to the necessary. End quote from Sabban.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical commentary in the hashiya style, addressing grammatical and terminological issues arising from the Sullam line. The commentary begins with an objection raised about the word 'awwala' (firstly): in Arabic grammar, what follows a conditional particle and its answer particle cannot grammatically govern what precedes it. Therefore, 'awwala' should be in the nominative as a subject, not accusative as an object of an implied verb. The licencing factor for this is that it is 'detailed' (i.e., the classification into essential and accidental allows the nominative). Al-Anbabi then discusses the statement 'the species is essential'. The concern is that if 'essence' (dhat) means quiddity, and species is the entire quiddity, then we would be relating a thing to itself (the species to the essence). To avoid this, some argue that 'essence' here refers to individuals (the referents) not the quiddity, so the relation is linguistic (part-to-whole?). Others say it's a terminological designation not a real relation, or it's for emphasis. Al-Shirshimi's note elaborates: if you object that relating the species to essence is self-relation, the answer is that 'essence' here means individuals, so it's fine. Then the commentary returns to the grammatical issue on the next page. The answer given is that the conditional particle is considered postponed in estimation, the 'fa' is extra, and the original word order is different. This solution is acknowledged as artificial. Then the commentary explains each part of the line: 'for the essence' means quiddity; 'if it is included' means it is a part, i.e., genus or differentia; 'then ascribe it' means say 'dhati' (essential). Then a second objection is raised: according to the rules of Arabic nisba (attribution), from 'dhat' (essence) one should derive 'dhawawi', not 'dhati'. The answer is that 'dhati' is not a genuine grammatical derivation but a terminological convention used by logicians. Similarly, for 'aradi' (accidental), the expected form would be 'aaridiyy', but 'aradi' is used as a conventional term. Finally, the distinction between 'al-araḍ' (the accident) and 'al-araḍi' (the accidental) is clarified: the accident is the attribute like laughter, and the accidental is the thing described by it like 'laughing'. So the relationship is that of the necessary to the necessary (the attribute is necessary for the description, not vice versa).

Key Terms
النسبة
تسمية اصطلاحية
على غير قياس

The Ambiguity of 'Dhat': Resolving the Self-Relation Problem
Author addresses the self-relation objection regarding the term 'dhati'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two usages of 'dhat' (quiddity vs. individuals) and use this distinction to resolve the self-relation objection in defining 'dhati'.

Core Idea
The word 'dhat' is ambiguous between quiddity and individuals; this solves the paradox of the species relating to itself.

When logicians say 'the species is dhati' (essential), they are relating the species to the essence. But if 'essence' means the quiddity, and the species is the whole quiddity, then the species would be related to itself. The commentators resolve this by showing that 'dhat' can also mean the individuals of the species.

Guided Path
1
State the self-relation problem
If 'dhat' (essence) means quiddity, and the species is the entire quiddity (jins + fasl), then saying 'the species is dhati' would be relating a thing to itself, which is problematic.
2
Identify the two usages of 'dhat'
Al-Bajuri explains that 'dhat' has two usages: (1) quiddity (al-mahiyya), and (2) referents or individuals (al-masadiq, al-afrad). This is key to the solution.
The same word can refer to the concept or its instances.
3
Apply the usage to avoid self-relation
If we take 'dhat' in the sense of individuals, then 'dhati' means 'related to the individuals of the species'. Since the individuals are not identical to the species quiddity, there is no self-relation. Al-Shirshimi confirms this.
4
Consider alternative solutions
Other solutions mentioned: (a) it is a terminological designation, so the relation is not real; (b) it is a case of emphasis (mubalagha) where relating a thing to itself is permissible for rhetorical effect.
These are less common but show the flexibility of the commentary tradition.
5
Contrast with other relations
Al-Shirshimi notes that the relation of 'animal' (genus) to the quiddity is part-to-whole, not self-relation. Similarly for 'rational'. This illustrates a genuine relation that does not pose the same problem.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical commentary in the hashiya style, addressing grammatical and terminological issues arising from the Sullam line. The licencing factor for this is that it is 'detailed' (i.e., the classification into essential and accidental allows the nominative). Al-Anbabi then discusses the statement 'the species is essential'. Al-Shirshimi's note elaborates: if you object that relating the species to essence is self-relation, the answer is that 'essence' here means individuals, so it's fine. Indefinite Propositions and the Analogy for Contradiction
Al-Anbabi questions whether the disagreement on indefinite propositions applies here, with al-Sharshimi's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disagreement about indefinite propositions (muhmala) in contradiction and evaluate whether the same disagreement applies to conditional propositions by analogy.

Core Idea
Indefinite propositions have a contested contradictory – the same issue resurfaces in conditional logic.

In this marginal note, al-Anbabi raises a subtle but important question: does the known disagreement about the contradictory of indefinite categorical propositions also apply when we move to conditional propositions? Al-Sharshimi's brief response confirms the analogy. Understanding this exchange sharpens your ability to trace how logical rules carry over from one domain to another.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Read al-Anbabi's statement: he asks whether the disagreement (khilāf) about indefinite propositions applies 'here' (i.e., in the discussion of conditional contradictories) by analogy with what preceded (categorical contradictories).
The 'disagreement' refers to differing views on whether the contradictory of an indefinite proposition is universal or particular.
2
Locate what is being extended
Al-Sharshimi clarifies that the phrase 'from what he mentioned' refers to the author's earlier rule for universal affirmative conditionals. The same rule applies later, implying the analogy holds.
The author (al-Bajuri) had previously given the rule: the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, etc. Now, for indefinite conditionals, the same pattern is used.
3
Understand the logical basis of the analogy
In categorical logic, an indefinite proposition (e.g., 'Man is an animal') is often treated as equivalent to a particular proposition, but there is controversy about its contradictory: some say it is a universal negative, others a particular negative. The same controversy is being projected onto conditional propositions.
The analogy is based on the structural similarity between categorical and conditional propositions regarding quantifiers.
4
Assess the commentator's response
Al-Sharshimi's answer ('likewise it is said later') indicates that the author's rule is sufficient and the same disagreements are indeed inherited. This shows the consistency of the logical system.
This is a typical pattern in Islamic logical commentaries: a later commentator resolves an ambiguity by pointing to the generality of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأنبابي: قوله : (وَفِي المُهْمَلَةِ) انظر هل الخلاف جارٍ في ذلك بين المصنف وغيره؛ قياساً على ما تقدَّم؟ (۱) الشرشيمي: قوله : ( مِمَّا ذَكَرَهُ) وهو قوله : «فَإِنْ تَكُنْ مُوجَبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةَ نَقِيضُهَا ... إلخ»، وكذا يقال فيما بعد.
al-Anbabi: His statement: 'And in the indefinite' – consider: does the disagreement apply here between the author and others, by analogy to what preceded? (1) al-Sharshimi: His statement: 'from what he mentioned' – and it is his statement: 'So if it is a universal affirmative its contradictory is ... etc.', and likewise it is said later.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This marginal note by al-Anbabi raises a question about the treatment of indefinite (muhmala) propositions in the context of contradiction. He asks whether the disagreement (khilāf) that exists regarding the contradictory of indefinite categorical propositions also applies here to conditional propositions, by analogy with what preceded. Al-Sharshimi responds by clarifying that the phrase 'from what he mentioned' refers to the author's earlier statement about universal affirmative, and the same applies later. In essence, the note highlights a possible confusion: the matn deals with quantified (maḥṣūra) propositions, but the commentator (Bajuri) extends the discussion to indefinite propositions. Al-Anbabi wonders if the same disagreement about the quantity of the contradictory of indefinite propositions (whether it is universal or particular) should be considered here. Al-Sharshimi's answer implies that the author's rule is sufficient and the analogy holds.

Key Terms
مهملة (muhmala)
خلاف (khilāf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Anbabi ask about the disagreement on indefinite propositions here?
A) Because the matn explicitly discusses indefinite propositions.
B) Because Bajuri extended the rule to indefinite conditionals, raising the same issue by analogy.
C) Because indefinite propositions are not subject to contradiction.
D) Because al-Anbabi disagrees with Bajuri's entire approach.

Key Takeaway
Disagreements about logical rules for categorical propositions often carry over to conditional propositions by analogy, requiring careful attention to how quantifiers and indefinite terms are treated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the conditional proposition: 'If a thing is eternal, it is not temporally originated.' Write its contradictory using the rule for universal affirmative conditionals. Then ask: does the same disagreement about indefinite quantifiers affect this contradictory? Explain using the analogy from this lesson.
?
Find a passage in al-Bajuri's commentary on contradiction (e.g., the chapter on 'tanāquḍ') where he uses an indefinite conditional. Identify which view of muhmala is implicitly being assumed (e.g., treating it as particular). Check if that assumption is consistent with the disagreement al-Anbabi mentions.
