Defining the Zodiac Constellation (Buruj) – Genus and Differentia
Commentator enumerates and defines the zodiac constellations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia of the term 'buruj' in its astronomical context and distinguish it from its Quranic interpretations.

Core Idea
The twelve zodiac signs are the genus 'parts of the fixed-star sphere' with the specific differentia of being twelve in number and order.

In this passage, the commentator defines the term 'abruj' by listing the twelve constellations and explaining that they are the divisions of the sphere of fixed stars. This is a classic example of a definition by enumeration, where the genus is 'parts of the sphere' and the differentia is the specific twelve signs. Understanding how definitions are built helps us in both astronomy and tafsir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The word 'abruj' is the plural of 'burj'. The commentator first gives a linguistic note that although it is a plural of paucity, here it indicates abundance because there are twelve.
2
Extract the genus
The commentator says the constellations are 'parts of the circle of the fixed-star sphere'. So the broader category (genus) is 'ajza' al-falak' (parts of a celestial sphere).
3
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes these twelve parts from any other parts? Their specific enumeration and names, which the commentator lists and even provides a mnemonic poem for.
4
Compare with other definitions
The commentator then mentions Quranic interpretations where 'buruj' could mean palaces, great stars, or planets. Each interpretation has a different genus and differentia, showing how context changes definition.
This step trains you to see that shifting the genus changes the entire meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَوْلُهُ : (أَبْرُجَا) جَمْعُ : بُرْج) ، وَهُوَ وَإِنْ كَانَ جَمْعَ قِلَّةٍ ، لَكِنَّ المُرَادَ مِنْهُ الكَثْرَةَ؛ لِأَنَّهَا اثْنَا عَشَرَ : الحَمْلُ، وَالتَّوْرُ ، وَالجَوْزَاءُ، وَالسَّرَطَانُ، وَالأَسَدُ ، وَالسُّنْبُلَةُ ، وَالمِيزَانُ، وَالعَقْرَبُ، وَالقَوْسُ، وَالجَدِي ، وَالدَّلْو - وَيُقَالُ لَهُ : الدَّانِيُّ - ، وَالحُوتُ. وَقَدْ أَشَارَ لِذَلِكَ بَعْضُهُمْ بِقَوْلِهِ : حَمَلَ النَّوْرُ جَوْزَةَ السَّرَطَانِ وَرَعَى اللَّيْتُ سُنْبُلَ المِيزَانِ وَرَمَى عَفْرَبٌ بِقَوْسٍ لِجَدِي نَزَحَ الدَّلْوَ بِرْكَةَ الحِيتَانِ. وَهَذِهِ الأَبْرُجُ هِيَ أَجْزَاءُ دَائِرَةِ الفَلَكِ النَّامِنِ؛ الَّذِي هُوَ فَلَكُ الثَّوَابِتِ وَهُوَ المُسَمَّى بـ «الكُرْسِيِّ». وَتَوْضِيحُ ذَلِكَ : أَنَّ الحُكَمَاءَ فَسَّمُوا الفَلَكَ المَذْكُورَ اثْنَيْ عَشَرَ قِسْماً ، وَهِيَ الأَبْرُجُ المَذْكُورَةُ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ الشَّمْسَ لَا تُفَارِقُ مُسَامَتَةَ هَذِهِ الدَّائِرَةِ أَصْلاً مَعَ كَوْنِهَا فِي فَلَكِهَا الَّذِي هُوَ السَّمَاءُ الرَّابِعَةُ، فَإِذَا فَارَقَتْ بُرْجاً مِنْ تِلْكَ الأَبْرَاجِ، وَابْتَدَأَتْ فِي مُسَامَتَةِ مَا يَلِيهِ يُقَالُ: "قَطَعَتْ بُرْجَ كَذَا، وَحَلَّتْ فِي بُرْجِ كَذَا" وَهَكَذَا. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ أَنَّهَا تَقْطَعُ ذَلِكَ بِسَيْرِهَا الذَّاتِي، وَهُوَ سَيْرُهَا إِلَى جِهَةِ المَشْرِقِ، لَا بِسَيْرِهَا العَرَضِيِّ وَهُوَ سَيْرُهَا إِلَى جِهَةِ المَغْرِبِ، وَهُوَ الظَّاهِرُ لَنَا. وَوَجْهُ تَسْمِيَتِهِ بِذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ عَرَضٌ لَهَا مِنْ حَرَكَةِ الفَلَكِ الأَعْظَمِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَتَحَرَّكُ بِحَرَكَتِهِ جَمِيعُ مَا احْتَوَى عَلَيْهِ مِنَ الأَفْلَاكِ وَمَا فِيهَا مِنَ الكَوَاكِبِ. هَذَا؛ وَقَدِ اخْتُلِفَ فِي المُرَادِ بـ «البُرُوجِ» فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَلَقَدْ جَعَلْنَا فِي السَّمَاءِ بُرُوجًا وَزَيَّنَّاهَا لِلنَّاظِرِينَ﴾ [الحجر: ١٦] عَلَى أَقْوَالٍ؛ أَحْسَنُهَا مَا قَالَهُ عَطِيَّةُ مِنْ : أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِهَا قُصُورٌ فِي السَّمَاءِ. وَقَالَ مُجَاهِدٌ : المُرَادُ بِهَا النُّجُومُ العِظَامُ. وَقَالَ أَبُو إِسْحَاقَ النُّجُومُ السَّبْعَةُ الَّتِي هِيَ الكَوَاكِبُ السَّيَّارَةُ، وَهِيَ : زُحَلُ وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ السَّابِعَةِ، وَالمُشْتَرِي وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ السَّادِسَةِ، وَالمَرِّيخُ وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ الخَامِسَةِ، وَالشَّمْسُ وَهِيَ فِي السَّمَاءِ الرَّابِعَةِ، وَالزُّهْرَةُ وَهِيَ فِي السَّمَاءِ الثَّالِثَةِ، وَعُطَارِدُ وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ الثَّانِيَةِ، وَالقَمَرُ وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ الأُولَى، وَقَدْ جَمَعَهَا بَعْضُهُمْ فِي قَوْلِهِ : زُحَلٌ شَرَى مَرِّيخَهُ مِنْ شَمْسِهِ فَتَزَاهَرَتْ لِعَطَارِدَ الأَنْمَارُ. وَهِيَ عَلَى هَذَا التَّرْتِيبِ كَمَا عَلِمْتَ.
His saying: 'abruja' is the plural of burj. Even though it is a plural of paucity, the intended meaning is abundance because they are twelve: Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius, Capricorn, Aquarius - and it is called al-Dani - and Pisces. Some have alluded to that by his saying: 'Aries carried the nut (Gemini) of Cancer, and the lion (Leo) grazed the ear of grain (Virgo) of Libra, and a scorpion (Scorpio) shot with a bow (Sagittarius) to Capricorn, drawing water (Aquarius) from the pond of the fish (Pisces).' And these constellations are the parts of the circle of the fixed-star sphere, which is the sphere of the fixed stars and is called al-Kursi. The clarification of that is: The philosophers divided the aforementioned sphere into twelve parts, and they are the mentioned constellations. Then the sun never leaves its alignment with this circle at all, while being in its own sphere which is the fourth heaven. So when it departs from a constellation and begins to align with what follows it, it is said: 'It has cut through such-and-such constellation and settled in such-and-such constellation', and so on. And know that what is meant is that it cuts through that by its essential motion, which is its motion towards the east, not by its accidental motion which is its motion towards the west, which is apparent to us. The reason for calling it that is that it is an accident for it from the motion of the greatest sphere; because by its motion everything that it contains of spheres and what is in them of stars moves. This; and there is disagreement about what is meant by 'buruj' in the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And We have placed in the sky constellations and adorned it for the beholders' [Al-Hijr: 16] according to several opinions; the best of them is what 'Atiyyah said: that what is meant by them are palaces in the sky. And Mujahid said: what is meant by them are the great stars. And Abu Ishaq said: the seven stars which are the planets, and they are: Saturn which is in the seventh heaven, Jupiter which is in the sixth heaven, Mars which is in the fifth heaven, the sun which is in the fourth heaven, Venus which is in the third heaven, Mercury which is in the second heaven, and the moon which is in the first heaven. And some of them have collected them in his saying: 'Saturn bought his Mars from his sun, so the flowers of Mercury became bright.' And they are in this order as you know.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the word 'abruja' (constellations) from the matn line. The commentator first gives the plural form and clarifies that although 'abruj' is a plural of paucity in Arabic, here it denotes abundance because the constellations are twelve. He then lists the twelve zodiac signs in their traditional order: Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius, Capricorn, Aquarius, and Pisces. He notes that Aquarius is also called al-Dani. To aid memorization, he cites a poetic verse that encodes the names of the constellations in a mnemonic: 'Aries carried the nut (Gemini) of Cancer, and Leo grazed the ear of grain (Virgo) of Libra, and a scorpion shot with a bow to Capricorn, drawing water from the pond of the fish.' This verse uses wordplay to reference each sign. Next, he explains that these constellations are the twelve divisions of the sphere of fixed stars, called al-Kursi. The sun, though in its own sphere (the fourth heaven), never deviates from alignment with this zodiacal circle. As the sun moves eastward through its own motion (essential motion), it is said to 'cut through' each constellation. The apparent daily westward motion is accidental, caused by the rotation of the greatest sphere. The commentator then shifts to a tafsir (Quranic exegesis) discussion of the word 'buruj' in Surah al-Hijr, verse 16. He reports three opinions: 'Atiyyah says they are palaces in heaven; Mujahid says they are the great stars; Abu Ishaq says they are the seven planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Sun, Venus, Mercury, Moon), each in its respective heaven. He then cites another poem listing these planets in order. This passage serves to define and illustrate the astronomical terms used in the matn, linking them to Quranic interpretation.

Key Terms
البروج
الفلك الثابت
الميزان

Key Takeaway
A definition by enumeration, like the zodiac signs, provides a complete description (rasm) by listing all members under a common genus.

Essential vs. Accidental Motion: The Sun's Path Explained
Commentator explains the sun's essential and accidental motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential (dhati) motion from accidental (aradi) motion using the example of the sun's daily and annual paths.

Core Idea
The sun's eastward motion through the zodiac is essential; its apparent westward motion is accidental — a key distinction rooted in the movement of the greatest sphere.

The commentator explains that when we say the sun 'cuts through' a constellation, we mean by its essential motion eastward, whereas the daily rising and setting is accidental, caused by the rotation of the outermost sphere. This distinction helps us understand how definitions of motion apply in celestial mechanics and in theological arguments about the temporality of the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of motion
The commentator explicitly distinguishes between essential motion (al-sayr al-dhati) eastward and accidental motion (al-sayr al-'aradi) westward.
2
Understand the cause of accidental motion
The accidental westward motion is not from the sun itself but is an accident caused by the rotation of the greatest sphere (al-falak al-a'zam), which carries all spheres and stars with it.
3
Apply the distinction to other celestial bodies
The same principle holds for all planets and stars: their daily rising and setting is accidental, while their own proper motion (eastward through the zodiac) is essential.
This is a universal principle in Ptolemaic astronomy.
4
Note the terminological point
The term 'aradi' (accidental) in this context is used in a scientific sense, meaning 'derived from another's motion', not in the logical sense of 'non-essential property'. This shows how technical terms shift between disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَوْلُهُ : (أَبْرُجَا) جَمْعُ : بُرْج) ، وَهُوَ وَإِنْ كَانَ جَمْعَ قِلَّةٍ ، لَكِنَّ المُرَادَ مِنْهُ الكَثْرَةَ؛ لِأَنَّهَا اثْنَا عَشَرَ : الحَمْلُ، وَالتَّوْرُ ، وَالجَوْزَاءُ، وَالسَّرَطَانُ، وَالأَسَدُ ، وَالسُّنْبُلَةُ ، وَالمِيزَانُ، وَالعَقْرَبُ، وَالقَوْسُ، وَالجَدِي ، وَالدَّلْو - وَيُقَالُ لَهُ : الدَّانِيُّ - ، وَالحُوتُ. وَقَدْ أَشَارَ لِذَلِكَ بَعْضُهُمْ بِقَوْلِهِ : حَمَلَ Interpreting al-Bajuri's Colophon: Planetary Mnemonic and Completion Date
Author concludes his hashiya with a mnemonic for planetary order and records completion date.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the planetary order in the Ptolemaic model as presented in al-Bajuri's colophon and explain the author's clarification of the term 'badr'.

Core Idea
A colophon records completion and embeds mnemonics for essential scientific knowledge.

This lesson examines the concluding section of al-Bajuri's hashiya, which includes a mnemonic for planetary order and the date of completion. While not overtly logical, it illustrates how scholars embedded key knowledge in verse. We will unpack the mnemonic and understand al-Bajuri's lexical clarifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of a colophon
Recognize that a colophon often includes the completion date, a prayer, and sometimes mnemonic verses to aid memory of important facts.
Here, the colophon of al-Bajuri's hashiya contains a mnemonic for the order of the seven planets in the Ptolemaic heavens.
2
Extract the planetary order
From the Arabic text, list the planets from highest to lowest: Saturn (7th heaven), Jupiter (6th), Mars (5th), Sun (4th), Venus (3rd), Mercury (2nd), Moon (1st). This is the ancient order used in Islamic astronomy.
The sun is treated as a planet in this geocentric model.
3
Analyze the mnemonic verse
The verse 'Zuhalun shara mirrikhahu min shamsihi fatazaharat li'ataridi al-anmaru' is a poetic device to remember the order. Break it down: Saturn (Zuhal), Mars (Mirrikh), Sun (Shams), Mercury ('Utarid). The words encode the sequence.
The verse is a mnemonic; understanding it helps recall the order without memorizing a list.
4
Examine the author's clarification on 'badr'
Al-Bajuri explains that 'al-badr' (full moon) means the moon on any night of its completion, not necessarily the 14th night, and that 'al-munir' (luminous) is an inseparable attribute because an eclipsed moon is not called 'badr'.
This shows the author's concern for precise terminology, a key trait in Islamic scholarly writing.
5
Note the completion date and closing formula
The hashiya was completed in early Jumada al-Ula 1226 AH (c. 1811 CE). The colophon concludes with standard prayers and thanks.
Such dates help scholars trace the transmission and historical context of the work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة
مَا قَطَعَتْ شَمْسُ النَّهَارِ أَبْرُجَا وَطَلَعَ البَدْرُ المُنِيرُ فِي الدُّجَى
الباجوري
السَّابِعَةِ، وَالمُشْتَرِي وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ السَّادِسَةِ، وَالمَرِّيحُ وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ الخَامِسَةِ، وَالشَّمْسُ وَهِيَ فِي السَّمَاءِ الرَّابِعَةِ، وَالزُّهْرَةُ وَهِيَ فِي السَّمَاءِ الثَّالِثَةِ، وَعُطَارِدُ وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ الثَّانِيَةِ، وَالقَمَرُ وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ الأُولَى، وَقَدْ جَمَعَهَا بَعْضُهُمْ فِي قَوْلِهِ :
زُحَلٌ شَرَى مَرِّيخَهُ مِنْ شَمْسِهِ فَتَزَاهَرَتْ لِعَطَارِدَ الأَنْمَارُ وَهِيَ عَلَى هَذَا التَّرْتِيبِ كَمَا عَلِمْتَ.
قَوْلُهُ : (وَطَلَعَ البَدْرُ) أَي : القَمَرُ لَيْلَةَ تَمَامِهِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ فِي لَيْلَةِ أَرْبَعَ عَشْرَةَ، وَقَوْلُهُمْ: «البَدْرُ هُوَ القَمَرُ لَيْلَةَ أَرْبَعَ عَشْرَةَ تَقْرِيبِيُّ .
وَقَوْلُهُ : (المُنِيْرُ) صِفَةٌ لَازِمَةٌ ، إِذِ البَدْرُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا مُنِيراً ؛ لأَنَّ المَحْسُوفَ لَا يُسَمَّى بَدْراً. قَوْلُهُ: (فِي الدُّجَى جَمْعُ: «دجيةٍ - بِضَمِّ الدَّالٌ وَسُكُونِ الجِيْمِ ، وَهِيَ : الظُّلْمَةُ؛ كَمَا فِي القَامُوسِ». وَهَذَا آخِرُ مَا يَسَّرَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى عَلَى هَذَا المَتْنِ النَّفِيسِ النَّافِعِ لِكُلِّ مَنْ أَرَادَ المُطَالَعَةَ أَوِ التَّدْرِيسَ، وَكُنْ يَا أَخِي غَيْرَ مُقَاصِرٍ ، لِمَنْ هُوَ فِي العُلُومِ قَاصِرٌ ، وَالْتَمِسُ لَهُ عُذْراً عَمَّا وَقَعَ مِنْهُ مِنَ الهَفَوَاتِ، فَإِنَّ الحَسَنَاتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّاتِ، وَالحَمْدُ للهِ عَلَى كُلِّ حَالٍ، وَنَشْكُرُهُ عَلَى حُسْنِ الكَمَالِ، وَالصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ عَلَى سَيِّدِنَا مُحَمَّدٍ ذِي المَجْدِ وَالإِفْضَالِ، وَعَلَى أَصْحَابِهِ وَآلِهِ خَيْرِ آلِ . وَكَانَ الفَرَاعُ مِنْ جَمِيعِ هَذِهِ الحَاشِيَةِ النَّفِيسَةِ فِي أَوَائِلِ جُمَادَى الأُوْلَى مِنْ شُهُورِ سَنَةَ ١٢٢٦ مِنَ الهِجْرَةِ المُنِيفَةِ، عَلَى صَاحِبِهَا صَلَوَاتٌ وَتَحِيَّاتٌ شَرِيفَةٌ ، وَلَا حَوْلَ وَلَا قُوَّةَ إِلَّا بِاللَّهِ العَلِيِّ العَظِيمِ، وَهُوَ حَسْبِي وَنِعْمَ الوَكِيلُ، وَصَلَّى اللهُ عَلَى سَيِّدِنَا مُحَمَّدٍ وَعَلَى آلِهِ وَصَحْبِهِ وَسَلَّمَ تَسْلِيماً كثيراً.
Conclusion. 'The sun of daytime cut through the towers, and the luminous full moon rose in the darkness.' Al-Bajuri. ... (Saturn) in the seventh heaven, Jupiter in the sixth, Mars in the fifth, the Sun in the fourth, Venus in the third, Mercury in the second, and the Moon in the first. Some have compiled them in his saying: 'Saturn bought its Mars from its Sun, so the stars shone for Mercury.' They are in this order as you know. His saying: 'And the full moon rose' means the moon on the night of its completion, even if it is not on the night of the fourteenth. Their saying 'the full moon is the moon on the night of the fourteenth' is approximate. His saying: 'the luminous' is an intrinsic attribute, for the full moon cannot be non-luminous, because the eclipsed is not called a full moon. His saying: 'in the darkness' is the plural of 'dujya' with dammah on the dal and sukun on the jim, meaning darkness, as in the Qamus. This is the last of what Allah facilitated on this precious beneficial matn for anyone who wants to study or teach. Be, O my brother, not deficient to one who is deficient in sciences. Seek an excuse for him for what occurred of slips, for good deeds erase bad deeds. Praise be to Allah in every state, and we thank Him for the perfection of completion. Prayers and peace be upon our master Muhammad of majesty and favor, and upon his companions and family, the best of families. And the completion of all this precious hashiya was in the beginning of Jumada al-Ula of the year 1226 of the noble Hijra. Upon him (the Prophet) be blessings and noble greetings. There is no power and no strength except with Allah, the High, the Magnificent. He is sufficient for me and an excellent trustee. May Allah bless our master Muhammad and his family and companions and grant them abundant peace.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion (khatima) of al-Bajuri's hashiya. It begins with a line of poetry likely from the matn, then the author provides a mnemonic for the order of the planets in the Ptolemaic heavens, from the highest (Saturn) to the lowest (Moon). He then comments on the phrase 'the luminous full moon rose in the darkness,' explaining that 'al-badr' (full moon) refers to the moon at its completion, not necessarily the night of the 14th, and that 'al-munir' (luminous) is an essential attribute because an eclipsed moon is not called badr. 'Al-duja' is defined as darkness. Finally, the author records that he completed this hashiya in early Jumada al-Ula 1226 AH (approximately 1811 CE). He includes a prayer for forgiveness and praises, and a standard closing formula.

Key Terms
البدر
المنير
الدجى

C) The luminous moon always

Key Takeaway
Al-Bajuri's colophon uses a mnemonic for planetary order and clarifies that 'badr' means the full moon at its completion, not necessarily the 14th night.

Refuting al-Hilali's Objections on the Basmalah Interpretation
Author refutes al-Hilali's objections to the chosen meaning of the basmalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Hilali's two objections to the author's interpretation of the basmalah and identify the author's refutations, including the correct identification of the accompanying relata and the temporal precedence of seeking help.

Core Idea
Accompaniment is between person and name, not utterance; seeking help is the attempt to gain ability and precedes the action.

This lesson examines a critical exchange in the commentary on the basmalah. The author defends his interpretation that 'bism' conveys either accompaniment (musahaba) with the noble name when intending an action, or seeking help (isti'ana) through it. Al-Hilali raises two logical objections: one concerning the timing of accompaniment, and another concerning the meaning of the 'ba' of isti'ana. By following the author's refutations, you will learn how to detect misidentifications of relata and misunderstandings of linguistic conventions.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Hilali's first objection
Find the part where al-Hilali argues that accompaniment is a relation between two things (the composition and the noble name), so if the composition is delayed after pronouncing the basmalah, the accompaniment is also delayed. Note the relata he assumes.
He thinks the two accompanying things are the utterance of the basmalah and the name of Allah.
2
Identify the author's response to the first objection
The author corrects al-Hilali by stating that the two accompanying things are the person and the noble name, not the composition. Since the person and the name exist before the utterance, the accompaniment is not delayed. Understand why this resolves the objection.
The person intends the action and has the name in mind before speaking.
3
Identify al-Hilali's second objection
Al-Hilali argues that the 'ba' of isti'ana indicates the instrument of the action, and seeking help through an instrument only occurs during the action, not before. Therefore, saying 'bism' before the action cannot be a request for help.
He distinguishes between the meaning of the ba' and a separate request for help (like 'asta'inu billah').
4
Identify the author's response to the second objection
The author asserts that linguistic isti'ana (seeking help) is identical to the signification of the 'ba'. Requesting help is an attempt to gain ability over the action, which necessarily precedes the action, just as preparing a pen precedes writing. Thus the basmalah can express isti'ana before the act.
The example 'katabtu bil-qalam' involves preparation of the pen before writing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا دخل على كلام قيد بقيد ما ، يتوجه إلى ذلك القيد، ... وكذا الإثبات، فما من كلام فيه أمرٌ زائد على مجرد إثبات شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه إلَّا وهو الغرضُ الأصلي والمقصود مِنَ الكلام (۱) . الله . فكيف لا يكون الأمر كذلك مع التصرُّف فيها بالنقل عن معناها الأصلي، فالمقصود بجملة «بسم الاستعانة بالاسم الشَّريف على ما يُراد مِنَ الفعل، أو المصاحبة له عند إرادة الشروع فيه، والمقصود الأصلي قبل النقل صار بعده تبعاً . وزعم بعض أفاضل العصر ونبهائه - يعني : الهلالي - أنَّ هذا الوجه المختار لا يصح : - أمَّا أَوَّلاً : فلأنَّ المصاحبة نسبة بين متصاحبين، فتأخُرهُما ، أو تأخر أحدهما ملزوم لتأخرها، والمتصاحبات هنا : التأليف والاسم الشَّريف، فإذا تأخر التأليف ونحوه عن النُّطق بالبسملة تأخَّرت مصاحبة الاسم؛ ضرورةً توقف النسبة على وجود المنتسبين . - وأما ثانياً : فإنَّ «باء» الاستعانة ـ كما قرر في محله الداخلة على آلة الفعل الحقيقيَّة ؛ هي نحو: «كَتَبْتُ بِالقَلَمِ»، أو المجازية نحو : فَعَلْتُ هَذَا بِعَوْنِ اللهِ تعالى، والاستعانة بالآلة إنَّما تكون حالة الفعل لا قبله، وتأخر الفعل مستلزم تأخرها ؛ كما في قولك: «سَأَكْتُبُ بِالقَلَمِ»، فجعل الجملة لإنشاء الاستعانة المفهومة مِنَ الباء) غلط نشأ من توهم أنَّها بمعنى: «طلب العون»؛ كما في: «أَسْتَعِينُ بِاللهِ»، وليس ذلك معناها، وإنما معناها إيقاع الفعل بواسطة مدخولها . اهـ. وهذا الزعم غير صواب؛ لأنَّ : - مبنى الإيراد في المصاحبة : توهم أخذها من حيث هي نسبة بين متصاحبين، وهما : التأليف والاسم الشَّريف، وليس كذلك، بل المتصاحبان: الشَّخصُ والاسمُ الشَّريف، ولا خفاء في تقدُّمهما على التأليف على جهة الاستعداد له؛ حيث كان المراد المصاحبة عند إرادة الفعل والعزم عليه . - وهو ومبنى الإيراد في الاستعانة توهم المغايرة بين الاستعانة بالمعنى اللُّغوي - و تو: «طلب العون - وبين مدلول «الباء»، وذلك ممَّا لا دليل عليه . فالصَّوابُ أنَّهما ،متحدان وطلب العون محاولة الاقتدار على الفعل، فهي سابقة عليه بالضَّرورة؛ كما في: «كَتَبْتُ بِالقَلَمِ»، فإنَّ تهيئته وتناوله على الوجه الخاص محاولة للاقتدار به على الكتابة، ولا خفاء في تقدمه عليها .
If a restriction enters a statement with some restriction, it directs to that restriction... and likewise affirmation. So there is no statement in which there is an extra matter beyond mere affirmation of something for something or negation of it from it except that it is the primary purpose and intended meaning of the statement (1). Allah. Then how could it not be so with the alteration in them by transfer from their original meaning? So the intended meaning of the phrase 'Bism' is seeking help through the noble name for what is intended of the action, or accompaniment with it when intending to commence it, and the original meaning before transfer became subsequent to it. And some of the eminent scholars of the age and its intelligent ones—meaning al-Hilali—claimed that this chosen view is not valid: First, because accompaniment is a relation between two accompanying things, so their delay or the delay of one of them entails its delay. The accompanying things here are the composition and the noble name. So if the composition and the like is delayed after pronouncing the basmalah, the accompaniment of the name is delayed of necessity because the relation depends on the existence of the two relata. Second, because the 'ba' of seeking help—as established in its proper place—enters upon the actual instrument of the action, like 'I wrote with the pen,' or metaphorical, like 'I did this with the help of Allah Most High,' and seeking help through the instrument only occurs during the action, not before it. The delay of the action necessitates its delay, as in your saying: 'I will write with the pen.' So making the sentence for initiating the seeking help understood from the 'ba' is a mistake arising from the delusion that it means 'requesting help' as in 'I seek help from Allah,' but that is not its meaning; rather, its meaning is performing the action by means of its object. End. And this claim is not correct, because: The basis of the objection regarding accompaniment is the delusion that it is taken as a relation between two accompanying things, namely the composition and the noble name. It is not so; rather, the two accompanying things are the person and the noble name. There is no hiddenness in their precedence over the composition with respect to preparedness for it, since what is meant is accompaniment when intending the action and resolving upon it. And the basis of the objection regarding seeking help is the delusion of a difference between seeking help in the linguistic sense—namely 'requesting help'—and the signification of the 'ba,' and there is no evidence for that. So the correct view is that they are identical, and requesting help is an attempt to gain ability over the action, so it precedes it necessarily, as in 'I wrote with the pen,' for its preparation and handling in a specific manner is an attempt to gain ability through it for writing, and there is no hiddenness in its precedence over it.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation. The author first restates his view on the basmalah: the phrase 'bism' is used to convey either the meaning of seeking help (isti'ana) through the noble name for the intended action, or accompaniment (musahaba) when intending to begin. He then presents al-Hilali's two objections. First, al-Hilali argues that accompaniment is a relation between two things, here the composition (i.e., the utterance of the basmalah) and the noble name. Since the composition occurs after pronouncing the basmalah, the accompaniment would be delayed. The author responds by clarifying that the two accompanying things are the person and the noble name, not the composition; the person and the name precede the composition as a preparedness. Second, al-Hilali argues that the 'ba' of isti'ana indicates the instrument of the act, and seeking help through an instrument occurs only during the act, not before; thus the basmalah before the act cannot express isti'ana. The author responds by equating the linguistic meaning of isti'ana (requesting help) with the signification of the 'ba', and argues that requesting help is an attempt to gain ability over the action, which necessarily precedes the action, just as taking up the pen and handling it in a specific manner precedes writing. Thus both objections are refuted.

Key Terms
مصاحبة
استعانة
باء الاستعانة

B) The person and the noble name
C) The action and the noble name
D) The utterance and the intent

Key Takeaway
Al-Hilali's objections are invalid because he misidentifies the relata in accompaniment and falsely separates the meaning of isti'ana from the function of the 'ba'.

The Rhetorical Choice of 'Al-Hamd' and Nominal vs. Verbal Praise
Reasons for beginning with 'al-hamd' and nominal vs. verbal sentences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Islamic scholars began praise compositions with 'al-hamd' and distinguish between nominal and verbal sentences in praise based on the permanence or renewal of the praised attribute.

Core Idea
The choice between nominal and verbal praise sentences is a rhetorical decision based on the permanence or renewal of the praised attribute.

This passage from the commentary on al-Bannani's poem explains why the matn opens with 'al-hamd' rather than another expression. The author gives three reasons, then delves into a balagha principle: when praising Allah, should one use a nominal sentence (like 'al-hamdu lillah') or a verbal sentence (like 'nahmaduka Allahumma')? Al-Fanari's rule ties the choice to whether the praised attribute is permanent or occurring. This lesson will help you understand the criteria behind such rhetorical choices in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three reasons for starting with 'al-hamd'
The author lists: (1) Quranic precedent, (2) phonetic comprehensiveness, (3) context demands praise. Locate these in the Arabic.
2
Understand the phonetic justification
The word 'al-hamd' contains letters from all three main articulation points (throat, lips, tongue), ensuring no articulator is left out. This is a subtle reason for its choice.
3
Grasp al-Fanari's balagha rule
Al-Fanari says: if the praised attribute is permanent (like Lordship), use a nominal sentence; if it is renewing (like a specific action), use a verbal sentence. The verb indicates renewal.
This is a key principle in Arabic rhetoric for praise.
4
Analyze the objection from Quranic verses
The objection notes that Quranic verses like 18:1 use nominal sentences for actions (sending down the Book), which are renewing. This seems to contradict al-Fanari's rule.
Find these verses in the passage.
5
Learn the response: essence vs. action
The answer is that in those verses, praise is attached to the essence of Allah, not just the action. The nominal sentence emphasizes the permanence of His praiseworthiness.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أنّى بِالحَمْدِ إنَّما أتى بالحمد، مع أنَّ المتبادر من العبارة: أنَّ المحمود عليه هنا نعمة (أَتَى العلم، وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿لَين شَكَرْتُمْ لَأَزِيدَنَّكُمْ) [إبراهيم: ٧]؛ لأنَّ: - ديباجة القرآن المجيد موشّحة بغرَّة التّحميد . - ولاشتمال «الحمد)) على الحاء الحلقيَّة والميم الشَّفويَّة والدَّال اللسانية، حتَّى لا يخلو مخرج عن نصيبه من ذلك بالكلِّيَّة . - ولكون المقام مقتضياً للحمد ، مع إرادة التّأسي . لم يقدِّم لفظ الجلالة وإن كان أهم في ذاته؛ إذ ليست البلاغة إلَّا مطابقة الكلام لمقتضى الحال . - فإن قلت : أيُّ الجملتين أبلغ ؛ هل الاسمية أو الفعلية؟ قلت : قال العلامة الفناريُّ : إِنَّ الَّذي يقتضيه جانب البلاغة رعاية المحمود عليه : اسمية. فإن كانت صفة ثابتةٌ كصفة الربوبية المحمودِ عليها في سورة الفاتحة جيء بجملة «الحمد» - وإن كان المحمود عليه أمراً متجدّداً حادثاً جيء بجملة «الحمد» فعليَّةً؛ لأنَّ الفعل يفيد التجدد والحدوث؛ كما في قول صاحب جمع الجوامع»: نحمدك اللهم على نعمٍ يُؤذن الحمد بازدیادها . اهـ (۲) . ويشكل عليه : الإتيان بالاسميَّة في قوله تعالى : الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ الَّذِي أَنزَلَ عَلَى عَبْدِهِ الْكِتَبَ [الكهف: ١]، الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ الَّذِى هَدَننَا لِهَذَا [الأعراف : ٤٣] ، اَلْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ الَّذِى وَهَبَ لِي عَلَى الْكِبَرِ إِسْمَعِيلَ [إبراهيم: ٣٩]، فإنَّ المحمودَ عليه هنا أفعال حادثةٌ . وقد يُجاب : بأنّه قصد فيها تعلُّق الحمد بالذات، فإنَّه تعالى يستحقُ الحمد لذاته ولنعمه،
His saying: 'He brought praise.' He only brought praise, although what is apparent from the expression is that the praised thing here is the blessing of knowledge, and Allah Most High said: 'If you are grateful, I will surely increase you' [Ibrahim: 7]; because: - The opening of the Noble Quran is adorned with the beauty of praise. - And because 'al-hamd' includes the guttural ha', the labial mim, and the lingual dal, so that no point of articulation is devoid of its share entirely. - And because the context requires praise, intending imitation (of the Quran). He did not put the word of majesty (Allah) first, although it is more important in itself, because eloquence is nothing but adapting speech to the context. - If you say: Which of the two sentences is more eloquent: the nominal or the verbal? I say: al-Allama al-Fanari said: What eloquence requires is consideration of the praised thing: nominal. If the praised attribute is a permanent attribute like the attribute of lordship praised in Sura al-Fatiha, one comes with the nominal sentence 'al-hamd'. But if the praised thing is a renewing, occurring matter, one comes with the verbal sentence 'al-hamd' because the verb indicates renewal and occurrence; as in the saying of the author of Jam' al-Jawami': 'We praise You, O Allah, for blessings whose praise indicates their increase.' End. And it is objected: The nominal (sentence) is used in the verses: 'Praise be to Allah who sent down upon His slave the Book' [18:1], 'Praise be to Allah who has guided us to this' [7:43], 'Praise be to Allah who has granted me in old age Ishmael' [14:39], while the praised things here are occurring actions. And it may be answered: That in them the intended meaning is the connection of praise to the essence, for He Most High deserves praise for His essence and His blessings.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the matn's use of 'al-hamd' to begin the poem. The commentator gives three reasons for starting with 'al-hamd': (1) The Quranic precedent—the Quran begins with 'Al-hamdu lillahi rabbil 'alamin', so it is emulated. (2) The phonetic comprehensiveness of the word 'al-hamd'—it uses letters from all three main points of articulation (throat, lips, tongue), ensuring no articulator is without a share. (3) The context (maqam) demands praise, especially since the author intends to follow the sunna (imitation of Quran). Then the commentator discusses a finer point of balaghah: whether to use a nominal sentence (al-hamdu lillah) or a verbal sentence (nahmaduka Allahumma). He cites al-Fanari's rule: if the praised attribute is permanent (like Lordship), use nominal; if it is renewing (like a specific action), use verbal. The objection arises that Quranic verses use nominal sentences for actions (e.g., '...who sent down the Book'), which are renewing. The answer is that in those verses, praise is attached to the essence of Allah, not just the action; He deserves praise for His essence and His blessings equally, so the nominal emphasizes the permanence of the praiseworthiness of His essence.

Key Terms
الحمد
جملة اسمية
جملة فعلية
بلاغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Fanari, which type of sentence should be used when the praised attribute is a permanent quality, such as Lordship?
A) Verbal sentence (jumla fi'liyya)
B) Nominal sentence (jumla ismiyya)
C) Either is acceptable
D) It depends on the speaker's preference

Key Takeaway
The choice between nominal and verbal praise sentences hinges on whether the praised attribute is permanent or renewing, but nominal can also be used for actions when praising the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

When Conflicting Restricteds Send Us Back to the Absolute
Super-commentator refines the principle of carrying two conflicting restricted terms to the absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that two mutually exclusive restrictions are set aside in favor of the absolute.

Core Idea
Conflicting restricteds cancel each other, leaving the absolute ruling.

In usul al-fiqh, when a general ruling (mutlaq) meets a specific restriction (muqayyad), the absolute is interpreted in light of the restriction. But what if two different restrictions conflict? This lesson explains the principle, based on al-Subki and the super-commentator's initial defense, that both restricteds are abandoned for the absolute.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard rule
Normally, when you have an absolute and a restricted text, you interpret the absolute as restricted. E.g., 'free a slave' is absolute, but 'free a believing slave' restricts it.
2
Identify the objection
Someone might object that in the case of conflicting restricteds, the known rule is the opposite: the restricted should be carried to the absolute? Actually, the known rule is that the absolute is carried to the restricted, but here the response uses the reverse.
The objection cited in the text says 'the known is the opposite' meaning that usually the absolute is carried to the restricted, not the other way around.
3
Understand the response
The response clarifies that the known rule applies only when there is one restricted. When two restricteds conflict and cannot be combined, both are discarded and the absolute is used.
This is to remove contradiction. The quote from Jam‘ al-Jawāmi‘ supports this.
4
Memorize the principle
Al-Subki's dictum: 'The restricted by two conflicting [restrictions] dispenses with both.' This is the core principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يُقالُ : فيه حمل المقيَّد على المطلق، والمعروف العكس. لأنَّا نقول : هذا المعروف إنَّما هو في مقيَّدٍ واحدٍ فيحمل عليه المطلق، أما إذا ورد مقيَّدان لا يمكن الجمع بينهما، فيحملان على المطلق دفعاً للتّنافي ; قال في جمع الجوامع»: «والمقيَّدُ بمتنافيين يستغني عنهما». اهـ فقوله: «فيحملان على المطلق ظاهرُهُ أنَّ كلَّ مقيَّدين لا يمكن الجمع بينهما يحملان على المطلق، وليس كذلك ; ألا ترى إلى قوله تعالى في قضاء أيام رمضان : ﴿فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ [البقرة: ١٨٤]، وفي كفَّارة الصيام : فَصِيَامُ شَهْرَيْنِ مُتَتَابِعَيْن [النساء: ٩٢]، وفي صيام التَّمَتُّع : فَصِيَامُ ثَلَاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ في الحَجِّ وَسَبْعَةٍ إِذَا رَجَعْتُمْ ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۹٦]، فأطلق الصّيام في قضاء أيَّام رمضان : عن التتابع والتفريق، وقيَّده في كفَّارة الظهار بالتتابع، وفي صوم التَّمَتُّع بالتفريق، ولا قائل بأنَّ هذين المقيدين يحملان على المطلق. وكلامُ السُّبكي الذي أتى به لا دليل فيه، بل معناه أنَّ المقيَّد بمتنافيين في موضعين وأطلق في موضع آخر؛ كالآيات المذكورة يستغني فيها أطلق فيه عنهما . ثمَّ إِنَّ كلام السُّبكي هذا مقيَّد بما إذا تعدد المحلُّ كما مثلنا، أمَّا إِنِ اتَّحد المحل فيحمل المقيَّدان على المطلق؛ كقوله : لا يَحِلُّ لِامْرَأَةٍ تُؤْمِنُ بِاللَّهِ وَالْيَوْمِ الْآخِرِ أَنْ تُسَافِرَ يَوْماً وَلَيْلَةً، إِلَّا وَمَعَهَا ذُو مَحْرَمٍ»، وروي : ثَلَاثَةَ أَيَّامٍ»، وروي : بَعْضَ يَوْمٍ ، وروي بالإطلاق، يحمل فيه المقيَّد على المطلق؛ لأنَّ المقصود ما يصدق عليه سفر، وأحاديث البسملة والحمدلة من هذا القبيل، فإنّ فيها حمل المقيَّدين على المطلق، فتأمل . قوله : (وَإِلَّا فَقَدْ وَرَدَ) الصَّوابُ إسقاط قوله: «وَإِلَّا»، وترتيب ما بعدها على ما قبلها؛ إنما في ذكرها مِنَ الرَّكاكة.
It is not said: in it is carrying the restricted to the absolute, and the known is the opposite. For we say: this known is only in one restricted, so the absolute is carried upon it; but if two restricteds come that cannot be combined, they are carried to the absolute to repel contradiction. He said in Jam‘ al-Jawāmi‘: 'The restricted by two conflicting [restrictions] dispenses with both.' So his saying: 'they are carried to the absolute' — its apparent meaning is that every two restricteds that cannot be combined are carried to the absolute, but it is not so. Do you not see to the saying of the Most High concerning making up days of Ramadan: 'a number of other days'; and in the expiation of fasting: 'fasting two consecutive months'; and in the fast of tamattu‘: 'fasting three days in Hajj and seven when you return.' So He made the fast absolute in making up days of Ramadan concerning consecutiveness and separation, and restricted it in expiation of dhihar with consecutiveness, and in the fast of tamattu‘ with separation; and no one says that these two restricteds are carried to the absolute. And the statement of al-Subki that he brought has no evidence in it; rather its meaning is that the restricted by two conflicting [restrictions] in two places and absolute in another place — like the mentioned verses — the absolute therein dispenses with them. Then, indeed, this statement of al-Subki is restricted to when the subject is multiple as we exemplified; but if the subject is one, then the two restricteds are carried to the absolute; like his saying: 'It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to travel for a day and a night unless with her is a mahram,' and it is narrated as 'three days,' and it is narrated as 'part of a day,' and it is narrated absolutely; in it the restricted is carried to the absolute, because the intended is what is true of travel. And the hadiths of basmalah and hamdalah are of this category, for in them is carrying the two restricteds to the absolute. So reflect. His saying: (and if not, there has come) — the correct is to drop his saying 'and if not', and to arrange what comes after it upon what before it; only in its mention is awkwardness.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a principle in uṣūl al-fiqh regarding how to handle conflicting restricted (muqayyad) textual evidence. The super-commentator first states the objection: someone might say that this (the earlier answer) involves carrying a restricted term to the absolute, whereas the known rule is the opposite (the absolute is carried to the restricted). The responder counters by explaining that the known rule applies only when there is a single restricted term; then the absolute is interpreted in light of the restriction. However, when two restricted terms are mutually contradictory, they are both carried to the absolute to remove conflict. This is supported by a quote from al-Subki's Jam‘ al-Jawāmi‘. But the super-commentator then refines this: the apparent meaning of al-Subki's statement suggests that any two conflicting restricteds should be carried to the absolute, but this is not universally true, as shown by Quranic examples: fasting for Ramadan make-up is absolute (no mention of consecutiveness), fasting for dhihar is restricted to consecutive months, and fasting for tamattu‘ is restricted to separation (three days in Hajj, seven after). No scholar carries those two restricteds to the absolute. Al-Subki's statement only applies when the restricted rulings are in different contexts (multiple subjects) and the absolute appears elsewhere; but when the subject is the same (e.g., a single prohibition on travel with different restrictions), then the restricteds are carried to the absolute to ascertain the common meaning (safar). The hadith on basmalah and hamdalah are of this latter type, so they are carried to the absolute. A final note recommends dropping the phrase 'and if not' for clarity. All examples are from Islamic law (Quranic verses on fasting and hadith on travel).

Key Terms
المقيَّد
المطلق
الجمع بين المتنافيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the super-commentator, when do two conflicting restricteds NOT get carried to the absolute?
A) When they are in the same subject
B) When they are in different subjects and an absolute ruling exists elsewhere
C) When they are both from the hadith
D) When they are from the Quran

Key Takeaway
Conflicting restricteds are carried to the absolute only when they concern the same legal subject; otherwise, each restriction stands in its own context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider two hadith: one says 'Do not fast on Friday unless you fast the day before or after' and another says 'Fast on Friday'. They conflict on the same subject (fasting on Friday). Apply the principle to determine the ruling.
?
Find an example in fiqh where two conflicting restricteds from different legal topics (e.g., zakat on livestock and zakat on crops) are not carried to the absolute, and explain why.

The Quranic Counterexample: When Restricteds Stay Separate
Super-commentator refines the principle of carrying two conflicting restricted terms to the absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which conflicting restricteds remain separate using the Quranic verses on fasting.

Core Idea
If two restricteds apply to different rulings, they are not merged; each context remains independent.

The super-commentator refines al-Subki's general statement by providing a clear counterexample from the Quran. The three verses on fasting — for make-up, dhihar, and tamattu‘ — have conflicting restrictions (consecutive vs. separated) but scholars do not combine them. This lesson examines why and extracts the key condition for when the principle applies.

Guided Path
1
Read the three Quranic verses
First verse: make-up fasting is absolute (no mention of consecutiveness). Second verse: dhihar expiation restricts to consecutive months. Third verse: tamattu‘ restricts to separated days (three during Hajj, seven after).
The verses are: 2:184, 4:92, 2:196.
2
Compare the restrictions
The restrictions on consecutiveness are contradictory: one requires it, the other prohibits it (by requiring separation). They cannot both be applied at once.
3
Check scholarly practice
No scholar says that the dhihar restriction should be applied to tamattu‘ or vice versa. Each ruling stands on its own.
This contradicts the apparent meaning of al-Subki's statement that 'every two conflicting restricteds are carried to the absolute'.
4
Identify the key condition
The distinction is that the restrictions belong to different legal subjects (make-up, expiation, tamattu‘). When the subject is the same (e.g., the travel prohibition), the restricteds are carried to the absolute.
The super-commentator explicitly states: 'this statement of al-Subki is restricted to when the subject is multiple... but if the subject is one, then the two restricteds are carried to the absolute'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why do scholars not carry the two contradictory restrictions on fasting (consecutive vs. separated) to the absolute?

Refutation of the Obligation of Restricted Praise and Recommendation of Absolute Praise
Author refutes the claim that restricted praise is obligatory and absolute praise is recommended.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the legal and metaphorical use of 'wajib' in the context of praise, and identify the two refutations given against the claim that restricted praise is obligatory and absolute praise is recommended.

Core Idea
The 'obligation' of praise is not a legal duty but a fulfillment of thankfulness.

In this passage, the author critiques a common claim that restricted praise (in response to a blessing) is obligatory, while absolute praise (without specific cause) is recommended. This claim appears in some works, but the author shows it is based on a misunderstanding of the term 'obligation' in this context. You will learn the two refutations and the correct understanding of praise according to the majority of scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Read the opening sentence: 'And likewise their saying: The restricted is obligatory, and the absolute is recommended is refuted in two ways.' This is the claim the author opposes.
The claim is found in some works, e.g., al-Mahalli's commentary.
2
Examine the first refutation
The author says the restriction is always intended in the absolute even if not mentioned. This means the distinction between restricted and absolute is not sharp; the absolute always carries an implicit restriction.
Think: when we thank Allah generally, it is still in response to His continuous blessings.
3
Examine the second refutation
If every blessing required a specific praise, it would be impossible to fulfill due to the countless blessings. This shows the claim leads to undue hardship.
The principle in shari'a is that hardship is to be avoided.
4
Understand the alternative definition
The author presents the majority view: restricted praise is what is in return for a blessing, absolute is not. This definition avoids confusion.
Malik and al-Shafi'i both support this, though they differ on which is better.
5
Clarify the meaning of 'wajib' in this context
The author cites Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi: the obligation here is that it fulfills the duty of thankfulness, not a distinct legal obligation. This resolves the apparent contradiction.
The usuliyyun do not use 'wajib' in this broad sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكَذَا قَوْلُهُمْ: إِنَّ المُقَيَّدَ وَاجِبٌ، وَالمُطلَقَ مَنْدُوبٌ مَرْدُودٌ بِوَجْهَيْنِ: (١) أَحَدُهُمَا: مَا ذُكِرَ مِنْ أَنَّ القَيْدَ مُرَادُ فِي المُطْلَقِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُذْكَرْ. وَثَانِيهُمَا: أَنَّهُ يُوهِمُ وُجُوبَ الحَمْدِ عَلَى العَبْدِ كُلَّمَا حَصَلَتْ لَهُ نِعْمَةٌ، وَهِيَ غَايَةُ الحَرَج؛ إِذْ لَا تَخْلُو لَحْظَةٌ مِنْ نِعَمِ المَوْلَى تَعَالَى عَلَى عَبْدِهِ لَا تُحْصَى، قصارة. وعند الجمهور: المقيَّد: «ما كان في مقابلة نعمة»، والمطلق: «ما لم يكن في مقابلتها»، وهذا مفهوم مِنَ الوجه الثاني الآتي في كلام هذا الشَّارح، ويدلُّ عليه كلام مالك والشافعي رضي تعالى عنهما، وصرح به المحلي في شرح قول السُّبكي: نَحْمَدُكَ اللَّهُمَّ عَلَى نِعَمِ» قال: «وإِنَّما حمد في مقابلة النعم لا مطلقاً؛ لأنَّ الأوّل واجب، والثاني مندوب». اهـ. فقد وجد المحمود عليه في كلٍّ مِنَ المقيَّد والمطلق على كلِّ مِنَ الاصطلاحين، وانتفى الإيهام؛ إذ لا إيهام مع التصريح بالمراد. قوله: (إِنَّ المُقَيَّدَ وَاجِبٌ، وَالمُطْلَقَ مَنْدُوبٌ) التَّعبير بـ «الوجوب» عبر به المحلي واللقاني وغيرهما، وعليه يأتي الإشكال والموجود لكثير في المسألة حكاية الخلاف: هل المقيَّد أفضل، فيكون أكثر ثواباً وعليه مالك؛ لأنَّه أكثر وروداً في القرآن أو المطلق وعليه الشَّافعي؛ وليس فيه تصريح بالوجوب، فلا إشكال؟ لصدقه على جميع المحامد. ومعنى الوجوب المعبر به هنا كما قال شيخ الشيوخ سيدي عبد القادر الفاسي: أنه حيث وقع فهو واجب؛ بمعنى: أنه يخرج به من وجوب شكر النعمة؛ إذ شكرها لا يتعيَّن له جهة القول ولا لفظ الحمد بخصوصه، بل هو أعم حسبما هو معلوم في الشُّكر، فإذا وقع بلفظ الحمد أدّى الواجب. اهـ. ولا يقال مراد من قال: إنَّ المقيَّد واجب أنَّه يُثاب عليه ثواب الواجب، لا أنَّه يأثم إن تركه. لأنا نقول: ليس في اصطلاح الأصوليين إطلاق الواجب على مثل هذا. قوله: (وَثَانِيهُمَا: أَنَّهُ يُوهِمُ وُجُوبَ الحَمْدِ عَلَى العَبْدِ ... إلخ) جوابه: ليس المعنى: أنَّه كلَّما
And likewise their saying: 'The restricted is obligatory, and the absolute is recommended' is refuted in two ways: (1) One of them is what was mentioned: that the restriction is intended in the absolute, even if it is not mentioned. (2) And the second: that it suggests the obligation of praise upon the servant every time a blessing obtains for him, and that is extreme hardship, since no moment is free from the countless blessings of the Lord Almighty upon His servant. [Separator] And according to the majority: the restricted is 'that which is in return for a blessing', and the absolute is 'that which is not in return for it'. And this is understood from the second aspect forthcoming in the speech of this commentator, and it is supported by the speech of Malik and al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with them, and al-Mahalli stated it in his commentary on al-Subki's saying: 'We praise You, O Allah, for blessings.' He said: 'He only praised in return for blessings, not absolutely; because the first is obligatory and the second is recommended.' End. So the praised object is present in both the restricted and the absolute according to both terminologies, and the illusion is removed; for there is no illusion when the intended meaning is explicitly stated. His statement: ('The restricted is obligatory, and the absolute is recommended.') The expression 'obligation' was used by al-Mahalli and al-Laqqani and others, and upon that the objection arises. And what is found with many in this matter is the narration of the disagreement: Is the restricted better, thus having more reward—and Malik holds this because it is more frequent in the Quran—or the absolute, and al-Shafi'i holds this? And there is no explicit mention of obligation, so there is no objection? Because it applies to all praises. And the meaning of 'obligation' expressed here, as said by the chief of the masters Sayyidi Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi, is that wherever it occurs, it is obligatory; meaning: that through it one fulfills the obligation of thanking for the blessing, since thanking is not specifically tied to speech or the particular wording of praise, but is more general as is known concerning thankfulness. So if it occurs with the wording of praise, it fulfills the obligation. End. And it is not said that the one who says the restricted is obligatory means that one is rewarded the reward of an obligatory act, not that one sins if one leaves it; for we say: In the terminology of the usuliyyun, obligation is not applied to such things. His statement: ('And the second: that it suggests the obligation of praise upon the servant... etc.') Its answer: The meaning is not that every time...
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion refuting the claim that restricted praise (muqayyad) is obligatory (wajib) and absolute praise (mutlaq) is recommended (mandub). The author provides two refutations. First, the restriction (i.e., the reason for praise) is always intended in absolute praise even if not explicitly mentioned, so the distinction between obligatory and recommended collapses. Second, the claim would imply that a servant must praise Allah every time he receives a blessing, which is impossible due to the overwhelming number of blessings. The author then explains another terminological distinction: according to the majority (al-jumhur), 'restricted' means praise in return for a specific blessing, and 'absolute' means praise not in return for a blessing. This is supported by the statements of Malik, al-Shafi'i, and al-Mahalli, who hold that praise in return for blessings is obligatory, while absolute praise is recommended. However, the author notes that the initial objection is based on the usage of 'obligation' by al-Mahalli and others. He clarifies that the disagreement among scholars is actually about which is better (more reward), not about obligation in the legal sense. The meaning of 'obligation' here, as explained by Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi, is that the restricted praise fulfills the duty of thanking for the blessing, not that it is a distinct legal obligation. The author further refutes the interpretation that 'obligatory' merely means rewarded as an obligatory act, stating that the usuliyyun do not use 'obligatory' in that way. The passage ends with the beginning of the answer to the second objection, but the main points are clear.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the claim that restricted praise is obligatory and absolute praise is recommended problematic?
A) Because it contradicts the Quran.
B) Because the restriction is always intended in absolute praise.
C) Because the absolute praise is more rewarding.
D) Because it is an innovation.

Key Takeaway
The classification of praise as obligatory or recommended is not about legal duty but about fulfilling the general obligation of thankfulness or performing an optional act.

The Two Objections Against the Claim of Obligation on Muqayyad Hamd
Author lists two objections to the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two objections against the claim that restricted praise is obligatory and explain why they are rejected.

Core Idea
Two objections dismantle the simplistic dichotomy: restricted praise is not necessarily obligatory if the restriction is implicitly present even in the unrestricted.

The author begins by presenting a claim: restricted praise (muqayyad) is obligatory, unrestricted (mutlaq) is recommended. He immediately rejects this with two objections. Let's analyze each objection and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being attacked
Find the initial statement in the Arabic: 'Indeed the restricted is obligatory, and the unrestricted is recommended.' This is the target.
2
Parse the first objection
The first objection states that the restriction (qayd) is intended in the unrestricted (mutlaq) even if not mentioned. Thus the distinction between the two collapses.
This is a logical objection: if the restriction is inherent, then calling one restricted and the other unrestricted is meaningless.
3
Parse the second objection
The second objection claims that if restricted praise is obligatory for each blessing, it leads to impossible hardship because blessings are countless.
4
Understand the resolution
The author explains that the majority defines muqayyad as praise in exchange for a specific blessing, and mutlaq as not. This resolves the implication because the obligation is only when the blessing is noted, not every moment. The objection is based on a misunderstanding of terminology.
The author asserts that when the intended meaning is made explicit, there is no implication (no iham).

(١) - أَحَدُهُمَا : مَا ذُكِرَ مِنْ أَنَّ القَيْدَ مُرَادُ فِي المُطْلَقِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُذْكَرْ . - وَثَانِيهُمَا : أَنَّهُ يُوهِمُ وُجُوبَ الحَمْدِ عَلَى العَبْدِ كُلَّمَا حَصَلَتْ لَهُ نِعْمَةٌ، وَهِيَ غَايَةُ الحَرَج؛ إِذْ لَا تَخْلُو لَحْظَةٌ مِنْ نِعَمِ المَوْلَى تَعَالَى عَلَى عَبْدِهِ لَا تُحْصَى،
وَعند الجمهور : المقيَّد: «ما كان في مقابلة نعمة ، والمطلق: «ما لم يكن في مقابلتها»، وهذا مفهوم مِنَ الوجه الثاني الآتي في كلام هذا الشَّارح، ويدلُّ عليه كلام مالك والشافعي رضي تعالى عنهما، وصرح به المحلي في شرح قول السُّبكي : نَحْمَدُكَ اللَّهُمَّ عَلَى نِعَمِ» قال: «وَإِنَّما حمد في مقابلة النعم لا مطلقاً ؛ لأنَّ الأوّل واجب، والثاني مندوب» . اهـ
فقد وجد المحمود عليه في كلٍّ مِنَ المقيَّد والمطلق على كلِّ مِنَ الاصطلاحين، وانتفى الإيهام؛ إذ لا إيهام مع التصريح بالمراد.
قوله : (إِنَّ المُقَيَّدَ وَاجِبٌ، وَالمُطْلَقَ مَنْدُوبٌ) التَّعبير بـ : «الوجوب» عبر به المحلي واللقاني ب: وغيرهما، وعليه يأتي الإشكال والموجود لكثير في المسألة حكاية الخلاف: هل المقيَّد أفضل، فيكون أكثر ثواباً وعليه مالك ؛ لأنَّه أكثر وروداً في القرآن أو المطلق وعليه الشَّافعي؛ المحامد، وليس فيه تصريح بالوجوب، فلا إشكال؟
ومعنى الوجوب المعبر به هنا - كما قال شيخ الشيوخ سيدي عبد القادر الفاسي - : أنه حيث وقع فهو واجب؛ بمعنى: أنه يخرج به من وجوب شكر النعمة ؛ إذ شكرها لا يتعيَّن له جهة القول ولا لفظ الحمد بخصوصه، بل هو أعم حسبما هو معلوم في الشُّكر ، فإذا وقع بلفظ الحمد أدَّى الواجب . اهـ .
ولا يقال مراد من قال : إنَّ المقيَّد واجب أنَّه يُثاب عليه ثواب الواجب، لا أنَّه يأثم إن تركه .
لأنا نقول : ليس في اصطلاح الأصوليين إطلاق الواجب على مثل هذا .
قوله : (وَثَانِيهُمَا : أَنَّهُ يُوهِمُ وُجُوبَ الحَمْدِ عَلَى العَبْدِ ... إلخ) جوابه : ليس المعنى : أنَّه كلَّما تجدَّدت نعمةٌ وَجَبَ الحمد عليها ، بل المعنى : إذا لاحظها، فإنَّه يجب عليه أن يحمد عليها بلسانه، أو ببعض جوارحه وأركانه، ويعاقب على ترك ذلك، وليس فيه الحرجُ الَّذي في الأوّل . ويدلُّ لهذا قول الكمال : الواجبُ ما كان في مقابلة النّعمة لفظاً أو نيَّة، وقول المحلي : «أي : في مقابلتها»، وحينئذ فقد خفَّ الإشكال.
قوله : (وَقَدْ صَرَّحَ فِي شَرْحِ صُغْرَى الصُّغْرَى مُؤَلِّفُهَا: بِأَنَّ حُكْمَ الحَمْدِ الوُجُوبُ مَرَّةٌ فِي العُمْرِ : الحَجِّ، وَكَلِمَتَيِ الشَّهَادَةِ، وَالصَّلَاةِ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ ، عَلَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ سُلِّمَ وُجُوبُ : الحَمْدِ عَلَى النِّعْمَةِ، لَمْ يَكُنْ لِلقَيْدِ أَثَرٌ فِيهِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَكُونُ حِينَئِذٍ مُرَتَّباً عَلَى حُصُولِ النِّعْمَةِ، لَا عَلَى ذِكْرِ القَيْدِ ، بَلْ ذُكِرَ أَوْ لَمْ يُذْكَرْ ، قَالَهُ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ.
And likewise their statement: 'Indeed the restricted [praise] is obligatory, and the unrestricted is recommended' is rejected by two aspects: (1) One of them: what was mentioned that the restriction is intended in the unrestricted, even if not mentioned. (2) And the second: that it implies the obligation of praise upon the servant every time a blessing occurs to him, and that is the extreme of hardship, since no moment is free from the countless blessings of the Exalted Master upon His servant. And according to the majority: the restricted is 'what is in exchange for a blessing', and the unrestricted is 'what is not in exchange for it'. This is understood from the second aspect coming in the speech of this commentator, and the speech of Malik and al-Shafi'i—may the Exalted be pleased with them—indicates it, and al-Mahalli stated it in his commentary on al-Subki's saying: 'We praise You, O Allah, for blessings.' He said: 'He only praised in exchange for blessings, not absolutely, because the first is obligatory and the second is recommended.' End. So the object of praise is found in both the restricted and unrestricted according to both terminologies, and the implication is removed, for there is no implication when the intended meaning is stated explicitly. His saying: 'Indeed the restricted is obligatory, and the unrestricted is recommended'—the expression 'obligation' was used by al-Mahalli and al-Laqani and others, and upon this the problem arises. The common view in the issue is the narration of the disagreement: Is the restricted better, thus more reward—and upon this is Malik because it occurs more in the Qur'an—or the unrestricted—and upon this is al-Shafi'i—[but] there is no explicit mention of obligation, so there is no problem? And the meaning of obligation expressed here—as Shaykh al-Shuyukh Sayyidi 'Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi said—is that wherever it occurs it is obligatory, meaning it discharges one from the obligation of thanking for the blessing, since gratitude is not specifically confined to the mode of speech nor to the specific wording of praise, but is more general as is known concerning gratitude. So if it occurs with the wording of praise, it fulfills the obligation. End. And let it not be said that what is meant by the one who says the restricted is obligatory is that one is rewarded with the reward of the obligatory, not that one sins by leaving it. For we say: In the terminology of the Usuliyyun, 'obligatory' is not applied to such. His saying: 'And the second: that it implies the obligation of praise upon the servant... etc.' Its answer: The meaning is not that every time a blessing is renewed, praise becomes obligatory upon it. Rather the meaning is: when he notices it, then it is obligatory upon him to praise for it with his tongue or with some of his limbs and organs, and he is punished for leaving that, and there is no hardship in it like the first. Supporting this is the saying of al-Kamal: 'The obligatory is what is in exchange for a blessing, verbally or by intention,' and the saying of al-Mahalli: 'i.e., in exchange for it.' Then the difficulty became light. His saying: 'And he stated explicitly in Sharh Sughra al-Sughra its author: that the ruling of praise is obligation once in a lifetime—like Hajj, the two words of testimony, and salutation upon the Prophet—and that even if the obligation of praise for a blessing is conceded, the restriction has no effect in it, because it is then contingent upon the occurrence of the blessing, not upon mentioning the restriction, whether mentioned or not.' Said by some verified scholars.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to a claim about the classification of praise (hamd). The author begins by stating that the claim 'the restricted (muqayyad) is obligatory and the unrestricted (mutlaq) is recommended' is rejected for two reasons. The first objection is that the restriction (qayd) is intended even in the unrestricted, even if not mentioned explicitly; thus the distinction collapses. The second objection is that it implies praise must be offered for every new blessing, which is impossible due to the countless blessings from Allah, leading to hardship. The commentator then explains that according to the majority of scholars (Malik, Shafi'i), 'restricted praise' means praise in exchange for a specific blessing, and 'unrestricted praise' means praise not in exchange for any blessing. This is understood from the second objection itself. He quotes al-Mahalli to support this. The commentator then addresses the meaning of 'obligation' (wujub) in this context. He clarifies, quoting 'Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi, that the obligation is not of the same type as legal obligations; rather, it means that if one offers praise, it fulfills the duty of thankfulness (shukr), since thankfulness is not confined to specific words. He rejects the idea that it is merely supererogatory with extra reward. In response to the second objection, he explains that the obligation is conditional upon noticing the blessing, not every moment, and it can be fulfilled with any limb, not just speech, thus it is not excessively burdensome. He supports this with quotes from al-Kamal and al-Mahalli. Finally, he cites a point from Sharh Sughra al-Sughra that the fundamental obligation of praise is once in a lifetime, and even if it were obligatory for each blessing, the presence or absence of the restriction in wording would not affect the obligation since it is contingent on the blessing itself, not the wording. This reinforces that the initial claim's terminology is not problematic when properly understood.

Key Takeaway
The two objections show that the simplistic claim about muqayyad and mutlaq hamd is based on a misunderstanding of terminology; when properly defined, the distinction holds without adverse implications.

The Technical Meaning of 'Wujub' for Restricted Praise
Author clarifies the meaning of 'obligation' in the context of hamd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how 'wujub' (obligation) is used in the context of hamd and distinguish it from legal obligation in Usul al-Fiqh.

Core Idea
The 'obligation' of restricted praise means it fulfills the duty of gratitude, not that it is a legally punishable duty.

The author addresses a potential confusion: using the word 'wajib' for restricted praise might be taken to mean a strict legal obligation. He clarifies, quoting Shaykh 'Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi, that the obligation here is one of discharging the duty of thankfulness (shukr), not a legally enforceable duty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem with the term 'wajib'
The term 'obligation' (wujub) can be misunderstood as a legal obligation with punishment for omission. Find where the author acknowledges this issue.
The author notes that the expression was used by al-Mahalli and others, and this leads to the problem (ishkal).
2
Extract the definition from Shaykh 'Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi
Read the quote: 'wherever it occurs it is obligatory, meaning it discharges one from the obligation of thanking for the blessing.' Note that it is about discharging shukr.
Shukr is more general than verbal praise.
3
Understand why it is not like other wujub in Usul
The author rejects the idea that it is merely rewarded like an obligatory act without sin for leaving. He says that in Usul al-Fiqh terminology, 'wajib' is not applied to such a category.
In Usul, 'wajib' implies sin for omission; here it does not.
4
Recognize the larger implication
Praise can fulfill gratitude but is not mandatory in a punitive sense. This distinction is crucial for understanding the flexibility of worship.
The obligation is a moral-religious duty, not a legal one.

وَكَذَا قَوْلُهُمْ: إِنَّ المُقَيَّدَ وَاجِبٌ، وَالمُطلَقَ مَنْدُوبٌ مَرْدُودٌ بِوَجْهَيْنِ :
(١) - أَحَدُهُمَا : مَا ذُكِرَ مِنْ أَنَّ القَيْدَ مُرَادُ فِي المُطْلَقِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُذْكَرْ . - وَثَانِيهُمَا : أَنَّهُ يُوهِمُ وُجُوبَ الحَمْدِ عَلَى العَبْدِ كُلَّمَا حَصَلَتْ لَهُ نِعْمَةٌ، وَهِيَ غَايَةُ الحَرَج؛ إِذْ لَا تَخْلُو لَحْظَةٌ مِنْ نِعَمِ المَوْلَى تَعَالَى عَلَى عَبْدِهِ لَا تُحْصَى،
وَعند الجمهور : المقيَّد: «ما كان في مقابلة نعمة ، والمطلق: «ما لم يكن في مقابلتها»، وهذا مفهوم مِنَ الوجه الثاني الآتي في كلام هذا الشَّارح، ويدلُّ عليه كلام مالك والشافعي رضي تعالى عنهما، وصرح به المحلي في شرح قول السُّبكي : نَحْمَدُكَ اللَّهُمَّ عَلَى نِعَمِ» قال: «وَإِنَّما حمد في مقابلة النعم لا مطلقاً ؛ لأنَّ الأوّل واجب، والثاني مندوب» . اهـ
فقد وجد المحمود عليه في كلٍّ مِنَ المقيَّد والمطلق على كلِّ مِنَ الاصطلاحين، وانتفى الإيهام؛ إذ لا إيهام مع التصريح بالمراد.
قوله : (إِنَّ المُقَيَّدَ وَاجِبٌ، وَالمُطْلَقَ مَنْدُوبٌ) التَّعبير بـ : «الوجوب» عبر به المحلي واللقاني ب: وغيرهما، وعليه يأتي الإشكال والموجود لكثير في المسألة حكاية الخلاف: هل المقيَّد أفضل، فيكون أكثر ثواباً وعليه مالك ؛ لأنَّه أكثر وروداً في القرآن أو المطلق وعليه الشَّافعي؛ المحامد، وليس فيه تصريح بالوجوب، فلا إشكال؟
ومعنى الوجوب المعبر به هنا - كما قال شيخ الشيوخ سيدي عبد القادر الفاسي - : أنه حيث وقع فهو واجب؛ بمعنى: أنه يخرج به من وجوب شكر النعمة ؛ إذ شكرها لا يتعيَّن له جهة القول ولا لفظ الحمد بخصوصه، بل هو أعم حسبما هو معلوم في الشُّكر ، فإذا وقع بلفظ الحمد أدَّى الواجب . اهـ .
ولا يقال مراد من قال : إنَّ المقيَّد واجب أنَّه يُثاب عليه ثواب الواجب، لا أنَّه يأثم إن تركه .
لأنا نقول : ليس في اصطلاح الأصوليين إطلاق الواجب على مثل هذا .
قوله : (وَثَانِيهُمَا : أَنَّهُ يُوهِمُ وُجُوبَ الحَمْدِ عَلَى العَبْدِ ... إلخ) جوابه : ليس المعنى : أنَّه كلَّما تجدَّدت نعمةٌ وَجَبَ الحمد عليها ، بل المعنى : إذا لاحظها، فإنَّه يجب عليه أن يحمد عليها بلسانه، أو ببعض جوارحه وأركانه، ويعاقب على ترك ذلك، وليس فيه الحرجُ الَّذي في الأوّل . ويدلُّ لهذا قول الكمال : الواجبُ ما كان في مقابلة النّعمة لفظاً أو نيَّة، وقول المحلي : «أي : في مقابلتها»، وحينئذ فقد خفَّ الإشكال.
قوله : (وَقَدْ صَرَّحَ فِي شَرْحِ صُغْرَى الصُّغْرَى مُؤَلِّفُهَا: بِأَنَّ حُكْمَ الحَمْدِ الوُجُوبُ مَرَّةٌ فِي العُمْرِ : الحَجِّ، وَكَلِمَتَيِ الشَّهَادَةِ، وَالصَّلَاةِ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ ، عَلَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ سُلِّمَ وُجُوبُ : الحَمْدِ عَلَى النِّعْمَةِ، لَمْ يَكُنْ لِلقَيْدِ أَثَرٌ فِيهِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَكُونُ حِينَئِذٍ مُرَتَّباً عَلَى حُصُولِ النِّعْمَةِ، لَا عَلَى ذِكْرِ القَيْدِ ، بَلْ ذُكِرَ أَوْ لَمْ يُذْكَرْ ، قَالَهُ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ.
And likewise their statement: 'Indeed the restricted [praise] is obligatory, and the unrestricted is recommended' is rejected by two aspects: (1) One of them: what was mentioned that the restriction is intended in the unrestricted, even if not mentioned. (2) And the second: that it implies the obligation of praise upon the servant every time a blessing occurs to him, and that is the extreme of hardship, since no moment is free from the countless blessings of the Exalted Master upon His servant. And according to the majority: the restricted is 'what is in exchange for a blessing', and the unrestricted is 'what is not in exchange for it'. This is understood from the second aspect coming in the speech of this commentator, and the speech of Malik and al-Shafi'i—may the Exalted be pleased with them—indicates it, and al-Mahalli stated it in his commentary on al-Subki's saying: 'We praise You, O Allah, for blessings.' He said: 'He only praised in exchange for blessings, not absolutely, because the first is obligatory and the second is recommended.' End. So the object of praise is found in both the restricted and unrestricted according to both terminologies, and the implication is removed, for there is no implication when the intended meaning is stated explicitly. His saying: 'Indeed the restricted is obligatory, and the unrestricted is recommended'—the expression 'obligation' was used by al-Mahalli and al-Laqani and others, and upon this the problem arises. The common view in the issue is the narration of the disagreement: Is the restricted better, thus more reward—and upon this is Malik because it occurs more in the Qur'an—or the unrestricted—and upon this is al-Shafi'i—[but] there is no explicit mention of obligation, so there is no problem? And the meaning of obligation expressed here—as Shaykh al-Shuyukh Sayyidi 'Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi said—is that wherever it occurs it is obligatory, meaning it discharges one from the obligation of thanking for the blessing, since gratitude is not specifically confined to the mode of speech nor to the specific wording of praise, but is more general as is known concerning gratitude. So if it occurs with the wording of praise, it fulfills the obligation. End. And let it not be said that what is meant by the one who says the restricted is obligatory is that one is rewarded with the reward of the obligatory, not that one sins by leaving it. For we say: In the terminology of the Usuliyyun, 'obligatory' is not applied to such. His saying: 'And the second: that it implies the obligation of praise upon the servant... etc.' Its answer: The meaning is not that every time a blessing is renewed, praise becomes obligatory upon it. Rather the meaning is: when he notices it, then it is obligatory upon him to praise for it with his tongue or with some of his limbs and organs, and he is punished for leaving that, and there is no hardship in it like the first. Supporting this is the saying of al-Kamal: 'The obligatory is what is in exchange for a blessing, verbally or by intention,' and the saying of al-Mahalli: 'i.e., in exchange for it.' Then the difficulty became light. His saying: 'And he stated explicitly in Sharh Sughra al-Sughra its author: that the ruling of praise is obligation once in a lifetime—like Hajj, the two words of testimony, and salutation upon the Prophet—and that even if the obligation of praise for a blessing is conceded, the restriction has no effect in it, because it is then contingent upon the occurrence of the blessing, not upon mentioning the restriction, whether mentioned or not.' Said by some verified scholars.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to a claim about the classification of praise (hamd). The author begins by stating that the claim 'the restricted (muqayyad) is obligatory and the unrestricted (mutlaq) is recommended' is rejected for two reasons. The first objection is that the restriction (qayd) is intended even in the unrestricted, even if not mentioned explicitly; thus the distinction collapses. The second objection is that it implies praise must be offered for every new blessing, which is impossible due to the countless blessings from Allah, leading to hardship. The commentator then explains that according to the majority of scholars (Malik, Shafi'i), 'restricted praise' means praise in exchange for a specific blessing, and 'unrestricted praise' means praise not in exchange for any blessing. This is understood from the second objection itself. He quotes al-Mahalli to support this. The commentator then addresses the meaning of 'obligation' (wujub) in this context. He clarifies, quoting 'Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi, that the obligation is not of the same type as legal obligations; rather, it means that if one offers praise, it fulfills the duty of thankfulness (shukr), since thankfulness is not confined to specific words. He rejects the idea that it is merely supererogatory with extra reward. In response to the second objection, he explains that the obligation is conditional upon noticing the blessing, not every moment, and it can be fulfilled with any limb, not just speech, thus it is not excessively burdensome. He supports this with quotes from al-Kamal and al-Mahalli. Finally, he cites a point from Sharh Sughra al-Sughra that the fundamental obligation of praise is once in a lifetime, and even if it were obligatory for each blessing, the presence or absence of the restriction in wording would not affect the obligation since it is contingent on the blessing itself, not the wording. This reinforces that the initial claim's terminology is not problematic when properly understood.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الشكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Shaykh 'Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi, what does the 'obligation' of muqayyad hamd mean?
A) It is rewarded like an obligatory act.
B) It discharges the duty of thanking for the blessing.
C) It is a legal obligation in Usul al-Fiqh.
D) It must be done with specific wording.

Key Takeaway
The term 'obligatory' for restricted praise is not a legal obligation but means it fulfills the duty of gratitude, a crucial semantic distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a scenario: Someone says 'Alhamdulillah' without any specific blessing in mind. Using the definition from this lesson, is that considered obligatory or recommended? Explain.
?
In a discussion about the obligation of thanking Allah, a student claims that 'wajib' always implies sin for omission. Identify the error and correct it using the author's clarification.

Commentary Notes
advanced
12 min
Resolving the Burden of Endless Obligation: Intent and Moderate Praxis
Author responds to the second objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the author resolves the objection that restricted praise would require constant praise, and understand the role of intention and physical actions.

Core Idea
Praise is only obligatory upon noticing a blessing, not for every moment, and it can be expressed through any limb, not just speech.

The second objection claimed that if restricted praise is obligatory for each blessing, it becomes an impossible burden. The author responds by clarifying that the obligation is conditional upon noticing (mulahadha) the blessing, and can be fulfilled with any limb, not just speech. This significantly lightens the perceived hardship.

Guided Path
1
Restate the second objection
The objection: if praise is obligatory for every blessing, it leads to unbearable hardship because blessings are countless.
2
Identify the author's answer
The author says the meaning is not that every new blessing requires praise, but only when one notices (lāḥaẓa) the blessing.
This is a key nuance: the obligation is conditional on awareness.
3
Understand the means of fulfillment
The praise can be done with the tongue or with other limbs and organs (jawaarih wa arkan). This includes actions like prostrating or giving charity as gratitude.
This reduces hardship.
4
Examine supporting quotes
Al-Kamal says 'waajib ma kaana fi muqabilati al-ni'mati lafdzan aw niyya' (obligatory is what is in exchange for a blessing, verbally or by intention). Al-Mahalli says 'ay fi muqabilatiha' (i.e., in exchange for it).
5
Consider the final point from Sharh Sughra al-Sughra
The author adds that there is a base obligation of praise once in a lifetime, like Hajj and the Shahada. Even if we concede obligation per blessing, the restriction (qayd) has no effect because the obligation is contingent on the occurrence of the blessing, not the wording.
This further reduces the perceived burden.
