And likewise their statement: 'Indeed the restricted [praise] is obligatory, and the unrestricted is recommended' is rejected by two aspects: (1) One of them: what was mentioned that the restriction is intended in the unrestricted, even if not mentioned. (2) And the second: that it implies the obligation of praise upon the servant every time a blessing occurs to him, and that is the extreme of hardship, since no moment is free from the countless blessings of the Exalted Master upon His servant. And according to the majority: the restricted is 'what is in exchange for a blessing', and the unrestricted is 'what is not in exchange for it'. This is understood from the second aspect coming in the speech of this commentator, and the speech of Malik and al-Shafi'i—may the Exalted be pleased with them—indicates it, and al-Mahalli stated it in his commentary on al-Subki's saying: 'We praise You, O Allah, for blessings.' He said: 'He only praised in exchange for blessings, not absolutely, because the first is obligatory and the second is recommended.' End. So the object of praise is found in both the restricted and unrestricted according to both terminologies, and the implication is removed, for there is no implication when the intended meaning is stated explicitly. His saying: 'Indeed the restricted is obligatory, and the unrestricted is recommended'—the expression 'obligation' was used by al-Mahalli and al-Laqani and others, and upon this the problem arises. The common view in the issue is the narration of the disagreement: Is the restricted better, thus more reward—and upon this is Malik because it occurs more in the Qur'an—or the unrestricted—and upon this is al-Shafi'i—[but] there is no explicit mention of obligation, so there is no problem? So if it occurs with the wording of praise, it fulfills the obligation. End. Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to a claim about the classification of praise (hamd). The author begins by stating that the claim 'the restricted (muqayyad) is obligatory and the unrestricted (mutlaq) is recommended' is rejected for two reasons. The first objection is that the restriction (qayd) is intended even in the unrestricted, even if not mentioned explicitly; thus the distinction collapses. This is understood from the second objection itself. He quotes al-Mahalli to support this. The commentator then addresses the meaning of 'obligation' (wujub) in this context. He clarifies, quoting 'Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi, that the obligation is not of the same type as legal obligations; rather, it means that if one offers praise, it fulfills the duty of thankfulness (shukr), since thankfulness is not confined to specific words. He rejects the idea that it is merely supererogatory with extra reward. He supports this with quotes from al-Kamal and al-Mahalli. This reinforces that the initial claim's terminology is not problematic when properly understood.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when does praise become obligatory in response to a blessing?
A) Every time a blessing occurs.
B) Only when one notices the blessing.
C) Only if the blessing is major.
D) Only if the blessing is verbalized.

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because the obligation is conditional on noticing the blessing and can be fulfilled through various means, making it practical, not burdensome.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Evaluating the Definite Article: Perfection vs. Generic Inclusion
Author analyzes whether the definite article indicates perfection or generic inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the definite article 'al' in a given Islamic textual phrase indicates perfection (kamal) or generic inclusion (jins), using the author's criteria and applying it to real examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The definite article 'al' can signal perfection or generic inclusion; correct interpretation depends on context and observable reality.

In this lesson, we examine a critical analysis from a classical commentary on the function of the definite article 'al' in the phrase 'liarbab al-‘uqul' (for the possessors of intellects). The author rejects the interpretation that 'al' indicates perfection, arguing instead that it must be generic (jinsiyya). His reasoning relies on a key observation: even those with imperfect intellects (e.g., the Sophists) have knowledge, which contradicts the perfection interpretation. We will walk through his argument and learn how to apply this linguistic-logical tool to similar phrases in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two functions of 'al'
Recognize that the definite article 'al' can indicate either perfection (kamal) – singling out the perfect individuals – or generic inclusion (jins) – encompassing all individuals of a category. The author critiques the perfection view.
Perfection 'al' is often replaced metaphorically by 'kull' (every), as in 'anta al-rajulu 'ilman' meaning 'you are the perfect man in knowledge'.
2
Extract the author's critique
The author argues that if 'al' were for perfection in 'liarbab al-‘uqul', the meaning would be that Allah gives knowledge only to those with perfect intellects. This contradicts observable reality because weak intellects also receive knowledge according to their capacity.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: the perfection interpretation leads to a false implication, so it must be rejected.
3
Examine the counterexample
The author cites the Sophists (al-sūfastā'iyya) and Samniyya as groups who have knowledge despite denying it. Their denial is obstinacy, not ignorance. Thus, intellect alone is not sufficient for perfection, and the phrase includes all possessors of intellect.
This counterexample shows that even those who reject knowledge still have it, so the 'al' cannot exclude imperfect intellects.
4
Assess the author's conclusion
The author concludes that 'al' must be generic (jinsiyya) to include all individuals. The intended meaning ('the perfect ones') is understood via metonymy (majāz). The generic 'al' encompasses characteristics, not individuals.
The author's reasoning is consistent: generic inclusion avoids the false implication and matches empirical evidence.
5
Apply to an Islamic science passage
Take a phrase like 'al-‘ulamā' warathat al-anbiyā'' (scholars are heirs of prophets). Analyze whether the 'al' in 'al-‘ulamā'' is for perfection or generic inclusion. Use the author's method: would the perfection interpretation exclude some scholars? If so, the generic interpretation is preferred.
In usul al-fiqh, such analysis affects the scope of rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَأَلْ» فِيهِ لِلْكَمَالِ وهي التي يخلفها «كل» مجازاً؛ نحو: «أَنْتَ الرَّجُلُ عَلَماً، فهي جنسية لشمول الخصائص لا الأفراد. قوله : لِأَرْبَابِ العُقُولِ (۱)) أي : لأصحاب جميع العقول، والمراد : الكامل منها، واختار كونها للجنس مجازاً، لا للجنس على سبيل الحقيقة؛ للإشارة إلى أنه قد حصل العقل لأقوام ولم يحصل لهم العلم كـ: «السُّوفسطائيّة، والسُّمنيَّة»، وهو في ذلك تابع للسَّنوسي في «شرح مختصره» وفيه نظر؛ إذِ السُّوفسطائية - وهم : قوم أنكروا العلم - لهم علم ولا يلزم من إنكارهم له انتفاؤه عنهم ؛ إذ إنكارُهُمُ الضَّروريَّات مكابرة وعناد، فالوجه أن تكون «أل» جنسيَّة؛ لشمول الأفراد؛ إذ لو كانت للكمال لكان المعنى : إنَّ الله تعالى أخرج النتائج لأهل العقول الكاملة، حتّى إِنَّ مَن لم يكن عقله كاملاً لا يعطى له علمٌ إذا نظر، والمشاهدة على خلاف ذلك يعطى لضعاف العقول مِنَ العِلم حسب عقولهم . قول المحشي : قوله : لأرباب العقول ليس في شرح البناني» هذه العبارة، ولعلها نسخة أخرى وقعت للمحشي .
His saying: 'And the 'al' in it is for perfection.' It is the one that is replaced by 'kull' metaphorically, like your saying: 'You are the man in knowledge.' So it is generic (jinsiyya) for encompassing characteristics, not individuals. His saying: 'for the possessors of intellects': i.e., for the owners of all intellects, and what is meant is the perfect ones. He chose it to be for the genus metaphorically, not for the genus as a reality, to indicate that intellect has been obtained by groups who have not obtained knowledge, like the Sophists and the Samniyya. In that he follows al-Sanusi in his Sharh Mukhtasiri, and there is a consideration in it. Because the Sophists - they are a people who denied knowledge - have knowledge, and their denial of it does not necessitate its absence from them, because their denial of necessary truths is obstinacy and stubbornness. So the proper view is that 'al' should be generic, for encompassing individuals, because if it were for perfection, the meaning would be: Allah extracts results for the people of perfect intellects, so that someone whose intellect is not complete would not be given knowledge when he looks, whereas observation is contrary to that, for weak intellects are given knowledge according to their intellects. The marginal note: The words 'for possessors of intellects' are not in Sharh al-Bannani; this expression perhaps is another copy that reached the annotator.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of a specific phrase in the poem: "وَأَلْ فِيهِ لِلْكَمَالِ" (the 'al' in it is for perfection). The author questions this interpretation. He first states that if 'al' were for perfection, it would be replaced by 'kull' metaphorically, as in 'anta al-rajulu 'ilman' (you are the man in knowledge) meaning you are perfect. But he argues that 'al' here is actually generic (jinsiyya) in the sense of encompassing characteristics, not individuals. He then critiques the earlier commentator al-Sanusi's view that 'al' is for genus metaphorically, introducing the idea that the phrase 'liarbab al-‘uqul' (for possessors of intellects) is meant to include all possessors of intellect, but the perfect ones are intended. However, the author objects: if 'al' were for perfection, it would imply that only those with perfect intellects receive knowledge from Allah, but observation shows that even weak intellects are given knowledge. He uses the example of the Sophists and Samniyya who have knowledge despite denying it. Thus, he concludes that 'al' must be generic (jinsiyya) to include all individuals. The marginal note at the end clarifies a textual variant.

الكمال

B) Because foolish people also have knowledge
C) Because al-Sanusi said so
D) Because the definite article cannot indicate perfection

Key Takeaway
The definite article 'al' in Islamic texts must be interpreted contextually; the perfection interpretation can be ruled out if it contradicts observable reality that imperfect individuals also possess the attribute.

Provide evidence from the context of the hadith (e.g., are imperfect scholars still considered scholars and heirs?).
?
Consider the Quranic verse 'innamā al-mu’minūn alladhīna āmanū billāhi wa rasūlih' (إنما المؤمنون الذين آمنوا بالله ورسوله, Al-Hujurat 15). Evaluate the 'al' in 'al-mu’minūn': is it for perfection (only perfect believers) or generic (all believers)? Use the author's criteria of observable reality (e.g., do imperfect believers still have iman?).

The Definite Article: Completeness vs Genus
Commentator debates whether 'al' signifies completeness or genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of the definite article 'al' (for completeness vs. for genus) and evaluate the arguments for each as presented in this commentary.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'al' changes whether only perfect intellects or all intellects receive knowledge.

In this passage, the commentator debates whether the definite article 'al' in 'li arbab al-'uqul' signifies completeness (equivalent to 'kull') or genus (covering all individuals of the class). This grammatical point has theological implications: if 'al' is for completeness, then only those with perfect intellects receive knowledge from observation; if for genus, then all intellects, even weak ones, receive knowledge. The commentator ultimately favors the generic interpretation, citing observable reality. Understanding this debate sharpens our ability to interpret Islamic texts precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
The passage presents two interpretations: 'al' for completeness (like 'kull') and 'al' for genus (generic). Note the example 'You are the man in knowledge' for completeness.
Completeness indicates perfection in the attribute; genus indicates all individuals of a class.
2
Examine the argument for completeness
The poet initially uses 'al' for completeness, metaphorically replaced by 'kull'. Focus on the phrase 'li arbab al-'uqul' meaning 'possessors of all intellects' but intended meaning is complete ones.
3
Consider the commentator's objection
The commentator argues that if 'al' were for completeness, only those with perfect intellect would receive knowledge, but observation shows weak intellects also receive knowledge according to their capacity.
This objection is based on empirical evidence from the world.
4
Refute the counterexample
The commentator addresses the objection that some groups (Sophists) have intellect but no knowledge. He argues they do have knowledge; their denial is stubbornness, not actual ignorance.
This refutation supports the generic interpretation.
5
Conclude the correct interpretation
The commentator concludes that 'al' is for genus, encompassing all individuals, because that aligns with observable reality.
The generic interpretation is deemed correct over completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَأَلْ» فِيهِ لِلْكَمَالِ) وهي التي يخلفها «كل» مجازاً؛ نحو: «أَنْتَ الرَّجُلُ عَلَماً»، فهي جنسية لشمول الخصائص لا الأفراد. قوله : لِأَرْبَابِ العُقُولِ أي : لأصحاب جميع العقول، والمراد : الكامل منها، واختار كونها للجنس مجازاً، لا للجنس على سبيل الحقيقة؛ للإشارة إلى أنه قد حصل العقل لأقوام ولم يحصل لهم العلم كـ: «السُّوفسطائيّة، والسُّمنيَّة»، وهو في ذلك تابع للسَّنوسي في «شرح مختصره» وفيه نظر؛ إذِ السُّوفسطائية - وهم : قوم أنكروا العلم - لهم علم ولا يلزم من إنكارهم له انتفاؤه عنهم ؛ إذ إنكارُهُمُ الضَّروريَّات مكابرة وعناد، فالوجه أن تكون «أل» جنسيَّة؛ لشمول الأفراد؛ إذ لو كانت للكمال لكان المعنى : إنَّ الله تعالى أخرج النتائج لأهل العقول الكاملة، حتّى إِنَّ مَن لم يكن عقله كاملاً لا يعطى له علمٌ إذا نظر، والمشاهدة على خلاف ذلك يعطى لضعاف العقول مِنَ العِلم حسب عقولهم.
His statement: 'And 'al' in it is for completeness' — and it is that which 'kull' replaces metaphorically; like: 'You are the man in knowledge', so it is generic for encompassing qualities not individuals. His statement: 'for the people of intellects' meaning: for the possessors of all intellects, and the intended is the complete ones, and he chose that it is for genus metaphorically, not for genus in reality; to indicate that intellect has been obtained by some people but they have not obtained knowledge, like the Sophists and the Sumneyya. And he follows al-Sanusi in his 'Sharh Mukhtasarihi' and there is consideration; because the Sophists — they are people who denied knowledge — have knowledge, and their denial does not necessitate its absence from them; indeed their denial of necessary matters is stubbornness and obstinacy. So the correct view is that 'al' is generic for encompassing individuals; because if it were for completeness, the meaning would be: God extracts the results for those with complete intellects, such that one whose intellect is not complete would not be given knowledge when he considers, but observation is contrary to that: weak intellects are given knowledge according to their intellects.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage discusses the grammatical and semantic interpretation of the definite article 'al' in the poet's phrase 'li arbab al-'uqul'. The commentator begins by noting that the poet uses 'al' for completeness, meaning it metaphorically stands for 'kull' (all). He gives the example: 'You are the man in knowledge' where 'al-rajul' indicates completeness in the attribute. However, the commentator then presents a different view: that 'al' here is for genus (jins), not completeness, because it encompasses all individuals of the intellect class, not just perfect ones. He argues that if 'al' were for completeness, then only those with perfect intellect would receive knowledge, but observation shows that even those with weak intellect are given knowledge according to their capacity. The commentator refutes the objection that some groups like the Sophists have intellect but lack knowledge, by stating that they do have knowledge despite denying it; their denial is due to stubbornness, not actual ignorance. Therefore, the correct view is that 'al' is generic for individuals.

للكمال
للجنس
السوفسطائية

B) The observation that weak intellects still receive knowledge
C) The example of the Sophists
D) Al-Sanusi's authority

Key Takeaway
The commentator argues that 'al' in 'li arbab al-'uqul' is for genus, not completeness, based on observable evidence that even imperfect intellects receive knowledge.

Two Types of Ignorance: Simple and Compound
Commentator defines and contrasts simple ignorance and compound ignorance with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple ignorance from compound ignorance and identify cases where the term 'ignorance' is inapplicable.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is worse than simple ignorance because it involves a false belief, not just a lack of knowledge.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from the commentator that defines and contrasts two types of ignorance: simple and compound. The commentator also clarifies a crucial condition: ignorance can only be attributed to beings capable of knowledge. By understanding this distinction, you will be able to apply the concept correctly in Islamic scholarship, avoiding errors like those in the poetic example cited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find the Arabic phrases that define simple ignorance (الجهل البسيط) and compound ignorance (الجهل المركب). Note the exact wording: simple ignorance is 'عدم العلم عما شأنه من العلم', compound ignorance is 'تصور المعلوم على خلاف هيئته'.
The definitions are given in the commentator's own words.
2
Analyze the condition 'مما شأنه من العلم'
The phrase 'that which ought to be known' restricts the application of ignorance. The commentator uses this to exclude walls and animals—they are not expected to know, so they cannot be called ignorant.
This condition prevents misuse of the term.
3
Distinguish non-existential vs existential
Simple ignorance is non-existential (عدمي) because it is a lack. Compound ignorance is existential (وجودي) because it is a positive misapprehension. The commentator declares the latter 'uglier' (أقبح).
This evaluative aspect is important for ethical judgments.
4
Evaluate the donkey example
Read the poetic example and see how the commentator refutes it. The poet describes the donkey as having simple ignorance, but a donkey is not capable of knowledge, so the description is false.
This shows practical application of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مِنْ) سَحَابِ الجَهْل الجهل على قسمين : أحدهما : «عدم العلم عمَّا شأنه من العلم، ويسمى : «الجهل البسيط». والثَّاني : «تصوُّر المعلوم على خلاف هيئته»؛ أي: ما هو عليه في الواقع، ويسمى: «الجهل المركب». والأوَّلُ عدميّ، والثاني وجودي وهو أقبح مِنَ الأَوَّل. واحترزنا بقولنا : عَمَّا مِنْ شَأْنِهِ العِلْم عن الجدار والبهيمة مثلاً، فلا يُوصَفُ شيءٌ مِن ذلك بالجهل، وبه تعلم بطلان قول القائل : قال حِمارُ الحَكِيمِ تُوْمَا لَوْ أَنْصَفَ الدَّهْرُ كُنْتُ أَرْكَبُ لأَنَّنِي جَاهِلُ جَاهِلُ بَسِيطُ وَصَاحِبِي جَاهِلٌ مُرَكَّ حيث وصف الحمار بالجهل البسيط.
His statement: 'from the clouds of ignorance' — ignorance is of two types: one: 'absence of knowledge of that which ought to be known', called 'simple ignorance'. Second: 'conceiving the known contrary to its reality'; i.e., what it is in actuality, called 'compound ignorance'. The first is non-existential, the second existential and it is uglier than the first. We restricted by 'that which ought to be known' to exclude, for example, a wall or an animal — such things are not described as ignorant. And by this you know the falsehood of the one who said: 'The philosopher Tomas's donkey said: if time were just, I would ride, because I am ignorant, simply ignorant, and my companion is compoundly ignorant' — where he described the donkey as simply ignorant.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of ignorance: simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basit) which is the absence of knowledge about something that should be known, and compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) which is having a false conception of something. The author clarifies that simple ignorance is non-existential (an absence) while compound ignorance is existential (a positive misperception) and is worse. He then refutes a poetic example that ascribes ignorance to a donkey, because a donkey is not expected to have knowledge of such matters, so it cannot be described as ignorant.

Key Terms
الجهل البسيط
الجهل المركب
عدم العلم
تصور المعلوم على خلاف هيئته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes compound ignorance worse than simple ignorance?
A) It is more common.
B) It is existential (a positive misperception) rather than a mere absence.
C) It can be cured by learning.
D) It affects more people.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance is only applicable to beings capable of knowledge, and compound ignorance (false belief) is worse than simple ignorance (lack of knowledge).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Interpreting 'Hatta' and the Implicit Metaphor
Commentator discusses meanings of 'hatta' and metaphor types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two possible meanings of 'hatta' in the passage and explain how the metaphor combines implicit comparison (isti'ara bil-kinaya) with imaginative metaphor (isti'ara takhyiliyya).

Core Idea
The commentator shows that 'hatta' can mean 'then' or gradual removal, and the metaphor is an implicit simile plus an imaginative attribute.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks two subtle points in the poetic line: the particle 'hatta' (until) and the relationship between 'sky' and 'intellect'. He suggests that 'hatta' could indicate immediate sequence like 'fa', or it could retain its original sense of gradual progression. Then he explains that the intellect is implicitly compared to the sun, and the mention of 'sky' is an imaginative addition that belongs to the image of the sun. This lesson will guide you through this analysis.

Guided Path
1
Parse the first interpretation of 'hatta'
Note that the commentator says 'hatta' here can mean 'fa' (then) for immediate sequence. This interpretation treats the removal of veils as happening immediately upon seeing the suns of knowledge.
2
Parse the second interpretation of 'hatta'
Alternatively, 'hatta' can keep its gradual sense, implying a step-by-step removal of veils: first the beginner sciences, then intermediate, then perfection.
This gradual interpretation aligns with the idea of progressive learning.
3
Identify the implicit simile
The commentator says the poet might have made an implicit comparison (tashbih mudmar) between the intellect and the sun. This is isti'ara bil-kinaya, where the likened-to is not stated openly but implied.
The comparison is in the mind, not in the text.
4
Recognize the imaginative metaphor
The attribute 'sky' is added, which is a necessary concomitant of the sun (the sun appears in the sky). This addition is isti'ara takhyiliyya—it creates a vivid image by transferring a feature of the image to the thing compared.
The sky is not directly compared to anything; it serves the metaphor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (حَتَّى بَدَتْ) «حتّى» هنا بمعنى: «الفاء» للتعقيب، ويصح أن تكون على بابها ؛ بأن تقدر الإزالة تدريجيَّة بأن يزال حجاب أوائل العلوم، ثمَّ حجاب أوسطها، ثمَّ حجاب كمالها . قوله : (««سَمَاءِ» إِلَى «العَقْلِ») ما حمله عليه غير متعيّن، بل يحتمل أن يكون شبه العقل بالشَّمس تشبيهاً مضمراً في النَّفس استعارة بالكناية، وإثباتُ السَّماءِ الذي هو من لوازم المشبه به استعارة تخييلية.
His statement: 'until they appeared' — 'hatta' here is in the sense of 'fa' for sequence, and it is valid that it be used in its original sense, by assuming removal is gradual: first the veil of beginner sciences, then the veil of intermediate ones, then the veil of their perfection. His statement: 'of the sky to the intellect' — what he assigned is not determined; rather, it is possible that he likened the intellect to the sun, an implicit simile in the mind, a metaphorical allusion (isti'ara bil-kinaya), and the affirmation of 'sky' which is a necessary concomitant of the likened-to is an imaginative metaphor (isti'ara takhyiliyya).
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage analyzes the poetic line: 'Until the suns of knowledge appeared to them, they saw their unveiled virgins.' The commentator discusses the word 'hatta' (until), which can mean 'fa' (then) indicating immediate sequence, or retain its original sense implying gradual removal of veils. Then he examines the metaphor where 'sky' is attributed to 'intellect' and 'clouds' to 'ignorance' in the previous line. He suggests that the poet may have implicitly compared the intellect to the sun, and then added 'sky' as a necessary attribute of the sun, forming a metaphorical image (isti'ara takhyiliyya).

Key Terms
حتّى
استعارة بالكناية
استعارة تخييلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which of the following is true about the word 'hatta' in the line?
A) It only means 'until' and cannot be interpreted otherwise.
B) It can mean 'then' (fa) indicating immediate sequence, or it can retain its gradual sense.
C) It is a mistake in the manuscript.
D) It is used to indicate a cause.

Key Takeaway
The commentator reveals that the same expression can sustain multiple rhetorical layers: the particle 'hatta' has two possible meanings, and the metaphor combines implicit comparison with an imaginative attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the hadith: 'The believer is like a bee.' Identify whether this is a direct simile or an implicit metaphor, and what imaginative attributes might be added.
?

Metaphors in the Poem's Opening: Isti'ara Bil-Kinaya and Takhyiliyya
Examines metaphorical expressions in the poem's opening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of isti'ara (metaphor) used in a poetic line and explain the commentator's reasoning for multiple interpretations.

Core Idea
Metaphors in balagha use implication and imaginative extension to create layered meaning.

This passage from the commentary examines the poet's use of metaphors in describing the intellect and knowledge. The commentator discusses two key rhetorical devices – isti'ara bil-kinaya (implicit metaphor) and isti'ara takhyiliyya (imaginative metaphor) – and shows how both can be applied to the same text. Understanding these tools is essential for accurate interpretation of Arabic poetry and the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key phrases
Locate 'the sky of intellect' and 'suns to knowledge' in the Arabic. These are the metaphorical expressions under analysis.
The phrases are: 'إِضَافَةُ سَمَاءِ إِلَى العَقْلِ' and 'وَشُمُوسِ إِلَى المَعْرِفَةِ'.
2
Understand the two metaphor types
Isti'ara bil-kinaya is a metaphor where the simile is implicit in the mind; isti'ara takhyiliyya attributes a property of the likened-to to the likened.
The commentator explains that 'sky' is a necessity of the likened-to (the sun), so it functions as takhyiliyya.
3
Apply the commentator's analysis
Note that the commentator offers alternative interpretations: either the intellect is likened to the sun (with sky as takhyiliyya) or knowledge is likened to a sky with suns (with 'suns' as takhyiliyya).
He emphasizes that both readings are possible and that the intellect does not prevent multiplicity.
4
Recognize the role of imagination
The mention of 'appeared' is explained as an illusion, similar to al-Hudhali's line about death sinking claws. This shows how the poet creates vivid imagery.
This illustrates the flexibility of metaphorical expression in Arabic rhetoric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (حَتَّى بَدَتْ) «حتّى» هنا بمعنى: «الفاء» للتعقيب، ويصح أن تكون على بابها ؛ بأن تقدر الإزالة تدريجيَّة بأن يزال حجاب أوائل العلوم، ثمَّ حجاب أوسطها، ثمَّ حجاب كمالها . قوله : (إِضَافَةُ سَمَاءِ» إِلَى العَقْلِ) ما حمله عليه غير متعيّن، بل يحتمل أن يكون شبه العقل بالشَّمس تشبيهاً مضمراً في النَّفس استعارة بالكناية، وإثباتُ السَّماء» الذي هو من لوازم المشبه به استعارة تخييلية . قوله : (وَشُمُوسِ إِلَى المَعْرِفَةِ) لا يتعيَّن هذا الذي ذكره، بل يحتمل أن يكون شبه المعرفة بسماء لها شموس، والعقل لا يمنع تعدُّدها، واقتصر على المشبه استعارة بالكناية، وتخييلية؛ لإثباته الشَّمسَ الَّتي هي من لوازم المشبه به، وذكر بَدَتْ» إيهام على حد قول الهذلي: وَإذَا المَنِيَةُ أَنْشَبَتْ أَظْفَارَهَا . قوله : (بِضَمُ اللَّامِ وَفَتْحِ الجِيمِ) تعرّض لضبطه؛ تعريضاً بالخلخالي حيث ضبطه بفتح اللام وكسر الجيم ، وقال : إنَّه الورق الذي يسقط شبه به الماء»، فإنَّه لم يفرّق بين لجين الكلام ؛ أي شريفه ولجينه؛ أي: رديئه، ولم يعرف هجانه ـ أي : خياره ـ من هجينه؛ أي: خسيسه؛ أي يقال له : «أين وجه الشَّبه؟» فربَّما يقال: «لعله محاه الماء» . قوله : (كَمَا قِيْلَ) قائل ذلك قدّورة.
His saying: (until it appeared) 'حَتَّى' here is in the sense of 'فَاءِ' for immediate succession, and it is correct to be on its original meaning by assuming gradual removal: the veil of the beginnings of sciences is removed, then the veil of their middles, then the veil of their perfection. His saying: (the addition of 'sky' to 'intellect') what he based it on is not certain; rather it is probable that he likened the intellect to the sun, a simile implicit in the mind as a metaphor by implication, and the affirmation of 'sky' which is one of the necessities of the likened-to is an imaginative metaphor. His saying: (and 'suns' to 'knowledge') is not certain as he mentioned; rather it is probable that he likened knowledge to a sky having suns, and the intellect does not prevent multiplicity, and he restricted to the likened by metaphor with implication, and imaginative; by affirming the sun which is a necessity of the likened-to, and he mentioned 'appeared' as an illusion like the saying of al-Hudhali: 'When death sank its claws...' His saying: (with damm of lām and fatḥ of jīm) he attended to its pronunciation, alluding to al-Khalkhāli who pronounced it with fatḥ of lām and kasr of jīm, and said: it is the paper that falls, like water—but he did not distinguish between the 'lujayn' of speech (i.e., its noble) and its 'lujayn' (i.e., its base), and did not know its pure from its hybrid; so one says to him: 'Where is the point of similarity?' Perhaps one says: 'Maybe the water erased it.' His saying: (as it was said) the one who said that is Qaddūra.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed analysis of the metaphors in the poem's opening. The commentator first discusses the word 'حَتَّى', noting it can indicate immediate succession or gradual removal. Then he examines the phrase 'sky of intellect', suggesting multiple possible metaphorical interpretations: either the intellect is likened to the sun (with sky being an implied metaphor) or knowledge is likened to a sky with suns. He emphasizes that the poet's wording allows for multiplicity. Next, he criticizes al-Khalkhāli's misreading of the word 'لجين' (silver), pointing out that al-Khalkhāli confused it with 'لُجَيْن' meaning paper, failing to distinguish between noble and base. The commentator closes by attributing the mistaken view to Qaddūra.

استعارة تخييلية

Correcting a Linguistic Error: Vocalization of 'Lujayn'
Corrects a linguistic error in the vocalization of 'lujayn'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct vocalization of 'lujayn' and explain how misreading it changes the meaning of the line.

Core Idea
A single diacritic can turn silver into paper; accurate vocalization preserves the poetic intent.

The commentator interrupts the rhetorical analysis to correct a mistake by al-Khalkhali, who misread the word 'lujayn' as paper instead of silver. This error altered the metaphor and confused the meaning. This lesson highlights the importance of precise pronunciation and understanding homonyms in classical Arabic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its two possible vocalizations
The word is 'لجين'. With damm of lām and fatḥ of jīm (لُجَيْن) it means silver; with fatḥ of lām and kasr of jīm (لَجِين) it means paper or parchment.
The commentator specifies the correct reading as the first (silver).
2
Understand the critic's error
Al-Khalkhali used the vocalization for paper, leading to a faulty interpretation where the poet compared water to paper falling. The commentator says he failed to distinguish between noble and base speech.
The line is about the metaphorical 'silver of speech' (noble words), not paper.
3
Recognize the consequence
The error made the metaphor meaningless or inappropriate. The commentator sarcastically says one might ask al-Khalkhali where the similarity is, and perhaps he would say 'water erased it', showing the absurdity.
This illustrates how a simple misreading can break the poetic image.
4
Apply the lesson to other texts
When reading Arabic, always check the diacritics, especially for homonyms. The context often clarifies the intended meaning.
This principle applies to Quranic recitation, hadith, and all classical texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting an Invalid Part-for-Whole Substitution Claim
Author refutes the claim that the substitution is badal ba'd min kull, citing logical impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a substitution claim in Arabic grammar is invalid due to a non-partitive relationship and explain the correct analysis using implicit links.

Core Idea
A part-for-whole substitution requires a genuine part-whole relationship; otherwise, the analysis fails.

In this lesson, we examine a critical refinement in Arabic grammar concerning the classification of a substitution (badal). The commentator refutes a claim that a certain substitution is a part-for-whole substitution (badal ba'd min kull). By understanding his logical objection, you will gain precision in analyzing grammatical constructions, especially in Quranic exegesis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the statement that the substitution is badal ba'd min kull. This claim was made by someone named Abd al-Latif.
The claim is explicit in the text: 'لا بدل بعض من كل كما قيل'.
2
Locate the author's objection
The author states that the intellect ('aql) is not a part of the people (the referent of the pronoun 'anhum). Therefore, it cannot be a part-for-whole substitution.
The key phrase: 'أنَّ العقل ليس بعضاً من مصدوق الضَّمير في عَنْهُمْ'.
3
Understand the basis of the objection
For a badal ba'd min kull, the substitute must be a part of the substituted entity. Here, the relationship is not partitive—the intellect is not a piece of the people.
This is a logical condition inherent in the definition of this substitution type.
4
Recognize the correct analysis
The author explains that the link (al-rabiṭ) between the substitute and the substituted is an implicit pronoun, or the definite article 'al' acts as a deputy for that pronoun, similar to the analysis of the Quranic verse 'Qutila ashab al-ukhdud'.
The alternative analysis avoids the part-whole issue and is consistent with standard grammatical practice.
5
Apply to similar constructions
Consider other examples where a part-whole relationship is assumed but may not hold. Practice identifying whether the substitute is truly a part of the substituted.
This skill prevents misclassification in Quranic and classical Arabic analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لَا بَدَلَ بَعْضٍ مِنْ كُلِّ كَمَا قِيلَ) القائلُ لذلك عبد اللطيف . ووجه المنع : أنَّ العقل ليس بعضاً من مصدوق الضَّمير في عَنْهُمْ، والضمير مقدَّر في البدل؛ أعني : الرَّابط بين البدل والمبدل منه، أو «أل» نائبة عنه كما قيل بهما في قوله تعالى: ﴿قُتِلَ أَصْحَابُ الْأُخْدُودِ﴾ [البروج : ٤] .
His statement: 'Not a badal ba'd min kull as has been said' - the one who said that is Abd al-Latif. And the reason for the prohibition is that the intellect is not a part of the referent of the pronoun in 'anhum', and the pronoun is estimated in the substitute; I mean the link between the substitute and the substituted, or 'al' is a deputy for it, as has been said regarding both in His statement: 'Cursed be the companions of the ditch' [al-Buruj: 4].
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) addressing a claim made by someone named Abd al-Latif. The claim was that the grammatical substitution (badal) in the preceding lines is a part-for-whole substitution (badal ba'd min kull). The author refutes this by pointing out a logical flaw: the intellect ('aql) is not a part (ba'd) of the people (the referent of the pronoun 'anhum). Therefore, it cannot be a part-for-whole substitution. The author then explains that instead, the pronoun (the link) is implicit in the substitute, or the definite article 'al' acts as a substitute, similar to the analysis of the Quranic verse 'Qutila ashab al-ukhdud' (the companions of the ditch). This analysis follows the standard grammatical methodology, showing that the substitution is actually inclusive (ishtimal), not partial.

Key Terms
بدل بعض من كل
الرابط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the verse is different
D) Because Abd al-Latif was mistaken about grammar

Key Takeaway
A part-for-whole substitution requires a genuine part-whole relationship; the author's critique demonstrates that without that, the substitution must be analyzed differently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Causal 'ala and the Specific Mention of Faith
Author explains 'ala as causal and mentions faith and Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the causative usage of 'ala in Arabic and explain why the commentator highlights faith and Islam in the context of blessings.

Core Idea
The preposition 'ala can denote cause, and specifying faith and Islam reveals their foundational role.

The commentator unpacks the poem's line 'upon the blessings' by noting that 'ala here is causative, as in the Quranic verse about magnifying Allah for guidance. He then defines in'am and explains why faith and Islam are singled out. This grammatical analysis reveals a theological priority: faith is the root of all blessings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical claim
Find where the commentator says 'ala is ta'liliyya (causative). Recognize that this is a specific usage indicating cause or reason.
The example from Quran 2:185 is given.
2
Define in'am
Extract the definition: 'an'ama bi-n-ni'mah' means bestowing and conveying a blessing. Note that the definite article 'al' indicates generality.
This is a lexical definition.
3
Understand the mention of faith and Islam
The commentator says they are specified for their honor and because they are the cause of every blessing. This prioritizes spiritual blessings over material ones.
This connects grammar to theology.
4
Connect to the poem's context
The author is praising Allah for all blessings, but specially highlighting faith and Islam as the greatest.
This sets the tone for the entire poem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول النَّاظم : (عَلَى الإِنْعَامِ) «على» تعليليَّة؛ كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلِتُكَبِرُوا اللَّهَ عَلَى مَا هَدَاكُمْ﴾ [البقرة: 185]، والإنعامُ مصدر: «أَنْعَمَ بِالنِّعْمَةِ» : إذا أسداها وأوصلها، و«أل» فيه للعموم. وخص نعمة الإيمان والإسلام بالذكر ؛ لشرفهما، ولأنَّهما السبب في كل نعمة. قوله : (لِيُفِيدَ تَوْلِيَةً ذَلِكَ بِنَفْسِهِ) فإن قلت: لم أتى بالنون، ولم يأت بالهمزة، مع أنَّ كلا منهما مفيد هذا المعنى؟ قلت : لأحد أمرين : ۱ - إما أن يكون قصد التشريك ؛ إيماء إلى العجز عن القيام بحمد الله تعالى استقلالاً، فاحتاج في ذلك إلى المعين ؛ تواضعاً واستحقاراً لنفسه عن الاستقلال بالقيام بحمد ربه . ٢ - ويحتمل أن يكون قصداً لإظهار نعمة الله تعالى عليه وهي تعظيمه بجعله أهلاً للتأليف؛ امتثالاً لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَمَّا بِنِعْمَةِ رَبِّكَ فَحَدِّثْ﴾ [الضحى: 11] .
And the author's statement: 'upon the blessings' - 'ala is causative, as in His statement: 'and that you magnify Allah for having guided you' [al-Baqarah: 185]. Al-in'am is the verbal noun of 'an'ama bin-ni'mah': when he bestows and conveys it. And the definite article 'al' in it is for generality. He specifically mentioned the blessing of faith and Islam due to their honor and because they are the cause of every blessing. His statement: 'to indicate that he personally undertakes that' - If you say: Why did he use the nun, and not use the hamza, given that both convey this meaning? I say: For one of two reasons: 1 - Either he intended partnership (tashrik), hinting at inability to praise Allah independently, so he needed help in that, out of humility and belittling himself from being independent in praising his Lord. 2 - Or it is possible that he intended to show the blessing of Allah upon him, which is His exalting him by making him worthy of authorship, in compliance with His statement: 'And as for the blessing of your Lord, proclaim!' [al-Duha: 11].
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the poem's line about praising Allah for blessings. The commentator explains that the preposition 'ala is causative, as in the Quranic verse about magnifying Allah for guidance. The word 'in'am' is defined as bestowing a blessing, and the definite article indicates generality. He then justifies why the author specifically mentioned faith and Islam: they are the noblest blessings and the cause of all others. The main discussion revolves around why the author used the plural form 'nahmaduhu' (we praise Him) with the nun, rather than the singular 'ahmaduhu' (I praise Him). The commentator presents two possibilities: first, to indicate shared praise, showing humility and inability to praise alone; second, to show gratitude for the blessing of being enabled to author this work, as commanded in the Quran. This follows a dialectical format: a hypothetical question ('fa-in qulta') followed by an answer. The commentary thus enriches the understanding of the author's linguistic choices.

Key Terms
على تعليلية
الإنعام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the definite article 'al' in 'al-in'am' indicates generality?
A) Because it refers to all blessings
B) Because it refers to the blessing of faith only
C) Because it emphasizes the singularity of Allah
D) Because it is a grammatical requirement

Key Takeaway
The commentator uses grammatical analysis to highlight that Allah is praised for all blessings, especially faith and Islam.

The Nun in 'Nahmaduhu': Humility or Gratitude?
Author discusses the two reasons for using the nun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author uses the plural nun in 'nahmaduhu' and evaluate the two reasons given by the commentator.

Core Idea
The nun in 'nahmaduhu' is a deliberate choice that conveys either humility in partnership or gratitude for authorship.

The commentator raises a hypothetical question: why use the plural nun instead of the singular hamza? He gives two possible answers, both rooted in theological and moral considerations. This shows how grammatical choices can carry deeper meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
Find the hypothetical question: 'Why did he use the nun and not the hamza?'
The question is introduced with 'fa-in qulta'.
2
Understand the first possibility
The commentator says the author may have intended partnership (tashrik) to indicate inability to praise Allah alone, out of humility.
This shows the author's modesty.
3
Understand the second possibility
Alternatively, the author may have intended to show gratitude for the blessing of being enabled to author this work, as per Quranic command.
This emphasizes thankfulness.
4
Compare the two
Both are valid interpretations. The first stresses humility, the second gratitude. The commentator presents them as alternatives.
The author may have intended both.
5
Apply to the context
Recognize that grammatical choices in Islamic texts often carry theological and moral weight.
This is common in Arabic discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول النَّاظم : (عَلَى الإِنْعَامِ) «على» تعليليَّة؛ كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلِتُكَبِرُوا اللَّهَ عَلَى مَا هَدَاكُمْ﴾ [البقرة: 185]، والإنعامُ مصدر: «أَنْعَمَ بِالنِّعْمَةِ» : إذا أسداها وأوصلها، و«أل» فيه للعموم. وخص نعمة الإيمان والإسلام بالذكر ؛ لشرفهما، ولأنَّهما السبب في كل نعمة. قوله : (لِيُفِيدَ تَوْلِيَةً ذَلِكَ بِنَفْسِهِ) فإن قلت: لم أتى بالنون، ولم يأت بالهمزة، مع أنَّ كلا منهما مفيد هذا المعنى؟ قلت : لأحد أمرين : ۱ - إما أن يكون قصد التشريك ؛ إيماء إلى العجز عن القيام بحمد الله تعالى استقلالاً، فاحتاج في ذلك إلى المعين ؛ تواضعاً واستحقاراً لنفسه عن الاستقلال بالقيام بحمد ربه . ٢ - ويحتمل أن يكون قصداً لإظهار نعمة الله تعالى عليه وهي تعظيمه بجعله أهلاً للتأليف؛ امتثالاً لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَمَّا بِنِعْمَةِ رَبِّكَ فَحَدِّثْ﴾ [الضحى: 11] .
And the author's statement: 'upon the blessings' - 'ala is causative, as in His statement: 'and that you magnify Allah for having guided you' [al-Baqarah: 185]. Al-in'am is the verbal noun of 'an'ama bin-ni'mah': when he bestows and conveys it. And the definite article 'al' in it is for generality. He specifically mentioned the blessing of faith and Islam due to their honor and because they are the cause of every blessing. His statement: 'to indicate that he personally undertakes that' - If you say: Why did he use the nun, and not use the hamza, given that both convey this meaning? I say: For one of two reasons: 1 - Either he intended partnership (tashrik), hinting at inability to praise Allah independently, so he needed help in that, out of humility and belittling himself from being independent in praising his Lord. 2 - Or it is possible that he intended to show the blessing of Allah upon him, which is His exalting him by making him worthy of authorship, in compliance with His statement: 'And as for the blessing of your Lord, proclaim!' [al-Duha: 11].
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the poem's line about praising Allah for blessings. The commentator explains that the preposition 'ala is causative, as in the Quranic verse about magnifying Allah for guidance. The word 'in'am' is defined as bestowing a blessing, and the definite article indicates generality. He then justifies why the author specifically mentioned faith and Islam: they are the noblest blessings and the cause of all others. The main discussion revolves around why the author used the plural form 'nahmaduhu' (we praise Him) with the nun, rather than the singular 'ahmaduhu' (I praise Him). The commentator presents two possibilities: first, to indicate shared praise, showing humility and inability to praise alone; second, to show gratitude for the blessing of being enabled to author this work, as commanded in the Quran. This follows a dialectical format: a hypothetical question ('fa-in qulta') followed by an answer. The commentary thus enriches the understanding of the author's linguistic choices.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is one likely reason the author used the nun?
A) To indicate that Allah is the only one worthy of praise
B) To express humility by sharing praise with others
C) To emphasize that the author is alone in praising Allah
D) To match the meter of the poem

Key Takeaway
The choice between singular and plural pronouns in theological texts can reflect humility or gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider other Islamic texts that use 'nahmad' vs 'ahmad'. What might the nun indicate in those contexts?
?
If the author had used 'ahmaduhu' (singular), what different message would that send? Compare with the two reasons given.
